O, son, since I have completed for you this book, which is the summit of the science of medicine, for its completion, its consequences, its exposition, and its clarifications, it seems to me that I should fulfill my obligation to you with this treatise, which is a part of the practice with the hand, that is, surgery.
Because the practice with the hand is almost entirely lost in our region and our time, to the point that its science is greatly diminished, and its vestiges are obscured; and nothing remains of it but some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which are mostly altered, leading to errors and hesitation, so that their intentions are unclear and their strength and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I should revive it by ordering this treatise according to a method of exposition, clarification, and abbreviation; and so that it may include the forms of cautery instruments and other surgical instruments, both by adding descriptions and by preparing them, which is necessary.
But the reason why a skillful artisan with his hands is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner must first be trained in the science of anatomy, which is lacking in Gaul, so that he knows the functions of the members and their forms, and their connections and separations; and has knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, and their number and arrangement; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their origin.
And for this reason Hippocrates says that in name it is much, but in practice little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable in what we have said about anatomy is surely liable to fall into error, by which men will be killed, as I have seen many examples, for some are trained in this science and then practice it without knowledge and experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he cut some arteries of the neck, so that a great deal of blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who had grown old, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone along with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and a black eunuch came to him who had a fracture in his thigh; and the physician, through his ignorance, pulled the fractured bones apart, over the wound, with cushions and splints with tight binding, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he loosened it according to his wishes, and then he bound it again, and then he left it for some days and commanded that we not loosen the binding; and it remained so until the thigh and foot became abscessed, and it came to ruin.
And I was called to him, and I prepared to unbind the ligature, and tranquility was achieved and thus a lessening of his pain.
But however, corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who punctured a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated for some days, until its malignancy was magnified with its lord (or master/owner).
The reason is that a cancerous sore is full of melancholic humor; therefore, it should not be approached with iron at all, but it should be in a limb which suffers so that it is completely contained.
And therefore, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be divided into two divisions: that is, in an operation which is associated with health, and in a work with which there is peril in the greatest dispositions (or conditions).
And I have already explained this throughout this book, in which operations come which contain error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you that you guard against this and avoid that, so that fools do not find a way to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with God's protection, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method, by the grace of God, leading to health and laudable success, and leave the terrible illnesses of difficult healing, and keep your instruments away from that which makes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not escape you: for it remains more to your glory, and higher in the world and behind your holiness.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad illness, lest you be called bad physicians".
And I have divided this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered from head to foot, on the forms of the instruments and the tools of cauteries, and on what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of suction and perforation, and of cupping glasses, and of wounds, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I should say the quality of the benefits of that cautery and its harms, and in what complexion cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and its harm is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and a little of their discourses suffices me, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, which are with matter or without matter, except two complexions, which are hot complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not suitable in a disease which is caused by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is unsuitable that a hot and dry disease should be healed by that which is hot and dry.
And I answer him who says the contrary, that benefit is done by cautery in every hot and dry disease that comes in the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of man and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say, concerning his discourse, that experience has revealed this to me several times.
However, it is not fitting that this should come upon one who is already experienced and exercised in the chapter on cautery by previous practice, and who has known the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of the diseases in themselves, and the causes of them, and their accidents, and the duration of those diseases.
But in the other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in the cold and moist diseases, for all physicians are agreed upon those, and they are not in disagreement about the benefit with cautery in those.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and its goodness, is superior to cautery with a burning medicine, because fire is simple, whose action does not reach except the member which is cauterized, and does not harm another member adjoining it, except with little harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches to those members which are distant from it, and quickly brings to the member a disease of difficult healing and quickly kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this, except that it surpasses. And it is already known to us by experiment and by the length of service of study with the art concerning the truth of the causes; and for that reason I am excused from the length of speech. And if it were not that it is not written in my book at this point a secret hidden with fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies and its expulsion by which it expels diseases, with philosophical demonstrative speech, which would be acute in your thoughts.
And know, sons, that they disagree on the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, and they place the best time in springtime.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, so that the harm due to accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is achieved with cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or necessary pains, strong and urgent, which do not suffer delay because it is feared that those evils may come, which are greater than the small harm caused by time.
And this does not enter your minds, oh sons, this is not esteemed except by the vulgar people and foolish physicians, that is, that for that illness which is healed by cautery there is no remission at any time, and they place that as a consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they believe, for cautery is only in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
However, cautery is superfluous over medicine because of the quickness of its efficacy and the power of its operation and the greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that his illness may return at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his illness, and his strength, and that which is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him, and that which has reached him in its acquisition in food and similar causes; except that the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a member with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar to that, it is possible that that pain may not return to him, but this happens in small things.
And a more common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and it is a true saying, not to that which they understand by it, for they believe that there is no cure that profits with medicine or with any other thing besides cautery.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer the methods of cure in some illnesses, and those medicines do not help, and then at the end of the cause one administers cautery, and it profits for that. And therefore from here it is seen that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the intention which the common people and too many foolish doctors understand.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the evenness of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not burn, as if that were not absolutely true, because I have already proven this and found that it does not do so except in some bodies without others, and cautery with this is good and better than with iron as they said; however, when you do cautery with fire with gold, it is not apparent to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity you desire by the roughness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you apply fire to it, the gold melts from the fire, and sometimes some sheets in the fire so that the artisan is busy with that.
And therefore cautery with iron is done more easily and more appropriately for the correctness of the operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those from the head to the feet, so that I might offer to the enquirer what he wants from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization counteracts the humidity and coldness affecting the brain; the accident of which has two causes: that is, the cause of excessive and abundant rheumy flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and excessive sleepiness, and toothache and sore throat, and universally of every illness that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, sopor, and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The form of this cauterization is this: first, order the patient to be evacuated with a purgative medicine, cleansing the head, for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, his age, and his constitution.
And then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then place him sitting between your hands on a low bench and have him place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm near the bridge of his nose, between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that spot, mark that spot with a probe, and then ignite the olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place that [cautery] on the spot marked with the probe, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand should be constrained little by little, and you rotate it.
And then lift your hand slightly and observe the area, and if you see some quantity of the head of the bone uncovered, like the quantity of a grain of barley, then lift your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron, with another of that kind if it is cooled, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the area and leave it for three days.
And then place on it cotton soaked in wine and leave it there until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the area with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And it has been said that when the wound remains open, it is better to leave the arm [unbound], because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the bone marrow should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's substance is hardened.
And then scrape away what is burned from the bone, and then it should be treated.
And others say that with the cautery, penetrate to the back, until it firmly enters the bone, until the bone is broken, and this is the form of a small alphecat [presumably a type of ulcer or lesion]: And they believe that vapors from the head exhale from that place.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then it should be healed until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men, according to the way of fear; and leave that, my friend, it is better with health, when it is; for the head is weakened by the solution of its continuity, just as we have testified in the other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a cold nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and I use and work with that one for health.
Chapter Two.
Concerning the cautery of the head.
When a chronic and prolonged pain occurs in the whole head, and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches, cupping, purgatives, plasters, ointments, poultices, and similar remedies, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we mentioned and none of these things benefit him, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution by nature; and if it is not cold and finds great coldness, then cauterize that with another cautery below the previous one a little. And then cauterize that on the temple of his head with a cautery until the skin is parted. Incise the skin and let the bone be uncovered to the extent we have recounted, and cauterize that with a cautery in the back part of the head in the place which is called the vas – and that is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And lighten your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient through this finds much greater pain, different from the pain of all the other cauteries of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place, for it is necessary that the cautery with which the temples of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter three.
Concerning the cauterization of recent migraine.
When a sharp pain arises in the head, or a spreading pain within the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the sections on diseases; and if nothing helps, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and peel it and cut off its extremities from both ends. Then incise the location of the pain in the temple with a lancet until a place is made there beneath the skin, until it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a compress for continuous constriction, and leave it for the space of three hours; then untie it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then place cotton soaked in butter on it until the area becomes soft.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner:  heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail or small nail, because its head is like the shape of a clove, in which there is some curvature, and in the middle of it is a small eminence.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the burning which is below is not hurt — and move the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount that is burned by the sharpness of the cautery is thus half of it, and lift your hand so that the artery below is not burned, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the location, and leave it for three days; and then place cotton soaked with  ointment, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of the knife as with a cautery, and beware of cutting the artery in this, specifically a not-ancient migraine.
Chapter four.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic migraine.
When you treat migraine with what we have said in the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment does not help, and sometimes because of the illness what we have said about the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is not enough, then it is necessary that you heat the knife cautery until it is white hot, so that you mark the location of the pain with a line whose length is half a finger, or something like that; and pause your hand for a moment and press it until you incise the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful of the continuation of the jaw, which is weakened by chewing, and that the muscle not be hardened and the nerve that serves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the guarding and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, because in its incision there is danger, and especially to one who is ignorant of what he is doing and is not studious and experienced; and leaves the operation to him at the beginning.
And I will come with a recollection of the treatment of accidental blood flow from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this illness is not alleviated by this cauterization and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize it in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of an artery with a cautery having two blades; this cauterization is better and more suitable.
Chapter 5.
On the cauterization of earaches.
When pain in the ear comes from cold and is treated with laxative remedies and with the other treatments that I have described in the section, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery; this is the one called "puncta," of which this is the form:
Then apply the point, then the ignition, and let it be moved around the entire ear as a wheel is turned, or in the circumference of each part if the pain is in one part. Extend the cautery from the root of the ear little by little, after you have marked the location with a probe.
And if the cautery requires ten points in the whole ear, or in a part of it, then treat the areas until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the cauterization of a cough.
A cough which is cured with cauterization is only one of those types which come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type of cough that arises from dryness or from a spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of cough with leeches, purgatives, and gargles, and your treatment is ineffective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries:  the first at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side from the affected part, because softening [or relief] only comes to the part that appears healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the ear, a little above the temple, and another at the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the thumb, and hold your cautery there until half the thickness of a finger's breadth is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of knife, the form of which is shown before [in a diagram, presumably]. However, it is more subtle if it is a little like this; and it is necessary to cut away a little of the superfluous thickness.
And then care for the place with what has been previously prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have said, and your medication is not effective, and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries: on all the horns of his head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And it is already cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; and it is further back; and then let it be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the cauterization of forgetfulness done by flame.
So that large enemas and purging pills are given to the patient at the beginning.
And then shave the patient's entire head and apply to their neck a plaster of the kind described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cautery. And do this according to the same order which I have already said.
And if he is healed by those means, it is good; but if not, then make for him three cauteries in the back part of his head, which descend from the uppermost part of the head to the lowermost part of the neck. And place between each cautery the width of a finger, and then treat the cautery with what has been prescribed.
But if you add to it and the patient tolerates it, then make a cautery in the middle place; and if you wish a greater addition, then cauterize that on both sides. (or ...then cauterize on both sides of it.)
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form previously given.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
That you proceed in the purification of the head with leeches and with what we have said; and then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery on all the sides of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to find it and the patient is tolerant and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery until it is burnt from the tailbone of him, and raise your hand. And then cure it until it is healed, and this should be an olive-oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
And therefore it is necessary that you purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatments which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and can tolerate the administration of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, before that for many days, with improvement of the diet.
Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the preceding method, and with another cautery in his occiput, and cauterize above each temple. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize him with the cauteries that I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
On the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and a thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cauteries which we have described in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is moist, then give him to drink that which cleanses his brain, according to what has preceded in the division (of treatments).
And then shave the head of the patient, and then make a decent bandage for him/her in a circle, and then place that in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright and held securely on all sides.
And then apply a liniment; and it is more worthy to use old sheep's grease. And heat that over the fire with moderate warmth, which the said sufferer will feel when it is applied to him/her. And then pour that into the middle of his/her head within the circle, and leave it there until it is cooled.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with his/her other good treatment until he/she is healed; or if you wish, cauterize it by making small punctures according to the patient's need, but hold your hand with a cautery thus prepared: this is a type of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
And then apply that cotton soaked in butter or goose grease.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the cauterization of water descending into the eye.
When the beginning of water in the eye is revealed to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something to drink that will purify his head, and protect it from all humidity.
And make him sweat in a bath for several days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the beginning of the descent of water is in both eyes, or on one side if the beginning of the water is in one eye.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the temple; and let the cauterizations be far apart in the width of the temples.
And beware of a flow of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see a flow of blood from it, stop it with any medicine which is possible for you.
And I come with wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries placed far apart.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears (likely referring to old wounds or sores, possibly fistulas).
When the tears (wounds, sores) of the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest location of the head, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we recounted in the first chapter concerning water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it requires additional treatment, then cauterize that in all parts of the corner of the eye above the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (foul smell from the nose).
When you treat this with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not help, then you prepare and give the patient cochlearia (a spoonfull, or possibly a specific herbal remedy) to drink for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a moderate-sized cautery, with an olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize that with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauteries above the two eyebrows, just below the hair a little.
And be careful with the burning, that you do not burn too deeply.
And of the clove-shaped cautery, this is the form (shape/method).
Chapter 15.
Of the cauterization for softening the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize that (area) above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries in each part, and they should be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too much with the cautery, but only enough so that a third part of the eyelid is hardened/dried.
Chapter XVI.
Concerning the cauterization of the eyelid of the eye when the skin is turned inward towards the parts within and punctures the eye.
When the skin of the eye is turned inward so that it punctures the eye, cauterization in such cases is done in two ways, for cauterization is either with fire or with a burning medicine.
If cauterizing it with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave the hairs as those who cleanse them do, until they are elongated and softened; and if it punctures beneath their roots, then you should bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so that they are not harmed until the hairs have grown back.
Then, when they are grown and softened, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid of their eye with a probe, with a mark which is according to the figure of a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then apply a cotton swab soaked in egg white or in mallow mucilage to the eye.
And then ignite the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the figure which you have marked, little by little, in many times, until the surface of the cornea, which is like a myrtle leaf, is entirely burned.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hairs around it raised from the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and then apply cotton with a bandage until the scar is healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if some matter returns through the hairs near their origin and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again in the same way as you did at first.
But if the hairs are under the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is even, and the hairs do not prick the eye.
But cauterize with a burning medicine; that is, order the patient to pluck the hairs until they are elongated and even, and then make a paper or cloth shape like a myrtle leaf.
Then take known soap and quicklime, one ounce, or whatever, and mix these ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not cool; then spread this on the paper which is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place this on one or both eyes; and place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it lightly, and do not move it until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like fire.
And then, when you perceive that he feels the bite, then leave the medicine and move it with your finger; then, when the biting sensation ceases, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then observe if you see that the eyelid is already raised; if it is not raised you should constrain it with fire or incision, for then it is well; and if not, return some of the medicine to the place where the medicine has not been applied and it has not blackened until your operation is even and the eyelid is contracted.
Then place cotton with a bandage on it until the scab from the cauterization is removed, and then treat it with healing ointment or something else until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to watch with diligent vigilance that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after a few days is softened and some hair roots push into the eye, then repeat your medicine on that place of the eyelid which is softened, and properly as you did in the beginning, and then heal it until it is healed.
And this is the method for myrtle leaves.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size and smallness, and therefore your constriction is made accordingly.
And the way of rectitude will not be hidden from him who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment does not succeed, then you must cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place their head in your lap, and have a minister hold their head between your hands in such a way that their head is not moved or agitated.
And then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or gum arabic on their eye.
And then heat the cautery, whose form is this:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cauterization is done.
And if you wish, let it be penetrating to the other end. And if you wish, let it be hollow like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the rottenness from it.
Then place the cautery, which should be well ignited, over it, and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not waver or the patient is disturbed, and then place the cautery on the granulated tissue of the eye and burn it away.
And so if at the beginning your cautery reaches the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then apply a dressing with lint and around it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply a sharp corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and is scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which some ancients described:  Locate the fistula and open it, and then place in that fissure a thin tube, the shape of which is like this:  And pour into it the quantity of one ounce of melted lead; and hold your hand with the tube with good retention, and do not disturb the patient so that the melted lead does not reach the eye; and it is necessary that you place over the patient's eye lint soaked in egg white or water, because the lead will adhere to the site of the fistula and heal it by marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to make an incision in the nose and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it will come to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
On the cauterization of the fissure of the well (likely referring to a wound or ulcer).
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and mostly on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat those fissures with what we have said in the divisions and your treatment does not help, then apply a small, knife-like cautery according to this form that is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then carefully place that instrument in those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
Then treat that with cerates until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops, then breaks, becomes chronic, and pus runs and a fistula is formed, and you treat it but the treatment is ineffective, then you should apply a cautery according to the size of the fistula. Then insert the heated cautery into the opening of the fistula, holding it with your hand until the heated iron reaches the bottom, and do this once or twice. Then treat it with what we have described for treatment until it is healed.
And so, if the wound is closed and heals, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to expose the area and remove the corrupted bone as will be described in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the cauterization of teeth and the softening of gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines without success, then place the patient's head on your lap, and then heat the cautery, the shape of which will be described later, in such a way that you place the bowl over the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, and hold it briefly until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization several times as needed.
And then let the patient fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth will be fixed, the softened gum will be tightened, and the corrupt moisture dried up.
Chapter 21.
On the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or worms in it, and you don't achieve a cure with medicine, then cauterize it in one of two ways: either cauterize with a cow's horn, or cauterize with fire.
Cauterization with a horn is done by taking a cow's horn and boiling it in a pot of iron. Then take cotton and wrap it around the end of a stick, and then dip it in the boiled horn, and quickly place it on the aching tooth until it cools.
And then repeat this several times until the virtue of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or cotton in the aforementioned boiled horn, and place that on the aching tooth, and place ignited iron on top of that until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is that you should use a small tube of bronze or iron, and there should be some thickness in its core, and the sharpness of the fire should not reach the patient's mouth.
Then ignite the cautery, the shape of which is appropriate, and place it on the tooth and hold your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times because the pain will be relieved either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, after the application of that cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good boiled water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize with whatever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the shape of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
On the cauterization of extrophulos (or possibly,  exanthematous eruptions/skin eruptions).
When extrophulos are from phlegm and cold humors and are not yielding to digestion by medicines, and you wish to digest them lightly, then ignite the cautery whose shape is as follows, having two open ends so that smoke escapes from one end of the cautery while the other is applied, and place this ignited cautery on the abscess once, and again if it is needed, until it reaches the bottom of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and apply a cloth soaked in butter over it until what the fire has burned is softened.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages and cloths until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the cauterization of hoarseness and the constriction of the throat.
When humors gather in the throat and lung, especially if this is accompanied by coldness of constitution, then it is necessary that the patient be evacuated initially with laxative medicines, and afterwards cauterize below the root of the throat in the constricted place.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn the windpipe, but only a little.
And cauterize that with another cautery below the junction of the neck at the last vertebra. And his cautery should be a nail, following the previous method.
And afterwards, treat it with what has been prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are caused by cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption and the illness is chronic, then cauterize with two cauteries on the clavicle in the depressed locations, left and right; and with another cauterization in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a nail according to the previous method.
And if you wish, your cauterization may be punctate with the cautery that is called a punctate cautery, the form of which precedes in the punctation of earache.
And let there be points within those [cauteries]; and then cure that with the same [methods] that are described before until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs, for cauterization is done with it: with one application you can make three cauteries according to this method:
Chapter 25.
Of the cauterization of the titillic, which is the head of the penis.
When the head of the penis is dislocated because of lubricous humors and is not fixed in the hour of its return from its dislocation until that which is accustomed to return to it is there, and then by a small movement it is dislocated again so that it returns, as we have testified, then it is necessary that you first reduce the dislocation; and then the patient should be turned on his back or on his healthy side; and then elevate the glans which is inside the prepuce to the outside with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated inwards.
And then heat the cautery having two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the glans until it penetrates from one side to the other; and the figure of the cauterization will then be of four cauterized heads.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count, and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place on the cauterized area cooked porridges with salt, and then keep the patient still, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a pointed cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened and the humidity is removed, and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
On the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors come into the stomach, so that it exceeds its capacity and remains multiplied within it, and if you continue the treatments and do not cure it, then the patient should lie supine, extend their hands and legs, and then cauterize it with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet, about the width of a finger, with a pointed cautery, and two cauteries below on either side of the cautery, so that the cautery forms a triangle; and space them so that they are not close together, which would cause rot.
And if the depth of the cautery is two-thirds the thickness of the finger.
And let the figure of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery in the form of a circle according to this form:
And let the stomach be cauterized, puncturing it where the cautery is feared: and it is that you mark on the stomach points according to the quantity you desire with a probe, and then cauterize those points with a cautery; and then treat it with the treatment that is previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
On the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain arises in the liver due to coldness, or humidity, or excessive flatulence, so that it is thrown from its normal composition by superfluous egress, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it does not help, then it is necessary that the patient be supine on his neck, and mark with a probe three cauteries according to this form and this same quantity on the liver under the hypochondria, where the patient's lying down is perceived.
And let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger, and let the cautery be according to the length of a straight finger.
And do not press too much with the cautery.
And if the quantity of what is burned is the amount of half the thickness of a finger, and no more should be applied; and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him be lying down, having already spread his thighs, and raise his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these places with a knife-cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And beware that you do not proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the thickness of a whole finger be burned so that the whole body is burned and penetrates to the intestines, because the finger is subtle here; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Of the perforation of an abscess of the liver with a cautery.
When there is an ulcer or abscess in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its tunic, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient finds heaviness and dull pain.
And if it is in the tunic of the liver, the pain is very sharp and sometimes the doctors are fatigued by its treatment.
Therefore, the patient should be turned onto their side; and then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then apply the cautery, which is shaped like a rake, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until all the abscess is consumed, until it reaches the bottom, until pus comes out.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this type of cautery be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is extensive, and in whose hands many illnesses have been treated;  however, let someone with experience sometimes dare to undertake this operation, but otherwise leave it to us, and it is better.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that a cautery of the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy, made in this manner, is effective.  And this is it: that you take a long, dry root of birthwort, one finger in length that is found, and let it be of the thickness of a finger.
Then immerse it in oil and hold it to the fire, and then cauterize with a cautery in the area that is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the clavicles, slightly inclined towards the part that is below the beard, and with two cauteries in the area that is declining between the fifth rib and the sixth, inclining slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another on the stomach; and with three cauteries on the back; and with a cautery in the area that is between the two shoulders, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary that you grasp your hand with the cautery; rather, let it be in the manifest work of the handle. (Meaning: Don't hold the cautery directly with your hand, use the handle).
And some ancients said that some men use a cautery of iron similar to a ray and ignite it, and place it in the area that is between the ribs, until it reaches that abscess and healthy flesh is formed, just as we have said in the case of an abscess of the liver.
But this perforation with a cautery is not without danger, because one can quickly kill the fistula in the place to which there is no healing.
Chapter 30.
On the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the disease of the spleen with what we have said about treatment in the division and your treatment does not succeed, then cauterize it in three ways, all of which are correct. One is to make three or four cauteries lengthwise according to the length of the spleen, following the shape of the liver cauteries, the shape of which has been previously described.
And let the width between each cautery be the breadth of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as that which we described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not press your hand on the cautery; and let the patient be lying on his or her side.
And another method of cauterization is to apply a cautery with two points, as we described in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and raise the rib which is in the region of the spleen where the patient lies on their left side.
And let your elevation, by which you raise the rib, follow the length of the body, so that the cauterizations are made according to the width of the body.
And then insert the two points, very hot, into that space, so that with them you penetrate the rib from the other side.
And then withdraw the cautery; and thus four cauterizations are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three points.
And then you treat the cautery until it has stopped discharging pus for many days, for this is more important than the other preceding actions in the treatment.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the cauterization of a dropsical patient.
Cautery is not applied in dropsy, but only in the case of [a swelling/inflammation -  'alcinte3' is likely a corruption or archaic term needing further context for precise translation].
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment which we have described in the division and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize him with four cauteries in a circle around the navel, and with one cautery on the stomach, and with another cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and with another cautery on the stomach. And the depth of the cautery should be approximately equal to the thickness of a finger.
And leave the cauterization open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not remove the patient from treatment near the cautery with what is necessary, so that two intentions are added to him, and he will be helped towards a speedy recovery.  (The phrasing is somewhat awkward;  it likely means don't interrupt the treatment prematurely, allowing for a more complete healing process.)
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its form is described above. And that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its shape is described above.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are inflamed with dropsy and filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the top of the foot, not in the space between the little toe and the ring finger. Raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; and then raise your hand, and do not completely lower it, because the yellow water will flow out.
The cautery is according to this form:  Then make two cauterizations on both thighs, and on each thigh, let a cauterization be made with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other below the middle of the thigh; and on each thigh two cauteries.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that some water may drain from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
On the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is afflicted by cold and humidity to the point that it weakens the virtues, that is, the digestive and retentive virtues which are in the stomach and intestines, and this is treated with methods of treatment and does not yield, and sometimes the patient suffers and cauterization is indicated, then cauterize it with a large cautery over the stomach as previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and with four small cauteries in the circumference of the navel, with a thin nail-like cautery; and with a cautery on the hypochondrium, large or with two cauteries.
And if sometimes the humors are exuberant and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize it over the pubic area and over the intestines, and quickly apply two cauteries to the stomach and near the inguinal regions, for it is a perfect and helpful treatment from which you will not err.
Chapter 34.
On the cauterization of chronic hemorrhoids.
When there are chronic hemorrhoids in the nature of thick, cold humors or corrupted humidities, and they are treated with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not work, then cauterize with three cauteries on the lowest part of the sacrum, small and slightly triangular, and with a cautery above the navel, placed as two fingers apart.
And if you suspect that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face inflamed, then cauterize that on the stomach with a large cautery as previously described; and with one cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the cauterization of swellings near their excision.
When you excise a swelling, then you ignite the cautery that is shaped like a ray. Then insert that ignited cautery into that excised swelling, and keep your hand there until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood comes.
And do this once or twice, for if the swellings are numerous, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and afterwards treat them with ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on someone, it is more effective in healing.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. On the cauterization of fistulas that are formed naturally in the private parts.
When nature does not obey the treatment that we have recommended in its place, and the patient is frightened by this, let it be healed with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in the anal region, and it is old,  it is filled with pus and corrupt humors.  Then what is most fitting to do is to probe it with a thin probe, and then determine the depth of it with the aforementioned probe.
Then ignite the cautery which resembles a ray (or rod). Then insert that ignited cautery into the fistula, according to the depth measured by the probe.
And repeat the cauterization until those corrupt bodies are burned, once, twice, or thrice, according to your necessity.
And beware of burning nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes to the body of the bladder or to the body of the bowel, then beware of all those places; and do not do this unless the fistula is in fleshy tissue and you think it is not penetrating (deeper structures).
Then care for the place until it is healed.
And if the place is sealed, and the matter is excised from it, and it remains so for a long time, then know that it is entirely cured.
And if the matter is not removed from it, then know that it is penetrating, or in the bone beneath it is corrupt, or this comes from those causes, the memory of which will come in its place (i.e., will be discussed later).
Chapter 37.
On the cauterization of wounds.
When in wounds pain arises from cold or excessive inflammation, and because of this the patient's body is weakened, then it is necessary that you cauterize that (area) over the wounds with a cautery, over each wound with a pointed cautery, of which the preparation has already been mentioned.
And quickly cauterize again a little below, so that three linear cauterizations result, and then they will be more helpful in healing.
Chapter 38.
On the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that (area) in the lowest part above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubic region begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side, and with another on the left.
And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the walnut; and cauterize that with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be nail-shaped, as is customary.
Chapter 39.
On the cauterization of the breast.
When cold and dampness come to the breast, and because of this pregnancy is prevented, and her menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and pain occurs with their arrival, then the place should be cauterized with three cauteries in the circumference of the navel, as we have said in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery on the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
On the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and a sign of this is that one thigh is longer than the other when compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it bulges.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient on the part of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the affected area as it is swollen, so that the affected area falls within the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the kind previously described in the cauterization of the stomach.
And if that cautery is not at hand, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be done according to the amount of swelling of the whole leg; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it doesn't work, and this is ancient practice, then you should purge the patient of gross humors with foul-smelling pills or artificial pills or similar; and then cauterize it.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
If according to two known methods, of which one is that you cauterize that area on the patient's hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of the thickness of a finger, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be in the middle of the area, on the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of a hip dislocation, so that it is a contained circle and burns the thickness of the entire hip.
And this is the method of cauterization in sciatica when the pain is confined to the hip and is not extended to the thigh.
But if it is extended to the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the place to which the patient points as the location of pain; and with one cautery on the kneecap, four finger-widths below the outer part (lateral side).
And if the cautery is knife-shaped, let the depth of the cautery be according to the thickness of the hip.
And if the patient indicates that the pain extends to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery in three or four points, or more if needed.
And if the patient indicates the pain to be below the buttock towards the thigh, then cauterize there with a knife-shaped cautery.
And be careful in all cauterization that you do not go so deep with the cautery as to burn nerves or major arteries, lest this cause harm or contraction to the patient.
And I have seen one and another of those whom I cauterized on the ankle, and went too deep in the cauterization, and the leg swelled until the swelling reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then a strong case of diarrhea followed, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places at once, cauterize each according to this method.
And some wise men say concerning the cauterization of the ankle, the cautery of which is this method: make a circle of iron, the circumference of which is half a palm; and let the opening be according to the thickness of a date pit or a little less.
And inside that circle, let there be another, a third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a walnut; and let those circles be open in two parts; and let the length of those irons be a walnut or two walnuts, and make in those irons a properly prepared mark in the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the part of the ankle, the patient lying on their side. Therefore cauterize that with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave that for three days; then plaster it with boder and leave the wound open for many days; and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
Says Albucasis: Oh, son, of this type of cautery we have used little because of the fear of its appearance, and because we have found few who dare to look upon it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and finds with it the place of it.  (This is a slightly awkward sentence; a better translation might be: "But the good cauteries are for him who endures them, and it finds the affected area.")
But cauterize with burning medicines, so that you make two circles having a perforated armature in which you place the hook of the iron door; let the length of the walls in the height of them be two nuts or whatever those are. (This sentence is very difficult to translate literally because of its archaic phrasing and unclear terminology.  The meaning is that a cauterizing instrument should be constructed in a specific way involving circles, an armature, and a hook, with the overall dimensions comparable to two nuts.)
And if the space which is between each is the quantity of the thickness of a finger; and they are open below and above, and they contain themselves between them according to this form: (The sentence describes the spacing and configuration of something, likely referring to a diagram not included in the text.)
Then place the patient on their side over the affected part of the hip, and press your hand on the hip area; then take warm water and pour it between the two circles, and the heat of it should be applied slowly, and hold it for about an hour, and let the patient suffer the biting and burning of it—because this causes a burning sensation like fire—until the burning stops.
Then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the warm water from the entire hip with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and apply a poultice until the blackness of the burn is removed, and leave it for some days, and pus will drain from it; this is the final treatment.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the hip or leg, apply a similar bath containing water as was done for the hip.
And this is the method for warm water, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, equal parts of each, and crush them and place them in a new pot, the bottom of which is perforated by a small hole, according to the amount that will enter the ray.  (The "ray" here likely refers to a specific instrument or location, perhaps a tube or body part).
And place another inverted pot below the bottom of the pot, and pour over the alkali and quicklime fresh water which covers them by one finger's breadth; and when you compress them with your hand with good compression, and allow the sharp water to descend from above into the inverted pot below.
And then collect all that water, and pour it over fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be of very strong acidity, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterizing other limbs, because it acts like fire.
And some medicines with which it is also cauterized are:  honey,  vinegar, and lime with soap, mixed together in this way.
And Galenus recounts the operation and cure of a man from ancient times by which pain in the hip and sciatic nerve was cured, and greatly praised the excellence of this method, and said that it needed no other cure, and that he was healed at once as was recounted, for he quickly entered the bath and was free from it.
And it is that fresh rue should be taken, and if fresh rue is not found, a fresh fig should be taken; and it should be crushed very well with a little lard and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and bandaged.
And it should be left on for three hours, or for as long as the patient feels a decrease in the burning sensation.
Then put him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, expose him to the sun, for the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that one should not use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
But already Dioscorides said that when the varicose vein is cauterized with goat's dung, it profits him much; and the cautery should be done in this manner: wool should be taken and soaked in old oil, and placed between the toes and the ankle.
Then take dried goat dung and burn it until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it until it is extinguished. Then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the feeling of the cauterization reaches the ankle and relieves the pain.
And this manner of cautery is called cautery of goats. (or, more literally:  And this method of cautery is called goat cautery.)
Chapter 42.
On the cauterization of toothache.
Sometimes pain is in the teeth from various causes, such as a fall or a blow or excessive evacuation, and from similar causes, by infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cautery is only suitable for that single type which is caused by an effusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after evacuation of the patient with purging pills and similar remedies,  make incisions on the tooth where the pain is located, three lines according to its width, marking with ink on each line five cauteries or more, depending on the patient's tolerance and their strength.
And let the cauterization be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries using a nail-shaped cautery, according to this form:
Chapter 43.
On cauterization at the beginning of gibbosity (or hunchback).
Often this illness happens to small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it occurs in their spine with elevation and movement.
And you find in the last vertebra of the back and neck that it begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And so when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize that with a cautery that is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in all parts of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the circumference of the vertebra with the pointed cautery two or three lines. And let the points be close to each other.
And then care for the place until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered in a swelling that is caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
On the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pains are caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is customary for doctors to call this gout properly.
And so if gout caused by cold is cured with a method of treatment and the pains are not removed, then cautery removes them.
And it is that you cauterize that near evacuation in the area of the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains rise into the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize that area on each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize that area, and do not press too hard with your hand and the cautery beforehand; let it only be around the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains rise into both knees, then administer the cautery which we have described in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands, and it is called a type of gout, then puncture in the circle of the veins as they wind around, puncture along those two lines.
And if the pains remain in the fingers, then puncture in the circle of the veins on each knuckle and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains rise to the sides, the hips or the muscles in a few days, then cauterize them on every side of the affected areas.
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and reception of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of fractures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, with the intestines protruding in front of the scrotum, and this is beginning to happen, then you must order the patient to abstain from food that day and to use lenitive measures so that the hernia may descend from his abdomen.
Then hold the patient between your hands over his groin, and instruct him to hold his breath until the hernia or intestine is expelled or appears.
Then return that [the hernia/intestine] with your fingers and gently push the rupture back onto the pubic bone, noting that its extremity should resemble a semicircle in the upper parts of the body.
Then heat the cautery, which is of this form:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not protrude.
And you have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion beneath them, and another servant should be at their legs, and another at their feet, who should hold their hands.
Then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and hold it there until you reach with it the bone of the penis to the middle of the scrotum; and repeat this another time if you did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And observe wisely the position of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn the intestine, lest death or other great harm come upon the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, do not consider your operation complete.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small according to their size and measure.
Then care for the cauterized area for three days with a poultice until the burn scab is softened.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and the wound are sealed/healed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which will cleanse his belly, and that the intestine does not come out due to the effort of healing.
And then, when he wants to get up after about forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be relieved of work and the overconsumption of food and drink, and of loud voices, for when he uses this regimen of complete healing, he will be completely healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen; and if, at the beginning, you want them not to protrude, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that which is excised from the wound be about one third of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is sufficient.
And some earlier authorities said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this shape, as it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and you apply your hand with a pointed cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion. (or: On cauterizing a torsion/twist)
When pain develops in some limb due to a fall or a blow, and that pain persists for a long time and does not yield to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cauterization of each limb be according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and its pain.
Therefore, if it heals with the first cauterization, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization; for it is customary for such pains to be punctured from one limb to the neighboring one, wherefore it is necessary to treat those with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Concerning the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes helped greatly by cautery, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so, if you wish to cauterize a leper, consider if the leprosy is spreading and if it has been treated with what we have said in the division and it has not ceased, nor remains, and you fear that the corruption will encompass the whole body and complexion of the patient, then cauterize the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and a second just below it, towards the forehead at the hairline; and two more on either side of the head, like horns; and one in the back, on the nape of the neck.
And finally, with the cautery, press lightly so that a small impression is made on the bone, and the scabs are dislodged from it, so that the expulsion of thick vapors is relieved; and cauterize over the spleen as needed.
And if the leprosy is already widespread on the patient and manifestly apparent, then you should cauterize with the cauteries we mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large cautery on the vertebrae near the coccyx, and one on the coccyx, and with two on the hips, and one on each buttock, and with two on the knees, and one on each knee, and with two on the ankles, and with two on the muscles, and with two on the tendons of his feet; and cauterize on all the joints of the fingers of the hands and feet, and on each heel of his feet, and on the knuckles of his hands.
And beware of the nerves that are on the heel, so as not to burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.
And know that the more precisely you apply cauteries, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as healthy people do, because his body is already numbed.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as is prescribed by the types of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On cauterization of numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes a numbness is cauterized which extends to the hands and feet, and to the vertebrae of the back, near the emergence of the nerves which move that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not presume to do this except one who is learned in the anatomy of the limbs and the emergence of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of white patches which is morphea.
When a white patch is old and you apply no ointments of medicine to it, then you cauterize it with a cautery iron slightly, according to the quantity and nature of the patch, until the whiteness and its color are relieved, and its color is changed.
And then treat it with flour of barley and lentils with rose oil and with leaves of arnaglossa, and with pigeon's blood or raven's blood; let each be a part and all be mixed, and then spread a cloth and place it over the area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you wish to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around its circumference as it is swollen.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with the pointed cautery in the middle; but to me this is not advisable, for I fear that it will become ulcerated, and I have already seen this happen several times.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 51.
On the cauterization of a boil/abscess.
When a boil is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the superfluity causing it, or the age of the patient, because they are old and decrepit, with little blood, or due to the time of year, and you want to hasten the maturation of the boil; then cauterize around its circumference with many small, punctate cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you want to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the boil until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in a place where there is pus below, so that the body of the abscess is relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the cauterization of corrosives.
A corrosive is a corruption moving in the limb, which corrodes it like fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place that can tolerate cautery with fire, then you immediately cauterize with small, hot nails, large or small according to what is appropriate for the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it entirely until you eradicate all the corrosion and nothing of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and place crushed sulfur mixed with oil on the cauterized areas until the scabs are completely eradicated and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with a flesh-generating ointment.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh has grown where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted places.
