And corrosion is sometimes treated with a sharp medicine, for that is a remedy in place of cautery. But however, cautery with fire is of more readily available help.
And I have already mentioned the treatment of it with sharp medicine in the section, and therefore take that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
Of the cauterization of wounds and other similar things.
Many times, a disease called a wound happens in the parts under the foot, and it is a large, penetrating injury which injures the foot; and cautery in those cases is according to two methods, that is cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
But cautery with fire is that you heat the concave cautery resembling a vulture's beak, which should be made of iron according to the size which contains the wound in all parts, and should be of fine edges.
And then place that heated iron on the wound, and then move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed slough. And then plaster it with crushed wild mallows mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it is not converted, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you must apply cautery with water to those areas.  Take a cannula of bronze or iron or a vulture's feather, and place it over the wound; then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand, and you rotate it with the pressure of your hand overall, and let the edge of the cannula be pressed into the root of the wound, and the hot water will find its way to the root of the wound; and let the patient endure the bite of the hot water for a while, about an hour, and then remove it, for the wound with its roots will be eradicated.
And do the same with those and then the others until you have treated all those that are in the body.
And then treat those, once the roots of the wounds are eradicated, with ointments that generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On the cauterization of tremors.
When someone suffers from tremors due to cold in the nerves, or from a fever of cold origin, or a similar cause, then you should cauterize them with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, using an olive-shaped cautery.
And his tremor will subside, and hasten the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
On the cauterization of accidental blemishes to the body.
When blemishes appear on the body, and there are pustules and fissures caused by thick, corrupted matter; then, at the beginning of their appearance, it is necessary that they be cauterized on the head of the fistula with a fine cautery with myrtle wood, the tip of which is heated in the fire; or with a long root of erestologia; or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes the cautery is also applied at the beginning of its expulsion in this manner; and they are not believed, and the superfluity is broken up by making them, and heals from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done near evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56. On the cauterization of a blood flow accident caused by an arrow wound.
Many times there is a flow of blood from an arrow wound that is incised near the wound, which flows outward, or near the incision of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and then it looks similar, and it is difficult to stop that.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you press with your hand to the opening of the arrow wound, and place your finger over it tightly many times until the flow of blood is stopped under your finger, and it does not come out.
And then put small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
And then take one of those, small or large depending on the location and wound where the artery is broken,  on that vein quickly after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding is stopped and constrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and withdraw the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from among the cauteries which are prepared in the fire, and then do not cease to do this one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that another ailment comes upon the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to stop the bleeding in those cases, and especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction if it is not extracted already, because when it is extracted the extremities are best burned and the blood is stopped, or that it be tied with thread or a ligature with strong constriction, or that medicines be placed on it whose virtue is to stop blood; and these are tightened with a cushion with appropriate compression.
But that which administers appropriate constriction with ligature and with tightness with cloths or by the application of cauterizing agents and the like, does not provide relief except late.
But if this happens to someone and the physician or medicine is not present, then gently press with the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and apply great pressure to it until the blood is stopped from the main part of the wound, and the finger should not be removed from the artery; and above the artery let it be sprinkled with very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and consider whether cautery or medicine is needed.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their similars.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire and with burning medicine is suitable, and its occasions and causes, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one seeking this will be relieved, then on that which he will have requested.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter is that which is in the first chapter on cauterization, but in a less frightening way.
And because it is necessary that there be great caution, for in the operations which are in this chapter, many times there is an evacuation of blood, with which life depends, because of the opening of a vein or incision on an abscess, or opening of a wound, or purification of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision of a stone, and the like. And all of these are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in most of them death occurs.
And I admonish you that this hesitation should not fall upon you, because sometimes it happens that you will deal with men with many kinds of illnesses. And some of them become angry with their illness and death is light for them; this is because of the intensity of what they suffer from their illness, and because of the length of their illness, and with the illness is a fear which signifies death.
And there are those who give you their possessions and say that you are hoping for their health, and their illness is pernicious.
Therefore, it is not fitting that you consent to those who come to you who are of this kind; and your caution should be greater than covetousness and your greed; and do not presume anything about this except with certain knowledge which verifies this to you, and to which a laudable outcome follows.
And use in the treatment of all your patients prognostication and indication of him to whom health is restored, for in this is to you aid in acquiring fame and memory, praise and glory.
O, sons, may God inspire you with His understanding and not withhold from you guidance and protection, for this is in His hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that this which you ask for and of which you have need may be alleviated for you.
Chapter One.
Of the treatment of the water which is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often happens to children during childbirth and when the nurse compresses the child's head without skill.
And sometimes it happens for a hidden cause which is unknown.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those whom I saw, death followed, and thus it is to me a work left undone in those cases.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could not support himself because of the size of his head, and moreover the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity is either collected between the skin and the bone, or it is collected under the bone on the skull.
For the operation in that case, if the humidity is between the skin and the bone and the abscess is small, then you should make an incision in the middle of the head with a fissure in width, and the length of the fissure should be about two nuts so that the humidity flows out, and this is the form of a spatula:
And so if the humidity is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two incisions in this manner:
And if the humidity is under the bone, the sign is that you see the sutures of the head opened on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress it with your hand on the inside parts, and this is not hidden from you. You should make an incision in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
And then extract all the humidity from the incision; and then bind the incisions with cloths and feathers; and then pour wine and oil over it until the fifth day, and then untie the binding and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little humidity until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore aim with the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood, and the patient dies from that blood flow with evacuation of humidity.
Chapter Second (or Chapter Two).
When someone develops cataracts or rheum in the eyes or feet, and this is chronic, and the cure is not achieved through medicines, then the last resort is incision of those two arteries (referring to the arteries near the ears mentioned previously).
And therefore, when you wish to incise them, have the patient shave their head, and then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear; and then the patient's neck should be constricted with a cloth; and then consider where the vein pulses; and the locations of each, above the places which are near the ears, are compressed, and thus hidden except in some men.
And then mark the artery with a probe, and then incise each one with a sharp lancet on each side, making the incision reach the bone, and this should be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and cut it at the incisions above.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers, because when a vein is incised, the blood comes out with a pulsatile flow, which spurts out frequently.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you must measure from the ear a space of three fingers, and then mark it with a probe, and cut down to the bone.
And the amount of blood that should be let is about six ounces, and quickly let more or less, and all this depends on the strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
And then consider the wound, and if any cause of the injury remains on the bone, then incise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
Then bind the wound with feathers and linen bandages, and treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrid humors and a quality in the tissues of the temples, or if it is an old, severe headache similar to these, and it is treated with medicinal methods of healing and this does not help, then in these illnesses we must attempt the extraction of arteries in the temples, or their cauterization, as we have shown.
And the method of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hair which is on the temples; and attend to the artery apparent in the temple, for it is shown to you by its pulse, and is hidden except in few men by the force of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the neck of the patient should be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the place with a rough cloth or evaporate the area with warm water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you carefully scrape the skin with that [spatula] until you reach the artery; and then put in it [the artery] ointment and extract it from the superficial parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are beneath it, from every part.
But if the artery is small, then wrap it with the end of the oncii [a surgical instrument], and then cut a part of it according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened and constricted, and there will be no flow of blood, for if they are not incised the blood will not rise at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with cords at a distance, and do not allow it to putrefy before the consolidation of the wound and a flow of blood occurs.
And then incise the excess which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone with a knife-shaped cautery until its ends are incised, and it will be in place of that same operation.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or is of a hot complexion; because cautery is one of those things that helps drying humors, and therefore it is better in youth.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you cleanse the place with old linen, and that powders are decently applied, and then it should be treated with dry medicines causing the flesh to grow and with bandages until it is healed.
And if, while you operate another time, a flow of blood from the artery occurs, then you must take care to stop that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the place with linen, and press with your hand until the bleeding is stopped. And if none of those things are at hand, then place your finger on the place until the blood is congealed, and bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and it is tightened as necessary.
And what is lighter in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and place each one, very hot, until they reach the bone and sever the vein in two places, so that what is between its extremities is lengthened, because it is not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be equal. However, if there is less sharpness than two knives it is better, because if they are sharp like two knives, it approaches each cold, and does not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since in those there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is held in them and the flesh is severed lightly.
And this method is better in every way and more gentle.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the amount of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter four.
Chapter Four: On the treatment of sharp and acrid watery eyes.
When there are acrid tears, and it is the flow from those veins which are on the head's surface, and you find no cause for it in the medicinal treatment, and you see the patient's face redden, and feel in their forehead a crawling as if of ants, and their eyes are strained, humid, so that the hairs around them are stuck together, and their eyelids are swollen by the acridity of the tears, the ancients had much skill in treating this, and they would treat this with a sponge.
And you should command the patient to shave the hairs which are on their forehead, and then incise three fissures in the forehead, equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure is two fingers: one fissure should be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length, a little from the movement of the muscles which are in the tear duct, and the third on the other side.
And beware of incising the arteries which are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jaws. And if the space of each incision is the quantity of three fingers, adjust it.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or dried cloths, use them to dry the blood.
And then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that [tool/spatula] the entire area which is between the two incisions, with the cyphus which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a curette, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that [curette] against the flesh attached to the bone, and its blunt edge inside the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle fissure, and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to that part of the bone, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed, press the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old linen, and put on those powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day untie the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm or tepid water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5:  On the treatment of tears due to cataracts and those descending into the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone suffers from acute and persistent cataracts, and the patient's eyes appear small and shrunken, and their vision is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their hair falls out, and there is a sharp, throbbing pain in the forehead and consequent sneezing, then know from these symptoms that the cataracts and humors come from the inner parts of the head.
And the best cure in such cases is this:  order the patient to shave their forehead, and then incise a fissure in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (beginning the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision reach the bone; and lengthen your cut from the temples, which are sore due to chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two edges of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then bind it tightly with powders soaked in wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind it and see that the abscess has diminished, then you should scrape the bone until new flesh begins to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes the growth of flesh, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, mixing them together, and making a plaster from them to be applied to these wounds to encourage the growth of new flesh.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of what falls into the ears.
Every substance that falls into the ears is one of four types:  either it is a mineral stone or something similar to stone, such as iron or glass; or it is a plant, such as a grain of wheat or a bean; or it is a fluid substance, such as water or sour wine and similar things; or some kind of animal.
And so, when a stone falls into the ear, or another substance similar to those things that do not soften or grow in the ear, and you expose the ear to the sun, and if you see the stone, then instill into the ear some violet oil or sesame oil, and then proceed with its extraction, and induce sneezing with a suitable remedy, and block the nostrils before the onset of sneezing. Then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and gently pull the ear upwards, for often it is expelled by these means.
And if it is not expelled, then proceed with its extraction using the instrument called a fine ear-pick, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the ear-pick, it is good; and if not, then proceed with its extraction using a thin probe of little doubling (or slight curvature).
And so, if it is not expelled by this, then make a small brass tube (cannula) and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear and seal around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open except the tube. Then extract it with your breath using strong suction, for often with this which we have recounted (this method) it is removed.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small amount of kindled (lit) tow or a similar material prepared for catching birds, and place that on the end of a thin rod, and then carefully wrap a suitable cotton (or similar soft material) around that. Then carefully insert that into the opening of the ear, and then dry the ear of its moisture.
And so, if it is not expelled by all the methods which we have recounted, then you should consider cutting it before it develops into a hot abscess or spasm.
And the manner of cutting it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to the patient's strength, and then hold the ear between your hands and turn the ear upward, and make a small incision at the root of the ear above the cartilage, in the well-prepared area. And let the incision be crescent-shaped so that it reaches the stone; and, the stone found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then immediately close the incision quickly, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause residing in the ear is a growth that increases and is inflamed, then administer its treatment and extraction; but if it does not obey your treatment, then take finely powdered litharge according to this method: and apply it to the excision of that growth which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and apply it in small pieces, and then extract them with tweezers or with a fine instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thus facilitated.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing as a first measure, and fill the ear with some old cotton, and lie down on the ear into which the water has entered; and if it is expelled by this, it is good; and if not, then take several heated stones or pebbles, according to the length of the finger, and warm them a little in the fire, and the patient inserts one of them into the opening of the ear and jumps on one foot on that side and strikes with another stone on the stone which is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water is not expelled in this way, take some paper or tow, and put one end of it in the ear and hold the other end to the fire until the majority of it is burned.
Then put in more tow and do this several times until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula, as was said above of the extracting stone.
But in the extraction of an animal entering that ear, if it is small, like a fly or similar, treat it as we have said in the division; but if it is of a large size, which appears to the eye's judgment, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks, and this cause is the easiest of all the other causes that befall the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment does not work, then you must expose the ear to the sun.
And so if any cause of the worms appears, then extract it with forceps or with fine hooks; but if no cause appears, then take a cannula narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And introduce the thin end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it with strong suction, and do this many times until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you plug and seal the circumference of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the treatment of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and flat above.
And then let it be such that it pushes what is in the cavity of the cannula continuously where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a ray (possibly referring to a thin, flexible instrument) and wrap its extremity decently with cotton, and then pour oil into the cavity of the ear, or the juice of some herb, or whatever you wish of these medicines into the cannula — and that is in the ear — and then insert the ray with the cotton on top and press with your hand with moderate compression until the oil is expelled into the cavity of the hearing and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is poured into the ear should already be slightly warmed by fire. And be careful that what you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of ear obstructions.
Sometimes children are born from their mothers' wombs without perforated ears, and this also happens in some adults, an obstruction in the ear canal due to injury or fleshy growth in it; and this obstruction sometimes occurs in the entrance of the ear canal, which prevents hearing, and sometimes in the outer parts.
And that which happens at its entrance, which prevents hearing, is in most cases of difficult healing.
And in the case where hearing is possible, you should expose the patient's ear to the sun and examine it. And then, if you see a visible obstruction, open it with a fine spatula, of which this is the form:
If its extremity is narrow, one side being sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be two fingers wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or in its meatus, then remove that with fine hooks and incise it vigorously until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the anterior part of the ear, then take a thin, fine rod and heat it slightly with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And then, if you have felt an obstruction with which the patient experiences difficulty, due to the severity of hearing, force the obstruction out gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a piece of linen, according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take a wick and soak it in warm water, and then crush and apply it; and if you see that the ear is already abscessed, then you must remove the wick and replace it with another wick which you will wrap in a cerate made with rose oil, until the abscess is reduced; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear, and use the other treatment which stops the bleeding, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which occurs on the eyelids.
Styes which occur on the eyelids of the eyes are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with an oncia, and incise them with a spatula from their roots, and then apply a poultice to the area.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a thin lenticular cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains from their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often there develops in the eyelids of the eyes a certain condition which resembles swelling in its firmness and hardness, and because of its size it is called swelling.
And it is an accumulation of thick humidity in the eyelid, below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you consider then if the swelling is proceeding in him, appearing from the eyelid and is spread to every part; and its cause is light.
Then make an incision on it lengthwise, and then peel it off completely easily until it is removed, and then suspend it with hooks and excise it.
And if the lesion is large, then you should close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will suffer no harm from it because the ointment will restore and heal it.
And if the growth is leaning towards the inner parts of the eyelid beyond the inner surface, then evert the eyelid and suspend the growth with hooks,  before you make any incision, and press it from all sides, and if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, this harms nothing.
Then, after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with a healing agent until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a growth on the upper eyelid.
A growth (or a tumor, depending on the specific meaning of "axcirnath") is a gray mass which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and mostly in children.  It aggravates their eyes and causes a great deal of pain, so that they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears are constantly produced.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and their lower eyelids, beneath their eyebrows, are moist, already showing swelling, and the appearance of a growth is imminent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the growth within.
And a certain method of operation is this: the patient places his head in your lap, and you place a cloth beneath him and make him comfortable, and make a circle of appropriate size to encompass the entire growth.  Then place that circle over the growth and press with your fingers all around so that the growth is brought into the middle of the circle.  Then, make an incision into the middle of the moisture with a sharp instrument, making a slit; and the slit should not be larger than the incision made in bloodletting (phlebotomy).
But in the beginning, it is necessary that you completely cut through the skin until you reach the growth, because in most cases the growth is incised to reveal a white, fatty substance.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers at the edges, and roll it between your right and left hand until it is completely removed.
And be careful not to push too deeply into the incision, so that with the instrument you do not reach the eye and injure it.
But if the growth does not appear at the beginning of the incision, then gently probe a little further into the incision until the growth is reached; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in wine and vinegar, and apply them to the area, and compress it with powders.
And some crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision so that it draws out any remaining moisture.
And then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the site, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the various ways of touching the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes there are growths beneath the natural hairs and they are inflamed, then these harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and membranes, until this becomes the cause of the destruction of the eye, which is called touching the eye (tactinir del huel).  And it is treated in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with acute medicine as previously stated in the chapter on cautery; or the touch is made by incision and suture; or by cannulas, as I shall explain.
Therefore, you should place the patient's head on your lap; then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand. If it is turned, good; but if not, insert a needle into the part where the matter is below the eyelid, and push the needle upwards through the matter — near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow — near the hairs, and pull the matter upwards with the eyelid, and turn it back with the ray (presumably a surgical instrument).
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid beneath the inflamed hairs, with a small sharp instrument, from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller; then extract the matter and place small pieces of cotton or cloth under the eyelid; and then sew it up over the eyelid with thread, and let it be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of elevation of the eyelid, for this varies in people, because some need a large incision of the eyelid according to the extent to which the eyelid is softened.
Then make (an incision) with the small sharp instrument along the two lines which you have sewn, beginning from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, let it be about the thickness of a king's (presumably a royal finger or some other royal measure of thickness).
Then place the hook in one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape it all, and then join the two lips of the wound with glue or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and stick what superfluously protrudes from the threads to two eyebrows with some sticky substance, if you wish to do so; and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture of the threads for three or four days, and then treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without suture, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised due to the sealing of the wound and its aggregation; but suture is better.  Therefore, the ancients advised this.
However, let there be diligence concerning the patient, and it is a good operation, and let there be no fear.
And there is another way to treat it, which is to mark on the eyelid a figure like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three hooks arranged and joined according to this form:
But if this is not possible, to hold the hooks, and this is not equal, then take a needle which has thread and place it in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads between your fingers equally, and then with those, lift your hand with equal elevation, raising the entire area which is marked as we have described.
And then join the two lips of the wound with a suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointment.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted; then it is necessary that you use on it that which we have said of incision; but suture in each case is more skillful.
To stop bleeding with canes is according to this method: and it is that you turn back the eyelid and cut the growth that is inside as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two thin canes or splinters whose length is according to the length of the eyelid and whose width is less than the width of the space. And you have already incised at the extremities of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully add each [cane] subtly over what is superfluous of the eyelid's skin, and bind the canes to each part with a firm ligature, and leave it for some days, for the growth, by the constriction, will die and dry up and will rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it off with forceps, and then care for the wound until it is healed.
Then, once it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of the two canes which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Chapter 12. Of the elevation of stinging hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is hard.
When unnatural, hard, rough, and stinging hairs are in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a subtle needle and place on it a subtle silk and lion thread, and then you adjust its ends, knotting each in a very subtle manner. And let the length of the threads be the quantity of a palm (handspan).
And then you prepare two other subtle threads underneath it briefly, and tie their ends if you wish.
And then have the patient put their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is illuminated, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the offending hair, which needs to be extracted, and make it penetrate the eyelid swiftly until you extract it above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair upwards and insert it into a solution, and let the hairs be one or two or three and no more.
And then pull your hand with that until the hair is removed with the solution from the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if the solution is pulled and the hair is not removed, you will pull the solution to the lower parts with the thread which you have composed with it, until the solution is removed from the upper opening.
And observe that with the vision of the eye, for then you will remove the solution and the thread, and gently cleanse and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is too short, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is elevated with that, or you leave it until it is lengthened by some space, and then you elevate it.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of  [a condition, likely a stye or similar growth] which appears on the upper eyelid.
When a [condition, likely a stye or similar growth] appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound, incision, or cautery, and it is called harelip [likely referring to a similar type of eyelid malformation], and this [condition] is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the healing of a wound, incision, or cautery, or by something similar that has been poorly treated.
The manner of the operation is that you incise that adhesion and separate its lips, and place between them a small piece of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not fitting that you use treatments that dry or constrict; for if you do this, the [condition] will return worse than it was before.
Use on it things that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply with water in which mallow and its seeds are boiled, or diachylon ointment, which should be liquefied with some oil, and immerse the cloth in it, and treat with that.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you should prevent, by all means, consolidation from occurring in a manner different from its original state.
And this operation which we have described is nothing but a rectification of the [condition] so that it does not return to a state different from its original form.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the treatment of [a condition, likely a swelling or inflammation] which occurs in the lower eyelid.
This swelling which occurs in the lower eyelid, which is truly called a swelling; and it is of [two] kinds, some natural, others accidental.
But accidental [swellings] are caused by a wound, or by a cut, or by cautery, or by other similar things.
And the method of its operation is that you take a needle with double thread, and place that in the flesh, and make it pass from the left lachrymal duct to the right lachrymal duct, until the thread reaches the two extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle and sew it with the needle, and incise it with a wide spatula. And then if the shape of the eyelid returns as it should be, it is good; and if not, take a splint and place it in the cleft, and with that splint reshape the eyelid, and sew the two fissures at the edge of the lower eyelid.
And let the extremities of the two fissures be at two angles of the incision which you have made so that they meet, and let an angle be made of them so that when they come together a figure is formed from them.
And this figure is a Greek letter: lambda.
Then remove the flesh from that side to the extent that the lower edge of the wound is acute, so that it may be the eyelid.
Then join the separated parts and sew them with wool thread; and then treat them with what we have said of softening medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if it is excoriated because of a fissure, suture, or cautery, then it is necessary that you make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid according to what has been previously described. Then separate [the wound] with a poultice between two lips, as we have said.
In summary, of the treatment of excoriation, whether above or below, the operation proceeds as it is prepared according to the form of the excoriation, and this is in the form of many variations.
And the skillful artisan always prepares the tools in every way possible for him so that he may restore the shape to its natural form, or close to it, and the patient according to what suits him for the operation and instruments in the best circumstances/disposition.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation happens to many men such that the upper eyelid is solidified with the white of the eye, and the ease of movement and its natural functions are impeded, then it is necessary that you insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid, and lift it up, or spread it with an unguent.
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharp in its incision, being rather blunt so that the patient is not disturbed by the operation, and move the spatula so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision be such that it separates that adhesion easily until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and the whole adhesion is healed.
And then you put salt water in the eye with which you wash it; or distill into it some honey dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small piece of linen, and place on the eye wool soaked in egg white, and then on the third day use consolidative remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types, for it is either nervous, and resembles hard, thin parchment; or it is non-nervous, resembling a congealed white humidity, which when an iron touches it, or is forced to be taken with tweezers, is severed and the tweezers do not penetrate it.
And both types do not begin except from the greater lacrimal gland, until it grows little by little, covering the vision and prohibiting light and eye movement.
But the manner of operating in its incision is that the patient places his head on your lap, and then you open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with small, double-ended tweezers and extend it toward the upper parts. Then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or oxhair, or cowhair, or something similar, or strong thread, or double the tip of the needle little by little and place it in the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it towards the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the edge of the nail which covers the pupil. And thus you follow it with the hair to its back (or root/origin).
And then you incise the remainder at the root of the lacrimal duct with a fine spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the lacrimal gland so that continuous tear flow does not result from the incision of it.
And sometimes the nail, with only tweezers extended or with thread, as we have said, and then it should be scraped with a lion's spatula, of which this is the form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then, into the incision, put a little crushed salt or a powder made of cyani (or possibly a plant similar in name and effect), and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with what is appropriate until it is healed.
And if the nail is not inflamed and you cannot insert the needle, nor is it embedded in it with tweezers, then often this type of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed as I have recounted in the division (section/chapter).
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the sore of the patient and take a thin lion's spatula of sharp steel, according to this form: with subtlety/precision. And if you see that it is rarefied and is shrinking and becoming embedded in the root, then distill from your alembic onto the sore a powder made of cyani (or a similar plant) or with rue powder or with crushed salt, and cover the sore until the next day.
And then repeat the operation on it until all that is in it is removed, unless on the contrary a hot abscess forms in the wound, for then you leave that, and treat the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it with reason until it is healed.
And the prominence of the lachrymal flesh, if it is noticeable in the wound to a great extent, should be suspended with hooks; and incise it in part, and do not prolong the incision so that you do not cut the flow of tears.
And then distill onto the lachrymal duct rue and cyani powder until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the incision of a felon and of that which is born of flesh attached in the wound.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is born and is super-imposed until it covers the pupil, or approaches or tightens on the eyelids, and quickly compresses the eyelids to the outside parts, and is similar to the rose of a balustrade.
But the operation in this case is that the patient rests his head on your lap; and then open the eyes of that one and collect all that flesh with forceps convenient for this, and hold that with a forceps or with scissors; and then incise the flesh at the beginning until all is finished with the lancet, and be careful of the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And let your incision, with which you incise those, be with a spatula which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which the hair is collected, according to the form that will come to you for this chapter.
Then, when your incision is complete and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt, or distill into it a red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place a cotton compress over the outside of the eye with egg white until it is secured from a hot abscess.
And if some cause of inflammation remains and hot blood is discharged, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and cure that abscess with what relieves that abscess, and then operate on it again until it is healed, and similarly do in the attached flesh which happens in the eye of this kind.
But however, you should avoid any treatment of the weak eye by natural or accidental means, working with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that the member is enduring that operation.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is thick, red veins which are stubborn on the eye and prevent its treatment, and they weaken the eye over a long period of days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and there is no other disease in it other than pus; then you should collect that pus.
And it is that you instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with an ounce or two, according to your judgment; and let that ounce be of subtle, doubled fabric, according to this form:
or two ounces, gradually, in one piece according to this form:
And then let those veins be collected with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins are removed from the eye and are retracted.
And be careful that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is in the midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation, that you incise only those veins.
And when you have completed [the procedure], then you distil into the eye verdigris or alum so that with its sharpness it corrodes what remains of the hair.
But if this is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster everything with something that relieves his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is soothed and the risk of a hot abscess is secured [i.e., avoided].
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Concerning the reduction of a nasal fracture.
When you treat a fracture with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as is described previously, and it is not healed, then there is no pus in it, but rather fissures on the abscess before its maturation, and you must extract all the moisture that is in it and the pus until the bone is uncovered.
And then, when the bone is uncovered and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a rasp, and it should be made of Indian iron, and its head should be round like a hazelnut, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle pointing (as is shown in the following form).
And place that [rasp] on the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the area with drying and styptic substances.
Then, if the area is consolidated and good flesh has grown there, and the body of the pus is excised and remains for forty days, and it is not renewed and is not festering and no accident befalls it, then you will know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is nothing but to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and according to this manner: that is, you uncover the second bone with an iron or with a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a trephine according to this form:
Let the extremity of the iron [trephine] be triangular, and its shaft be slender and tapering to the extremity, like this.  (The "like this" refers to a likely accompanying diagram.)
Then place that [trephine] on the bone, and let it be near the lachrymal bone; and extend your hand a little from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the trephine until it penetrates the whole bone, and the patient will feel it because they will feel air escaping when you hold your hand.
Then restore the area with styptic and drying medicines, as has been taught to you.
Then, when the flesh grows and the area hardens, the matter which flowed to the outer parts returns to the nose; therefore this is easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the reduction of the protrusion of the eye.
When the eye protrudes entirely and is not injured by a wound or diminution, then you should leave the patient, and afterward follow him, and afterward apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification and suction for good suction; and afterward place on the eye a liniment which is made of acacia, aloe, enses [likely a misspelling or variant of a herb, perhaps *ensifolia* or similar], and sarcocolla, and afterward fasten the plaster over the eye with much powder.
And the manner of the powders is that you take many linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded into four layers; and make [a poultice] of these.
And afterward place these one upon another according to the amount that the eye needs. And afterward bind over these with a firm cloth with a strong binding; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and afterward remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if meanwhile the ligament is softened, then it should be very tightly bound.
And afterward remove the binding during the day and night, as we have said.
And if at times the eye is already reduced, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the binding and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Of the incision of the eminence of the uvea tunic.  (Note: "uvea tunic" refers to the uvea, the pigmented layer of the eye.)
When there is a rupture in the uvea tunic, and it is prominent and protrudes beyond the eyelids like a grape, and the person's form is marred by it, and you want to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted, that you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from below to above, and then place another needle with double thread on the side of the lacrimal duct, and make that penetrate and leave the first needle according to its position; and then incise the place with the double thread and lift with it the part of the uvea around the needle, and tighten that tightly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and allow the thread with the uvea to remain.
And then treat the eye with something that will comfort it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes collect from all sides with a circular cloth around the eye a large prominent swelling, and then pierce it with a fine spatula that reaches the pupil of the eye, and run the albuginea humor, and the eye descends and enters immediately. (Note:  "albuginea humor" likely refers to a type of fluid or aqueous humor in the eye).
And then tighten it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of alconati. (Note: "Alconati" is likely a now-obsolete or regionally specific term.  It might refer to a specific disease or condition, perhaps a type of eye inflammation or injury.)
This illness that is called alconati is nothing but a swelling which is located in the eye, similar to descending water, but it is not that. (The phrasing suggests a distinction between the "alconati" swelling and a simple watery discharge or inflammation).
But the way to work on it is to make the patient sit on a prepared and straight chair, and then take his head in your hands on both sides and massage it until you see the swelling descend to the lower parts, and then it should be dried so that it does not descend, and light should be blocked, and the patient should see things as he used to.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water. (Referring to the fluid in the eye; if it doesn't move downward it's simple fluid, not the specific condition "alconati".)
But if the descent of the swelling is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a fine spatula and with it, on the pannicle, gently scrape below the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the corona (towards the empty space), until the swelling is expelled. (This describes a more invasive procedure using a small, fine instrument to remove the swelling.)
Then, when it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then it should be treated with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of the water descending from the eye (referring to an eye infection or discharge).
We have already discussed the types of eye discharge in the division and which ones should be perforated, declaring this according to the health (of the patient).
Then you should make the patient sit between your hands on a chair and bind their healthy eye, very tightly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of their eye, if the eye with the discharge is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is the left one; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then place the tip of the almond near the cornea, along the thickness of the iris in the white of the eye, on the side of the smaller lachrymal duct, and then press the almond gently and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate the white of the eye and feel that the almond has reached the empty space (or cavity).
And it should be that the distance of the almond to the pupil is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the edge of the blackness (iris), and that is the cornea of the eye; for you will see with that pupil through the vision of the eye by the clarity of the corneal tunic.
And then place the almond into the place where the water (fluid) is, and then you draw it down one time then another, and then if the water descends immediately, then the patient sees that upon which he focused his vision immediately, and the almond in his eye.
And then rest a little; then if the water has descended, then you remove it another time before the almond is thrown out (or expelled).
And then when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and gently roll it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the water and with that wash the eye inside; and then place on the outside of the eye cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white and bind the eye with it.
But we and those of our time place on that a compress soaked in egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you upon entering the eye because of its hardness—for there are some men whose eyes are very hard—then it is necessary that you take the herb which is named *alberid* (likely a misspelling or dialectal variation of a plant name), of which this is the form:
Then pierce that joint with it, and do not linger with your hand in the hole, but only make a subtle passage for the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And for the completion of your work, let the patient be prepared a darkened, quiet sleeping place where he sleeps on his back, and let all movement be forbidden.
And place him in such a position that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And let the healing be according to its disposition within three days.
And then release him from that dark room and expose him to a gentle view, and show him some things, and then return to the treatment within seven days.
And it is not fitting that you do this at the hour of treatments or immediately after an infusion. Having said that, you must avoid this because the water may lightly, greatly and vehemently,  [cause damage/have an effect - the meaning of "reguardament" is unclear without more context, it may also refer to a specific medical procedure].
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you must open the wound before the seventh day and treat it with something that reduces the abscess.
Then you cover his eyes and place a cloth over his face, by which his vision will be regulated for some days, while he remains in the dark room.
And then let him be moved from that room gradually and converse on pleasant matters.
And know that in the case of a cataract, the pupil is not excused from some vision at times, and then you should be bold about the operation.
And it has come to us from some of those who are in Artach, and some say that they make fire in Ayrac, a perforated place through which water is drawn.
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is novel.
And these are the forms of the types of wound, so that he who is ignorant of them may know them:
And let them be made of bronze or iron properly, and let their ends be made with this triangular subtlety, the sharp end thus, as that is the form.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of raw flesh in the nose.
When various fleshy growths are produced in the nose, some of which are similar to a very small scorpion, or to a polyp fish; and this illness is called polyp because it resembles that fish in appearance. And from it is cancerous flesh,  scaly, of dark color; and from it is that which is lion flesh, not of dark color.
Therefore, in that which is lion flesh, neither malignant nor cancerous, you should make the patient sit between your hands and place him in the sun, and open his nostrils, and apply your unguent to those growths. Then extract that to the outside parts, and then cut away what you have grasped of those with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh is removed; if any part remains from which excision is possible, then scrape it with one of the fine instruments easily until nothing remains of it. But if it bleeds profusely or a hot, malignant abscess forms, then apply what is necessary; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you should incise and cauterize it until the bleeding is stopped and all the cavities are removed.
Then let there be poured into the nose near the incision, strong wine and water or wine. But if the nose is open and no moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that it is healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is rotten flesh in the upper bones of the nose to which excision has not reached.
Therefore, it is necessary to introduce a linen thread into it, to which some thickness is attached, and tie many knots on it, and place between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and instruct the patient and introduce one end of the thread into the nostrils with a rod or with another means which is possible for him.
And when this is done, let him draw in its odor until it reaches the nasal canals, and it will be expelled through the throat, for many times children do similar things in schools, and it is an easy thing for anyone who wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end coming out of the nose and the other remaining in the nose, and then administer a sawdust of meat with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
And then remove the thread, and apply to the nose near the extraction of the blood a poultice, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that is rotten in the flesh.
And finally, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for several days until it is healed.
But if you need a more intense cure, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument, a head-purger, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or brass, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small bowl, and the spout similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the head-purging instrument should be flat and open;  it should have a handle at the back, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or whatever you want of the juices and suctible substances in it.
Chapter 25.
Of boils appearing in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a boil is born in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in some days, until it is very easily seen.
And for that it is necessary that you incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. And then place on the spot either a cautery or a burning medicine which will stay in place.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened,  hard, of a dark color and of little feeling; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess; for many times he who incises this abscess suffers great harm from it, from the one who had it.
And if the abscess is of a light touch and is not of a dark color, and times when incision is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and cure the place with that which exudes and compresses until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suturing of the nose and of the lips and of the ear when the continuity is broken by a wound or the like.
Know that when there occurs a solution of continuity in one of these cartilages, you will have little success in that operation except in certain men.
Therefore it is necessary, when such a thing happens to someone, that you consider if it is a fresh wound with its blood, and then you join the two lips of the wound with suture, and then cure it until it is healed.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then it is necessary that you scrape each part of their apparent skin until blood comes out. And then join the two lips with suture and tighten them, and over them sprinkle dragon's blood and finely ground incense, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or of some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave it bound for two or three days, and then unbind it and change that plaster and leave it until the stitches by themselves are removed, and then cure with ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of the suture is that you join the solution of continuity either with needles as we have recounted in the suture of the abdomen, or join the solution of continuity with thread as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things that come into the two lips in the inner parts.
Many times there happen to many men in the inner parts of their lips certain hard abscesses resembling the fat of a pig, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn back the lips and split each of those hardnesses on all sides, and then, having made the excision, fill the place with crushed sage until the blood is restrained, and then it should be washed with sharp wine and the place treated with something styptic, until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh grown in the gums.
Very often there grows upon the gum an excrescence of flesh which the ancients called an albulus; then it is necessary that you suspend that with a hook or hold it with forceps and incise it below its root, and allow the pus or blood to flow.
And then place on the area crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for after cauterization it will not return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the treatment of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums, rough, decayed, and sometimes blackened crusts are attached, and they become yellow or green, so that the gum is affected by this corruption and the teeth become decayed.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and rest his head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those crusts and similar things appear, like sand, until nothing remains of them.  And do this to remove the blackness, yellowness, and other things until they are cleansed.
If this, which is the beginning of the treatment, has been achieved, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the treatment on those teeth on the following day, and the second, and the third, until you achieve the desired result.
And know that the teeth require many scrapers of different shapes and forms, according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the scraper with which the tooth is scraped from the inside is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other shapes, and these shapes of scrapers should be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
That you cure the tooth of its pain with all diligence and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that surpasses its sight when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance, so that there is an excuse in its extraction.
But if the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is the aching one, for many times the pain deceives the patient and he thinks it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and aching tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operations of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which tooth is aching, and you know it well, then you should scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the tooth and gum on all sides, and then loosen it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, little by little at first, until it is completely loosened.
And then apply strong, large forceps for a good grip or firm hold, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold it well and it does not move, and then extract the tooth straight, in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not dislodged, then take one of those instruments, and place it under it on all sides with ease, and force its removal in the same way as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or eroded, then you should fill that crack with cloth and tighten it with the end of a thin rod so that it is not broken at the time you tighten the forceps on it.
And you should open carefully with scarification around all parts of the gum and be very careful not to break it and leave a piece behind, which would return to cause the patient pain worse than the first.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity in extraction, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses in men which are more difficult to cure, and their roots remain, all or some of them, or that they extract it with some bones from the jaw, as we have witnessed many times.
And afterwards, after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with hot wine or sour wine with salt.
And if bleeding ensues from this, as often happens, then crush some quantity of alum and sprinkle it on the wound, and if the alum is not effective, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and fine tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be of long, narrow, and thick handles, so that they do not bend when you work with them.
And let them be, as it were, of thick handles, so that when you squeeze with them they are not bent, [but rather] of short extremities.
And let them be made of Indian iron, skillfully made with straight ends, and at the ends of these let there be teeth, of which some interlock with others and tighten by strong constriction, and sometimes the ends of these are shaped like the beak of a bird called a *ciconia*, that is, a stork, and are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the extraction of teeth and the removal of fractured jawbones.
When you must extract a tooth which, being broken, remains partially embedded, then you should place a poultice on the area for a day or two until the area is softened; and then insert forceps, the ends of which resemble the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork, the shape of forceps:
Their ends should be made inwardly like a file or rasp.
And if it does not yield to you in this way with these forceps or pincers, then you should dig down to the root and expose all the flesh and insert the instrument which is called an *atali*, that is, a small lever, of which the shape is this:
And let it be of short, somewhat thick extremity, and let it not be bent, lest it be broken.
Therefore, if by this the root is extracted, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is of a triangular extremity, in which there should be some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of a more subtle extremity.
And you are now aided with this instrument having two prongs, of which this is the form, approximately the following: and with other instruments which precede in the extraction of teeth; for the work is easily done with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, and this is its form:
of the triangular, curved or short extremity, which should have some thickness, and should not be bent so that it is not broken.
And know also that the instruments for removing teeth are similar to the other instruments.
But the wise and subtle artisan in the art finds for himself, according to what the operation and the various illnesses signify to him; for there are illnesses for which the ancients did not describe nor provide a specific instrument, due to the variety of forms and types.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken, or if it is possible, then it should be treated in its place with what is convenient from the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of the roots of the teeth, and help yourself with a splint of which this is the form:
And let there be some small thickness in it, and let it be tightened or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or move until the bone is completely set; and then restore the area with medicines suitable for this.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural alignment, their shape is flawed, and this is especially true for women, and particularly for venerable old women; therefore you must consider: if a tooth is next to another tooth and closure or alignment is not possible, then extract it; but if it is not connected to another tooth, then you should incise it with an instrument of the form shown here; and it is similar to a boar's tusk and should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp point.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be made in several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other tooth is damaged.
And if the protruding tooth is suitable for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of this shape:
And let the whole thing be of Indian iron and very smooth; and let it have very fine points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days easily, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some rasps.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and hurts the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and is filled so that the tongue is not injured and speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth are damaged by concussion and loosening due to a blow or a fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated it with styptic medicines, and your treatment has not been effective, then so that they do not fall out, they must be fastened with gold wire and not silver, because silver turns green and almost disintegrates after a few days, but gold is never altered.
And let the wire be of such thinness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the method of tying is that you take the wire and pass a double length of it between two healthy teeth, and then you make two turns of the wire between two damaged teeth, or one or many, until the wire reaches a healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving back to the point where you began, and tighten with your hand gently and skillfully until it is firm.
And let your tightening, with which you fasten the wire, be such that it does not slip from the roots of the teeth.
And then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and smooth those ends at each location, and bend them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightly fastened.
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and the manner of the binding of two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, as you see them thus bound:
And when it is done that one or two teeth return to the position of each in their places, and are grown back as we have recounted, and that they remain fixed.
And this is only done by skilled and wise artisans.
And sometimes a certain remedy is prepared from cow bones, and it is made from them in the form of a tooth, and it is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is secured as we have said, and it remains and serves in that place for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the incision of the ligament which occurs in the tongue and prohibits speech.
This ligament, which is found under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the patient's mouth, their head being in your lap; and raise their tongue, and then incise that nerve-like ligament according to its width until the tongue is freed from its constraint.
But if in that [area] there is any hardness or knot, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you should make an incision and divide it across its width until you have cut the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the depth of the flesh and that you cut only the ligament and that a flow of blood does not occur.
And then wash the area of the incision with warm water or with vinegar or with cold water; and then place under the tongue linen cloths; that is to say, a cloth which the patient should keep under their tongue each night so that it does not heal together again.
But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
