Sometimes an abscess similar to a small tumor occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it is quickly enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting in front of the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is tending towards white, and contains moisture, then you place your finger there, and incise it with a fine spatula, and cut it completely; and if blood flows during your operation, then apply crushed vitriol to it until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it is washed with wine and salt; and treat it with the other appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36.  On the extraction of an abscess from the two ears and from that which is located in the throat and other abscesses.
When a goiter develops in the throat, these are glands that have accumulated, which protrude outwards and are called "two ears," which when you treat them with what we have said in the division and it is not healed, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, and with little feeling; then do not touch it with an iron instrument.
And if it is red in color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron because of the flow of blood; rather, let it mature, and then either you will extract it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white in color, soft, and its root is thin, then it is one that should be incised.
And the operation on it is that you should consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already matured completely through rest or if it has diminished in some way; and then make the patient sit before the sun, and their head should be in your lap, and open their mouth, and the servant, taking between his hands, should press down the patient's tongue with a very fine instrument, of which this is the form:
And it should be made of silver or bronze, and be as thin as a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue with that and the abscess is shown to you, and you look at it carefully, then take tweezers and put them into the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can. Furthermore, if you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Furthermore, if you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to forceps, however its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula of which this is the form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner, and immediately after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, let him gargle with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar styptic substances until the flow of blood is restrained, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those two mentioned above, and incise those according to what I have said about the incision of two sides equally.
And I myself once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat. This abscess was dark-colored, almost insensitive, and had already closed off her throat, so that the woman was breathing through a narrow passage. I had already forbidden her to eat or drink water, and she would have died within a day or two if she had not been treated.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had formed two openings from which pus came out.
Then I treated it using a fistula approach, and inserted a probe into one of the openings, and drew it out; a large amount of pus was drawn out.  Then I incised the area corresponding to the other opening, and then I opened her mouth, compressed her tongue, and then I placed the probe into that abscess.
And then I excised a large portion of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was completely freed, and she immediately began to drink water, and then she took food. Then I continued to incise the abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess formed in its place, which I incised until it was completely healed.
Then I burned and cauterized her throat; thus it ceased to be infected. And then I went away and did not know what God would do regarding her after I left.
Chapter 37.
On the incision of a swelling when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula and it is abscessed and elongated, then it is called a perpendicular swelling; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a bubo.
But when it is treated with what we have said in the division, and your treatment is not effective, and you see that the cauterized abscess is still present and is subtle, then you must incise that which is round and aggregated; and if it is of great length, and is bloody, dark in color, or black, and there is no feeling in it, then you must attempt its incision, for in that case there is danger to the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner I have described, in its whiteness and length, you should make the patient sit and press down their tongue with an instrument which conforms to its shape, and then insert the lancet into the uvula and draw it down, and incise it with one of the two instruments which are described in the incision of the tonsils.
And you must not incise it except what is exactly at the natural limit without addition, for if you incise more, you will impair their speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of the tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use ingenuity in cauterizing it without causing fear, and the method of cauterization is not only with sharp water (a strong caustic solution), but you should place the patient's head in your lap, and then press down their tongue with the instrument we have mentioned, and then take the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with a live coal, and apply it neither thickly nor thinly, and with this fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the shape:
Let the end of it be concave, in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on their side so that the saliva or mucus from their mouth runs out because of the caustic medicine, so that nothing of it descends into their throat and harms them.
And then take your hand with the medicine, and press it against the uvula for half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried by the action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine, and insert the spoon with the cotton into a cannula from above so that the cotton adheres to that cannula, and do this as many times as it takes to achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it because it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
And if you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it, and after the cauterization, wipe the area around the uvula with cotton soaked in wine, and clean with it whatever remains around it from the medicine, and wash it with cold water, and treat the outside with an embrocation (a topical ointment or lotion), and the inside with gargles until it is healed.
And this method of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method more subtle than cautery or incision, for it is cured with fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, origanum, rue, dried Arabic gum, chamomile, and similar herbs, and mix all these ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them in sour wine, and boil; and the pot should be well sealed with a suitable lid, and in the middle of the lid there should be a hole, over which a concave copper instrument should be placed in this way; it may be made of silver or copper.
And insert the end [of the instrument] into the patient's mouth so that the vapor can reach the uvula through the cannula, until the uvula is sufficiently fumigated.
And then repeat this several times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the development of an abscess, because it would increase the abscess too much, and you should not do this, but rather you should cauterize the abscess at its start/manifestation.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a cane and attach a chicken egg shell to the end of it, so that the patient's mouth is protected, for the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and that which adheres in the throat and other causes.
Many times something adheres in the throat, whether a fish bone or some other thing.
Then it is necessary that you extract that which is apparent, on which the eye falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument before the light, so that that which is in the throat may be revealed to you.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, you must cause the patient to vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very quickly the thing attached to the throat will be expelled by vomiting; or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sea sponge, tie it firmly with a thread, and then have the patient swallow it, for when it reaches the place where the thorns are, it will extract them and they will be expelled. And also take an eel, tied with thread, and let it be inserted and quickly withdrawn; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly draw it out.
But if it is not expelled by those things which we have said, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be thicker than a small ray, and at its end let there be a concavity which you may easily insert into his throat.
And let him have his head raised, and be careful not to touch his throat bone, so that it may happen to him that he may push the bone or spine with it, or that the physician may do so with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself insert it, because of his knowledge of the location of the attached cause, and push downwards, and pull his hand upwards with the instrument, all this according to the quantity which is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and does not work, then consider placing it on the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed his tongue with the instrument which we have recommended to you.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with gentle, appropriate movements.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a glowing iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this as many times (until the leech is dead and detaches itself from the throat), and the patient should abstain from water all day.
And then take a vial full of cold water, and open his mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not swallow anything from it and stir the water every now and then with your hand, because the leech immediately falls off when it senses the water.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cymene or asafoetida using the instrument which we have recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall out.
And another method of operation in the fumigation of this is that you take a pot in which there are burning coals and the pot is covered with a cover in the middle of which there is a hole, and the end of the instrument is placed in this hole; and then let the fumigation rise; and let the patient put his mouth to the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall off.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and do not drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is removed from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps in appearance, except that it has some curvature: which is placed in the throat, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is hollow, in which there is a roughness of filing, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the sum of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and on their incision.
Abscesses are of many species, of many manners, as you will remember from their description, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is by the species of abscesses themselves; and by what we understand of the humors.
And the second manner is according to the locations in which they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and each of them has its own judgment for operation.
And moreover, of abscesses, there are some which ought not to be perforated except near maturation of the pus in them or completion of their formation.
There are other abscesses which must be opened when they are raw and not matured according to their completion, such as abscesses that are near the joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its being is prolonged until that which is in its circumference is digested, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore there is made a cause of contraction of that member; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured, it will harm the principal member; or it is near the anus, therefore perforate that which is not matured; and it is not fitting that you perforate it raw, of uncompleted digestion, except that which is near the anus, so that its matter may not fester and be elongated to the inner parts of the anus, and a fistula be made, or that it be done at such a time that it may not heal. But it is necessary that you know the time of the perforation of the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when there is a lessening of the pain and a cessation of the fever, and also a diminution of the redness and pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know that the perforation falls in the place lowest in the abscess and from the highest point in its healing.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and away from the tendons, the cords of the nerves, the arteries; and, lastly, in all even places in which there is no reflection; for in those which are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you delay its perforation until its digestion is completed, as we have said; for if you perforate it before that, the course of the pus is prolonged and much filth remains, and quickly its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation, or several incisions, according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which it is necessary that the skin be incised and hollowed out, when it is made like bread and is ripe, as happens in cases of swellings and buboes.
And some of these are incised with a three-angled incision; and others are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And there are those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are made, and similar ones.
But for those which are not like the aforementioned superficial abscesses, you should perforate them with only a superficial or flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should perforate it and do not worry about extracting all the pus at once; having extracted some of it, then dress the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger portion, and do this many times according to the size until it is all evacuated.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in a weakened state, because the vital spirit is often dissolved with the egress of the pus, and the patient dies very quickly, and you open these (abscesses).
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, you should cleanse the wounds and consider whether the wound of the abscess is small or a flat incision; then you apply lint or old linen; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you should place a wick in each opening until they join together.
And if a part of the skin is cut away in the abscess, or you have hollowed it out in the circumference, then you should fill it with old linen or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have removed splinters or roots, without moisture; and bind it tightly until the third day; and then proceed with this and treat it with the ointments necessary until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and sour wine in which you soak a linen cloth, and apply this to the bleeding site many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you should administer the treatment and cure and powders which I have recommended in many places in this our book and division (or section).
But if it is in winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you should soak powders in hot wine and oil, and apply them to the areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, and all this should remain cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and clean it and administer to its treatment what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction I have already spoken of each abscess, in what manner is the way of its treatment, by chapters individually.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which appear in the back of the head.
In the back of the head, small abscesses are found, and these are a kind of nodes which contain tunics (membranes) which are attached to their surfaces; and the species of these are varied.
And some of them are pimple-like, and those contain a humidity similar to slime. And some of them contain a humidity similar to pus, and are similar to them; and some of them are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, since the extraction of these does not cause contrary effects.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is like this, which is called a probe, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then incise it lengthwise with a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin the incision from line B to line G.  Then, when the humidity is evacuated, you should scrape that skin which contained the humidity, and do not leave any of it at all, for often the problem returns if any of it remains.
And then immerse cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in salt water; and fill the wound with this and leave it there until the next day, for with this you corrode all that remains of that membrane.
And then apply the Egyptian ointment to that cotton a second and a third time if necessary, until you are confident that no part of that membrane remains; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you should incise it according to this form: with an incision in the shape of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from every part, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane which contains it.
But if the suppuration opposes you, then do what I told you first.
And similarly proceed with a stony abscess in incision and treatment as we have told you; and the incision on a stony abscess is easier, for it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are in the head in some men, in which there is no moisture at all; the reason is that I incised an abscess in the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a dull, hard, white stone, which could not be crushed, and if anyone were struck with it, it would hurt him.
But in other accidental abscesses in the head other than this, this is not the chapter, such as abscesses that occur in the heads of children and behind the roots of the ears, but the incision of all of these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those in the lowest part of them so that the body is relieved of the pus in the lower parts.
And then treat those with what is suitable for that treatment.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42. Of the incision of scrofulas which occur in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck and in the armpits and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck, and many that are generated among them, and every scrofula of those is within its own tunic, and just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, for of those some are stony, and of those some contain humidity, and of those some are malignant which do not obey cure.
Therefore, in that which you see of good disposition in the touch, of which the color that is manifest is similar to the color of the flesh, and is softened in every part, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, it is necessary that you incise that with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body from the line of G to the line of B, and scrape that from every part, and extend the lips of the flesh with an inch, or with many inches if you have need of those, just as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract that little by little.
And it is most important that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the scalpel not be too sharp, so that it does not injure your hand with the incision, or that it disturbs the patient, and that you incise only what is necessary to incise; for if you should incise a vein or artery and prevent your operation, then place on the wound crushed vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and bind the wound tightly, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess is calmed and the wound is softened and begins to fester. When the blood is separated/cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then investigate with your finger if there is any cause of other small scrophulas; then incise those and cleanse them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, then it is necessary that you do not incise that scrofula at its root. Beforehand, it is necessary that you bind it with strong doubled linen and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then cure it.
And if you incise the whole scrofula, then you must adjust the two lips of the wound and stitch it as soon as there is no remaining superfluity.
But if you see that there is remaining superfluity because of the size of the scrofula, then you must understand how to incise its root, and extract it and administer suture and what is said.
And that one of the scrofulas which contains humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and place the perforation so that it is the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound is already cleansed, then you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the head of the abscess accidentally within the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the abscess, but no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the writing/description of the speech of those (ancients).
But in men having a throat abscess, the extraction of the head (of the abscess) must be avoided, because it is not beneficial, since all the veins and the major ones of the patient are there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth or throat or near the ears, when the illness is not in the windpipe, it is necessary to use the incision of the head (of the abscess) to avoid the loss which is caused by perforation (or suppuration).
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the head of the abscess below three circles of the circles of the abscess, or four, with a small incision in width in the area between two circles, such that the incision is in the skin, not in the cartilage; and this location is suitable for incision because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are far away.
And if the physician is timid, then he should extend the throat membrane with a hook, and then incise the membrane until he reaches the abscess, but he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then incise the skin as we have recommended; and the incision of the windpipe is indicated by the inflammation which comes from it, by what is broken from it, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Then, when the time has arrived in which perforation is feared, bring together the two edges of the wound, and sew it up alone, without the cartilage.
Then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the sum of the discourse which we have recounted, is an exposition that shows that some of these abscesses have already blocked the throat of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and his breath is cut off, and some go to incise the epiglottis so that the patient breathes through the site of the wound for some respiration, and is saved from death.
And for this reason I order the wound to be left open until the malignancy of the disease is consumed; and its malignancy lasts for three days; and then I order the wound to be sewn, and cure it until it is healed.
And what I have seen myself, is that a servant took a knife and put it in her throat and incised with it a part of the windpipe, and I was called to her treatment, and I found her moaning as one moans from being wounded, and I uncovered the wound and found the blood which had issued from the small wound.
Therefore, I was satisfied that she had not incised a vein or artery, and wind was issuing from the wound, and I treated it until it was healed.
And the servant suffered nothing but hoarseness in her voice, and after a few days she returned to better health.
And now I say that in the section of the tonsil there is no fear.
Chapter 44.
Concerning an incision on an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess called throat hernia is a large abscess according to the complexion of the body, and it is very common in women.
And it is of two kinds, for it is either natural or accidental.
And in the natural kind, there is no need for a cure.
And the accidental kind is of two types, of which one resembles fatty nodes, and the other type resembles an abscess which is caused by a knotting of an artery, and in this case there is danger. And one should not presume to use iron (surgery) on it except in the case of a small one. And you should probe and investigate it with your finger, and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you should incise it as you would the nodes, and extract it with whatever contains it from the skin and bursa; and if not, then its entire being should be examined, and then cure the place with what is necessary for healing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the incision of the types of nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already described all their types in the division (of chapters/topics).
And it is necessary that I recount the difference between a node and an abscess, as there is doubt (about the distinction).
Therefore I say that with an abscess there is heat, fever, and vehement pain until the boiling (inflammation) of the superfluity is sedated; and putrefaction is complete, at which point the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that leonine humour which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is made like a vesicle, whether larger or smaller, and there are two types, for they are either fatty and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division (of chapters/topics).
Therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a node, you must express that (humor) and incise it at the beginning with an instrument which is called an intromes, according to how its form will appear in the chapter which is near this one.
And the manner of incision of abscesses and of all nodes is that you take this instrument and introduce it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will revolve your fingers with this little by little until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the membrane.
And then extend your hand according to the size of the abscess, and then extract the intromes and consider what has come out in its track.
And if the flowing humidity, of whatever color it may be, is expelled, then you incise it with a superficial section as I have said in the case of other abscesses.
And if no humidity emerges from the track of the intromes, then know that it is fat (or thick/dense).
And dry it out with an incision in the shape of a cross, according to this form: Thus as I have shown you, and suspend it with hooks, and easily peel off the skin from all sides, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract that pus with the node (knot or swelling).
But if it is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract all the pus, and this happens many times, and then you extract it gradually until nothing remains.
Because if anything remains, little or much, the swelling will return again.
And if you see that a small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and tighten the wound, and place over it a hot poultice, and treat it with another cure until it is healed.
And if it is a large swelling, then you sew the two lips of it and treat it with that which causes it to form new flesh (granulation).
But if a pulsating vein is present, or a non-pulsating one, then you consider it and fill the place with crushed vitriol, and let it stay there for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood subsides; and then return to incise that which remains of the swelling.

Chapter 47.
Concerning the care of men's breasts which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are inflamed due to the habit of sleeping on them; and then the breasts are inflamed, and they resemble women's breasts, and become purulent, ulcerated.
And for one who has this, it is necessary to incise it with a lunate incision according to this shape, from line B to line G, and then scrape away all the thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medicine, and then approximate the two lips of the wound with suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the breast droops downwards and is softened by its size, as happens in women, then let it be split in two halves, its upper parts, with two incisions resembling a lunar shape, each contained within the other by the end of each, so that the larger line contains the smaller according to this shape, from line B and line G. And then scrape the skin which is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said of the suture and the powders, that which is necessary in the healing until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what must be incised because of the patient's unease, and because of blood flow, then you must fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes what is superfluous fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of the perforation of abscesses that appear below the nipple.
These abscesses that appear below the nipple are a type of scrofula, hard, which contain a sac-like skin.
And some of them contain humidity; and those that contain humidity must be incised with a lunar incision according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all that is in the abscess of pus is evacuated, then you fill it with old cotton and leave it there until the next day.
And then the cotton should be removed and it should be treated with a suitable ointment for this, until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the type of nodes and scrofula, then you incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula similarly.
But if the wound is so extensive that it produces putrefaction, then administer cautery to it as we have previously described.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from archery or from a vein.
When an artery is broken, and the skin over it is consolidated, then often an abscess occurs. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are from an artery or vein: for when the abscess is from an artery, it is long and attached to the flesh of the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel in it a kind of grating or creaking sound (stridor).
And that which is from a vein is round on the surface of the body.
And incision of this abscess is an error, especially in that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large, therefore its treatment with iron (cautery or knife) should be avoided.  And that which is in the extremities, or on the head, should be avoided [treated with caution/alternative methods].
But concerning that which is from them [abscesses] by inflammation of the opening of the artery, make a long incision in the skin; and then widen the incision with hooks and near the artery, and free it from the tunics until it is uncovered.
And then place a needle on it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with double thread in two places—which method I have shown you in the extraction of two arteries which are in the temples.
And then pierce the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood which is there comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer treatment in which powder is applied until the ligaments fall away; and then treat that with ointments suitable for this until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess from a severed vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand as much as you can of the abscess with the skin, and then insert the needle below the place which you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be double thread on it so that you extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with a good ligature, as I have told you in the ligature of a grape [vein] in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that it will pull out the thread, then insert the thread with that needle under all the abscess below the incision of the first needle, and tighten the threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until, when what is inside it has come out, then you incise the excess skin, and leave what is tied there.
And then place on it a powder which you have submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which happens because of a twisted nerve.
Just as abscesses occur in arteries and veins, similarly abscesses occur in the nerves when they are struck or injured by excessive work, and similarly.
And it is formed mostly in the places of the joints or of the heel, or in any place where the joints are injured.
And the abscess is serious, and it resembles the color of the other body parts, and it is in the greatest part of its disposition without them, and when it is strongly constricted, the patient feels a kind of numbness there.
And the abscess is not aggregated in the front of the body, and therefore it is below the rib cage, and it is injured in every part and does not spread to the front or back parts.
Therefore, that which is in the joints should not be incised with iron, for contraction will quickly result.
But that which is on the head or forehead, incise with a spatula. But if the abscess is small, then take it with forceps and excise it from the root.
And if it is large, then you suspend it with a hook, and excise it, and then remove it; and close the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the cheek, which also occurs in the abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies, those things which are called fungals by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and sometimes the lips of these are inverted, and it is of those that is small, and it is of those that is greatly magnified.
And I myself have seen one, and in his belly were two things which were most similar to fungi, and there was no difference between them, white, with a thin root.
And sometimes the lips of these were inverted and split, and humidity flowed from it constantly.
Therefore I incised them and found in one about eighteen ounces, and in the other about six ounces.
And the operation in the incision of them is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the thing is white, humid, of subtle root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and let cauteries be in your presence in the fire, because many times after the section of it much blood is expelled; and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, beware of incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind the thing with that, in the manner which we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease to tighten the lead strongly around the thing, until it is cut off and falls by itself without effort.
But if the thing is of thick root, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the belly.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize that so that it does not return.
And then care for it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to incise that which is of dark color, little feeling, and little suppuration, for that is possibly cancerous.
And I come to the remembrance of cancer regarding this.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the treatment of umbilical hernia.
Umbilical hernia is caused by many things; either by fissures of the peritoneum which is above the belly, and through it the intestine or bowel protrudes as it happens in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as previously stated, or by constricted flatulence within it.
But if it is due to a fissure of the peritoneum and protrusion of the intestine, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the bowel, then its location is as we have recounted, with even greater variability, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and quickly returns; and it is easily felt there, and is greatly increased by the entry of a bath and strenuous work.
But if it is due to humidity, then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it grow.
And if it is due to blood, then the swelling appears with these signs, tending towards blackness.
But if it is due to raw flesh, then the swelling is heavy, large and hard, and is fixed in a certain place.
But if it is due to wind/gas, then the touch of it is soft.
And the operation on it is that you consider then if the swelling of the umbilicus is from arterial blood, or from a vein, or from wind/gas; then it is necessary that you abstain from its treatment, because in that case there is fear and danger, as I have taught you in the chapter in which I discussed swellings from artery and vein.
And if the swelling of the umbilicus is due to the intestine or the membrane, then you must order the patient to hold his breath, and be lying down and stretched out; and then mark with a probe around the whole umbilicus, and then order him to lie between your hands on his back, and then circumscribe with a broad spatula around the umbilicus on the place which you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the swelling upwards with an unguent; and then bind it around with strong thread or with a bandage, and in a proper manner of binding.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling over the ligature and insert your finger into that opening and search for the intestine; and if you have found it and it is held by the ligature, then you extract it gently and push the intestine into the inner parts of the belly.
And if you have found the membrane, then take that with an unguent; and incise its superfluities.
But if it is contrary to your [expectation/finding regarding] artery or vein, then surround that spot, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and put in those two strong threads and insert two needles into the circle which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, which you cause to penetrate; and then tighten the swelling in four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the extended flesh dries and falls off by itself; or incise it when it is thinned and dried, and then cure it with what is appropriate of unguents until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the navel is of raw flesh there or from humidity, then it is necessary that you concavate the swelling as we have said and extract the blood or humidity, which you find there, and then cure it with what is appropriate and consolidate the area.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken, in the division, of the type of cancer and in what manner it is subject to treatment with medicines, and that it is avoided in it, treatment with iron so that it is not ulcerated; and we have spoken of cancer engendered in the manner and caution of its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, as with cancer that is in the breasts or in the groin and in similar members, to extract all of it, and especially when it is small and beginning.
But when it is old and large, one should not apply oneself to it, for I could not cure anyone of it nor have I seen another who has succeeded in this.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you proceed and leave the patient and purge the black bile sometimes, and then bleed him, if a manifest repletion is found.
And then prepare the patient with a preparation in which he is placed for the operation, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then scrape thoroughly from all sides with a good, sharp curette until no part of its roots remain. And let the blood flow, and do not staunch it lightly, and press the area, and wipe away the thick blood completely with your hand or with an instrument, whichever is possible for you among the instruments.
But if you have difficulty with profuse bleeding from an incision of a vein or artery, then cauterize it until the flow of blood has ceased, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a chapter numbering system)
On the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy of water, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites, and iron is not applied to the species of tympanites or the species of anasarca, for that is deadly.
And when you have treated this species of tympanitic dropsy with medicines and your treatment is not effective, then consider this: if the patient already suffers from debility, or has another disease along with this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain and similar things, then beware of treating them with iron, for there is danger in that.
But if you see the patient with exuberant strength, and with no other illness but dropsy alone, and neither a child nor an old person, then the manner of operating on them is that you lift the patient between your hands, and the assistant gently presses their abdomen with their hand, driving the water towards the pubic region, and then take a sharp lancet in this manner:
Let it be two-edged, long, with a sharp point like a lancet; but nevertheless let there be some small shortness in it, so that in the operation with it you do not pierce the intestine, and injure it.
And then consider if the origin of the dropsy is from the intestines, for then you must make your incision three fingers' breadth below the navel, directly above the pubic region.
But if the origin of the dropsy is due to a liver ailment, then your incision should be three fingers' breadth to the left of the navel.
And if its origin is from the spleen, then your incision should be on the right.
And your incision should not be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie, lest the superfluities flow to that vulnerable place.
Then perforate the whole peritoneum with the instrument, and insert it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the peritoneum and the omentum, and you will peel back that (omentum); if it is the amount of a fingernail or thereabouts.
Then perforate the omentum until the spatula reaches the empty space, which is the location of the water. Then extract that and insert an instrument of which this is the shape: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or tinned lead, which at its lower part has a small hole, and on its sides there are three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its incised extremity is shaped like a feather, like this:
And at its uppermost extremity is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then that (ring) immediately settles on the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in the right amount, for if you evacuate more than is necessary at once, the patient will very quickly die from the dissolution of his animal spirit, or a syncope will occur which is near to death.
But evacuate it according to the amount of its virtue and according to what the patient's condition indicates to you through the virtue of their pulse and their overall state.
Then extract the instrument, and retain the water, the reason being that it is immediately retained by the caulking which seals the hole that is on the cup, which I have described to you that you should perforate in that manner.
Then reapply the instrument the next day if you see the patient tolerating it, and extract a small quantity of water, and do this for several days cautiously, and beware of error so that only a little water remains.
But if you fear for the patient, and leave a large quantity of water, then treat him with warm sand burial, and induce sweating in a bath, and expose him to the sun for a long time; and treat him with drying medicines until he is healed.
And cauterize the stomach, the spleen, and the liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 54.  (Assuming Roman numeral numbering;  LV is 55 in Arabic numerals, but chapters are often numbered consecutively in a running order.)
Of the treatment of children who are born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born from its mother's womb and the opening of its urethra is not perforated (or is a narrow opening or is not in its proper place).
Therefore it is necessary to treat it with perforation as soon as it is born, with a very thin instrument, according to this method:
And then place a thin lead probe into the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when it wants to urinate, it should be stimulated and it will urinate; and then return the probe.
And if you do not place the lead [probe], it is possible that the urine which goes to that place will not allow it to close.
But that [opening] whose hole is narrow should be treated with a lead probe, as we have said, for several days until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its proper place, so it is said, the cause of which is that in such individuals, the opening is located below the tip of the penis, so that they cannot urinate unless they raise the penis with their hand to the upper part; and this is not conducive to procreation because the sperm cannot reach the womb in a straight path, and it is a very prolonged illness.
And the manner of the operation is that the patient should be placed on his back, and then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with a vigorous pull, and incise the tip of the penis at the location of the prepuce with a sharp scalpel, like a feather incision, or that you shave some part, so that the middle of it becomes similar to the head of the penis and so that the opening falls into the middle location as needed.
And be careful in your operation about the flow of blood, for this often happens; and stop it with something that will staunch the blood, and treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Concerning the whitlow which occurs in the prepuce at the head of the penis, and of corruption, blackness, and adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis.
Many times this whitlow occurs on the penis and is a swelling of foul flesh.
And of these, one is malignant and the other is not malignant.
For the non-malignant, you should suspend it with a fine hook and excise it until you have removed it all; then place on that wound a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment; and afterwards, treat it with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
And if the whitlow is malignant, of a foul color, then you should apply cautery to the incision of it and its root.
But if the whitlow is in the prepuce of the head of the uncircumcised penis, and one part of the whitlow is inside the prepuce and the other part is outside, then you should remove the whitlow which is inside first until it is consolidated; and then you treat the outside one, because when you treat both at the same time with the prepuce, it is not ensured that it will not be injured.
And if you find in the genitals and prepuce blackness and corruption, then you should remove everything that is already blackened and is beginning to corrupt or is already corrupt; and then apply to it a poultice made with honey, which is mixed with finely ground and sifted pomegranate rind flour and crushed and sieved herbs.
And then treat it with another cure until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
because cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, that is, for the flow of blood and for the wound when it is corrupted.
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you should insert a lead cannula into the opening of the prepuce so that the patient can urinate through it.
In the adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis (and this adhesion only occurs in one whose prepuce is healthy, and circumcision has not been performed; and when adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess) it is necessary that you scrape with a blunt spatula until the bond is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate these exquisitely, then you should scrape with a spatula some part of the head of the penis and the prepuce, and this is because the prepuce, when thin, is easily perforated by its slight thinness.
Then separate the prepuce from the head of the penis with a fine linen cloth, which has been moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
Then let it be treated with styptic wine until it is healed.
Chapter 57.
Of the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to those circumcised.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and since it is properly done on infants, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest path leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not discuss circumcision in any of their books, for they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience.  The reason is that I have found most of the artisans and barbers use circumcision and cleansing with a lancet and with forceps; and they use ligature with thread and incision with the fingernail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with scissors, with ligature with thread, than cleansing with the fingernail, because many times the outer skin escapes, for the foreskin has two layers, and the inner layer remains, and is easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with cleansing which is done with a sharp instrument, and is done with the fingers, which cobblers use, the incision of the extremity of the virile member is not secured, because it easily enters the hole.
But in the operation with the fingernail, it quickly becomes rusty and spoils your work. And the foreskin of a child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need of cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with scissors and ligatures with thread, the goodness, experience has shown me, for scissors are of proportional incision, because the sharpness which is above is as the sharpness which is below; and thus as you press your hand, there are two proportional sharpnesses, and it incises according to a comparison at one time; and a compression is made of the thread with a figure containing the foreskin of the penis from every part, with which no error at all occurs.
And the manner of the first operation is that you make the child stand, especially if it is one of those who understands a little, so that you do nothing but tie the thread around its penis only, and leave it until the next day.
And then make him happy in every way possible that he can understand. And then have him lie still between your hands, prepared, and let him not be fidgety; and hide the scissors in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them nor any of the other instruments.
And then put your hand to his penis and blow on the foreskin, and pull it back until the head of the penis is exposed, and then cleanse it of whatever dirt is attached to it.
The shape of the scissors for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let one end be blunt and the other sharp, so that the nail does not come out, and let the length of the two sharpened points be the same as the length of two fingers equally.
But the error in circumcision is that you too quickly remove the entire inner foreskin, or part of it, after the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you stretch that [skin] with your fingernail before the suppuration of the area and that you incise it evenly.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with forceps and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the inflamed penis, then you should leave it until the hot abscess subsides, and gently scrape it, and incise it as you see fit.
And beware of the head of the penis, for that will harm him.
And then treat it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised excessively from the scrotum and reaches the parts above, that will not harm him.
And further, treat it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
On the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction from a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or a tumor, and similar to those things we have described in the methods of treatment in the division.
And if it is not dissolved with what we have said and the cause is severe for the patient, then extraction must be administered with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver and be subtly hollowed like a bird's quill, with the subtlety of a long ray, similar to a palm and a half in length, which has a thin tube at its tip.
And the manner of drawing the urine with this is that you take a doubled cloth and tie wool or cotton at its end with a good knot; and insert the ends of the cloth into the lower part of the syringe, and cut with scissors if any excess wool is present; but insert it into the cannula forcefully. Then lubricate the syringe with butter and egg white.
And make the patient sit on a chair, and moisten his bladder and penis with moist oils, olive oil and rosewater.
Then gently insert the syringe until it reaches the root of the penis; then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; then push the syringe inside that; then push it until it reaches the bladder, and that indicates to the patient that it has reached the void, and this is not done according to this order, but rather the meatus which goes in a tortuous way.
Then gradually extend the cloth with the wool, because the urine will soak the wool.
Then extract it and take care of the urine, and use the syringe, do not cease to do this and use the syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Concerning the quality of bladder irrigation with a catheter and similar instruments suitable for this purpose.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or congealed blood is present in it, or putrefaction is constricted, and you want to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument which is called a catheter, and this is its form:
except that its extremity is blunt, that is, closed. And in it there are three openings: two on one side, like eyes.
At the beginning, there is a concave space, into which that which is to be instilled is poured, and it should be according to the quantity which fills it, without addition, until when something is drawn from the humors with it, it is drawn out, and when it is expelled, with that it is drawn out, and when it is expelled far, as the catheter does with which naphtha is injected in sea battles.
Therefore, when you want to draw humors from the bladder, insert the extremity of the catheter into the humor, and draw with it that which is pushed upwards, because the humor is drawn into the concavity of the catheter.
And then insert the end of the catheter into the penis as we have recounted in the syringe; and then expel the humor with that which you expel, for that humor arrives at the bladder immediately, and the patient feels it.
The form of the subtle catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is its form:
And let it be made of silver or tin, of which the uppermost part should resemble a small feather, and below it a slender tube.
And then take a goat's bladder, and place in it the humor that you want to instill into the bladder, and then tie it between two with a strong double ligature, and heat that humor over the fire.
And then insert the end of the catheter into the penis.
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid with gentle pressure until the patient says that the liquid has reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it; and pierce it all around its circumference; and then insert strong thread into those holes, so that it is tightly bound; and then attach the circle in such a way that it is firmly sealed, so that you can put into it what you want of the liquids, and oils, and waters.
And then you tie that to the instrument and do with it as you have done with the bladder, and express with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have already mentioned in the division the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones formed in the kidneys and stones formed in the bladder, and their symptoms.
And we have discussed the stone which requires a manual operation and incision.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating this with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that stones generated in the bladder mostly affect children; and among its symptoms is that the urine that is expelled from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs his genitals, and often cries, and is restless; and with this,  pus comes out of the anus in many of these cases.
And the healing of children from this is easier until they reach fourteen years; and the cure is difficult in the elderly. But in young people it is moderate because it is between those extremes.
And in the case where the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is the opposite.
Therefore, to achieve a cure, at the beginning we must cleanse the patient with a clyster which extracts all the fecal matter that is in their bowels; for this fecal matter sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone through its examination.
Then the patient should be taken with their feet up and massaged and shaken so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or sometimes leaps from its place.
Then make that small incision between your hands, prepared beforehand, and his hands should be placed under his thighs so that the entire bladder is tilted downwards.
And then search for it and remove all of it from there.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the area, then immediately proceed with an incision upon it.
And if it does not completely subside under your touch, then you should anoint the index finger of your left hand with oil, if the patient is a child, or the middle finger if they are a young adult, and insert it into their rectum. And press on the stone until it stays beneath your finger. And return it little by little to the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards to the place from which you removed it.
And instruct an assistant to press on the bladder with their hand; and instruct another assistant to extend the genitals upwards with their right hand, and with their other hand to support the perineum, which is below the genitals, near the place where the incision is made.
And then insert a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: And incise what is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be above the stone, and your finger be in the anus, and let the incision be transverse, so that the external incision is wide and the internal incision narrow, according to the size that the removal of the stone allows, and no larger, because your finger, which is in the anus, will easily compress the stone against the incision, and it will be expelled without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and jagged, which makes their extraction difficult because of this.
And those that resemble an acorn, and are round, are easily extracted.
Therefore, if it is one that is angular and jagged, adjust it in the fissure (or incision).
And if it is not expelled in this way, then you must push on it, or press on it with a suitable instrument, the end of which is like a file with which you press against the stone, and do not let it escape; or introduce underneath it a thin instrument with a curved end.
But if you cannot [remove it] in this way, then you enlarge the opening a little, and if you see a flow of blood, staunch it with crushed vitriol.
But if the stone is more than one, then initially you apply your hand to the opening of the bladder, and then incise over it; then push it a little; and similarly do if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is unwise to incise it with a large incision, for this causes one of two things to happen to the patient: either death, or a continuous urinary fistula, because the area is not completely consolidated (healed).
But administer its expulsion until it is expelled, or work on its fracture with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Then, when you have completed your operation, fill the wound with aloe shavings and dragon's blood, and bind it tightly. And place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be calmed.
Then lay the patient supine on their back, and do not unbind the bandage until the third day.
And when you have unbound it, bathe the area with water and much oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and diabazilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound there occurs a hot, burnt, and corrosive abscess, and of such appearance as if blood were frozen in the bladder, and because of it urine is obstructed (and this is known by the passage of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains it provokes corruption of the bladder and putrefaction thereof.
Then wash the wound with sharp wine, water, and salt, and with all appropriate remedies, with whatever is suitable for its healing until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the entire course of treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is lodged there, and the passage of urine is obstructed, then treat it with what I have recommended before you resort to incision, for many times because of this treatment incision has been necessary; and I have already proven this, and it is that you take a heated iron perforator, of which this is the form:
of triangular extremity, sharp, set in wood.
Then take a thread, and tie the penis below the stone with it so that it does not return to the bladder.
And then insert the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the perforator reaches the stone. And turn the perforator with your hand on that stone little by little, and you will enlarge the perforation until you make it penetrate from the other side; for the urine will immediately be released.
And then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outside of the penis, because that is broken, and is expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this treatment is not suitable for you for any reason that prohibits it, then tie one thread below the stone, and another thread above the stone. And then incise upon the stone in that penis between the two ligatures. And then extract it. And then untie the ligatures and clean the congealed blood which is formed in the wound.
And it is not necessary to tie a thread below the stone, except so that it does not return to the bladder; and the other thread above, because when the thread is untied near the extraction of the stone, the stone returns to its place and covers the wound.
And for this reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the thread above, that you elevate the stone to the upper parts, or that it returns during your procedure, and covers the wound as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
Stones are rarely generated in women.
But if a stone is found in one of them, then its cure is difficult, and is forbidden by many methods.  (Or: ...and is forbidden in many ways.)
One reason is that women are very easily made virgins again. (The implication is that surgical intervention would be counterproductive due to the woman's capacity for healing and/or the risk of damaging her virginity.)
And the second is that you will not find a woman, be she chaste or married, who willingly reveals herself to a physician.
The third is that you will not find a woman who knows this art well, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for a stone in women is far from the location of the stone itself, wherefore a deep incision is needed, and in that there is danger.
But if necessity compels you to this, then you must take a female physician who is skilled, and they are rarely found.
But if you are deprived of that, then seek a skillful and subtle physician, or present a matron well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who knows some part of this art.
Therefore, present that woman, and command her to do all that you order, from the initial examination for the stone; the reason is that if she considers whether the woman is a virgin, then she must put her finger in her anus and feel for the stone.
Therefore, if she finds it,  constrict that area below her finger, and then command her to incise there.
But if she is not a virgin, then command the matron to insert her finger into the vagina of the patient and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder, and compress it with a good compression.
Therefore, if she finds it, then she must gradually soften it from the opening of the bladder to the lower parts with the sum of her skill until she reaches the root of the thing.
And then incise it near the junction of the vagina and the root of the thing, wherever it is stopped for her, and center the stone in that part, and her finger should not be removed from the stone, constricting below it.
And the incision should be small at the beginning; and then insert the instrument (or probe) into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then adjust the incision according to the size that she knows the stone will be removed from.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches (or protrusions).
Therefore you should know how many types there are of them so that you may understand what you want to do.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the place and keep it there for an hour until the bleeding stops, and then return to your operation until the stone is removed.
And ensure that you prepare the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you are aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been incised, then you should place powder on the place and bind it tightly with powders and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will quickly die. And then treat the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the blood has subsided after a few days, and the place is dry, you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white scrotum, which is below the root of the genitals containing the testicle, and is called scrotum.
And sometimes it is in the proper tunic which nature has prepared for it in the area of the testicle, so that it seems that there is another testicle; and this is the root of the genitals between the white scrotum which we have mentioned, and this is nothing but a swelling.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it occurs due to a blow inflicted on the genitals.
And this moisture has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or reddish, or black and foul-smelling, or a watery white, and this is the most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white scrotum which we have mentioned, then the abscess is round, inclining slightly to length, like the shape of an egg, and surrounds the testicle, for the moisture contains it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper tunic, then the abscess is round near the testicle and therefore one thinks it is another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the  cord of the genitals and the white scrotum, then it falls below the feeling/sensation.  (Meaning the swelling is lower down and the area is numb.)
And if you wish to know the color of the moisture, then you test the color of the moisture in the abscess with a probe, the form of which is described beforehand; and according to what has come out on the probe, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to its cure with iron [cautery], then it is necessary that we order the patient to be phlebotomized if that is possible for you, and you see his body is full.
And then let him lie supine on his back on something slightly raised, and place many cloths beneath him.
And then sit on his left side, and order the assistant to be on his right side, who should extend his finger to one of the two sides of the cord of the genitals and the part of the navel.
And then take a broad spatula and cut the cord of the genitals in the middle lengthwise almost to the perineum.
And let the incision be made straight and equally distant from the line that divides the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the included white fat.
And scrape that [white fat], and observe, and be careful not to cut it; and your scraping, with which you scrape it partially, should be suspended with the finest and most subtle scraping possible according to the quantity.
And then pierce the water-filled cyst with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the cyst upwards; and do not touch the cord of the genitals within, and incise the cyst in whatever way is possible for you. And incise it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, because if you do not carefully examine its incision, there is no certainty that the water will not return.
But if the egg [testicle] protrudes from its sheath during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the cyst, return it inside, and then join the two lips of the sheath of the genitals with a suture, and then treat it like other wounds until it is healed.
And if you find the testicle corrupted by another illness, then you must tie the vessels which are on the spermatic cord, and remove the testicle, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the fluid is collected in two separate areas, then know that there are two hernias; therefore, incise that area in the same way as you did the other; and if it seems to you that it is a single operation, then you perform it.
And then you wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place on top more wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread this over the genitals and the area of the abdomen; and place on top doubled cloths and powders, and bind these on top with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then two cloths should be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and the ends should be made with which it is bound with cotton or damp wool, and they should be added with a bandage in the waistband of the breeches, and one of the ends should reach from the buttocks to the two thighs, and the second should reach to the groin area, and two ends below the two thighs and two other ends above the two knees, and the whole bandage should be fastened in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and with it you incise the skin of the genitals, and it is ignited as we have recounted until the white cyst containing the water is exposed, and then you take a cautery in this form: and it resembles the Greek letter.
And then pierce the cyst with it, and ignite it until all the moisture is expelled.
And then extend the cyst with forceps, and scrape with forceps, and scrape with a thin, ignited cautery; and incise it as much as possible until you eradicate all of it; and be careful that the fire does not touch the egg (testicle).
And know that this operation is safer and involves less blood loss, and better than operation with incision.
But if an abscess hinders you during the incision or cauterization, or blood flow, then you must cure whatever hinders you, with the appropriate treatment as described.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I have recommended; and it is when you are certain, because of what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should make the patient sit on some raised seat, then perforate the abscess in its lowest part with a wide spatula, and position the perforation according to the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough so that all the water flows out.
Then place a cotton pad over the area and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water drains. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is resistance to the outflow of water at that time, you should perforate it: and this is done only because the white panniculus [layer of fat] in the orifice of the wound offers resistance and prevents the exit of the water; then you should place in the wound a pump or instrument with which water is extracted from dropsies, the form of which has been described previously; or apply gentle pressure to the wound opening.
And know that if the water returns and is accumulated again within six months or thereabouts; and when it is accumulated, then it should be perforated in the same manner, for the patient will thus pass his days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
