Know that in the incision of this hernia there is a fear of loss leading to death in most cases; therefore, in my opinion, its removal and being saved by that is better.
And I am recalling the operation on that and the types of abscesses.
Therefore, I say that abscesses arise in the genitals which are composed of these, and this is caused by many things: either by an acute, acrid superfluity, which is poured into the genitals, or by percussion.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly is a liquefied abscess, of which the color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by the knotting of the arteries, and by the inflammation of those as it is already perceived by the memory of it, or by inflammation of the veins.
But that which is from inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we compress it with our fingers; and it is not fitting that it be touched.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any cause of compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that an incision is made on this type of swelling and on the other flesh; and it is that you incise the skin of the genitals, and then extend the testicle upwards, and extract it from the white tunic, and free the suspensory ligament, and tie it, and incise the suspensory ligament after you free it from every part of the testicle.
Therefore, if the testicle is already consolidated with those inflamed tissues, then it is necessary that you extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between any part of the tunic, or between that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the place of the adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you incise all that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Then, when your operation is complete, fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of a hernia that is of the scrotum (or a scrotal hernia).
A scrotal abscess is an inflammation enveloped by some covering similar to a sac, with softening of the testicles, and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, fear is caused by the recollection of what preceded it.  (Alternatively:  And in treating this illness, one must remember what preceded it, for it causes fear.)
However, we must describe the operation in the same way as the ancients did.
And I say that you must make the patient sit on a raised seat; and then gently pull the suspensory ligament of the testicles downwards; and then hold the scrotum with your fingers, along with the veins that are close to the penis; and let another assistant hold and powerfully extend them.
And then incise with a sharp, broad scalpel, with a transverse incision, directly over the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
And then flay the entire area in the manner we have described for the extraction of arrows that are lodged in the temples.
And then insert a double-threaded needle into them, and tie it at the first point where you made the incision and tie the other end at the back of them (the vessels). And then incise them in the middle with a straight incision along the length of the body, and extract that which is gathered from the fetid and corrupt humors.
And then treat the wound with the same treatment as other wounds in which you wish healing to be engendered, so that the vessels which nourish one of the two genitals may fall (heal), for that is not harmful.
But if you have cut all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed, or that the testicles be tied off with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they be destroyed, or that they be tied off so that there is no further growth there.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen on the side of the inguinal rings of the abdominal cavity, causing the intestines to protrude from that rupture to some extent.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these two types occur for many reasons:  a blow, a jump, a shout, the lifting of something heavy, or similar causes.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, is that it happens gradually over a long time, and not suddenly, and it is an inguinal swelling near the groin because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, is that at the beginning it causes sudden, intense pain; and there is a visible swelling below the groin, close to the apparent surface, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and its body in the areas outside the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" is likely a variant spelling or a word from a now-obsolete dialect.)
And sometimes it is caused by flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and there it is retained, and this causes great suffering for the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of this type of illness, iron is dangerous, therefore its use should be avoided.
And the manner of operating on it is that you command the patient to return the intestine with his hand to its cavity if it yields to reduction; then lean over his hip between your hands and raise his legs.
And then stretch the scrotum which is the sac of the testicles upwards, and incise the scrotum lengthwise; then insert into the two lips of the incision hooks, as you would need to open the incision with them.
And if, according to the amount possible for you,  the egg (testicle) is expelled from that incision.
And then scrape away the skin that is under the scrotum, until the hard white tunic of each part is exposed.
Then you insert your finger into what is the testicle, into what is between the white tunic, which is under the tunica vaginalis of the testicle, and the second tunic, and dissolve with it the adhesion that is in the back part of the testicle.
And then gently pull the scrotum upwards with your hand, and with this stretch the tunica albuginea upwards with your left hand, and elevate the testicle with the tunic to the edge of the incision; and instruct the assistant to hold the testicle upwards, and you dissolve the adhesions behind it completely.
And check with your fingers that there is no bowel enveloped in the hard white tunic. But if you find any such thing, then you gently push it back into the abdomen.
And then incise in the area under the scrotum, through an incision from which blood and pus will flow, as we have recounted in what we have said.
Then apply wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have mentioned (and sometimes the white tunic is cauterized, which we said above to stop the flow of blood, because this often happens) and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed with warm water, and keep it there until it falls off as we have said.
Then treat the wound with the other wound treatments until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Of a ventose hernia.  (Or: Concerning a hernia with wind/gas.)
In this hernia, I have not seen anyone who has been bold enough to treat it with iron. (Or: In this type of hernia, I have never seen anyone dare to treat it with cautery/iron.)
And the first [doctors] said that it is done as we have recounted in the hernia that is with wine; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on that [hernia] with ease; and then dry out the middle area around the abscess with that which opens it, until the vessels collapse.
And cure the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the rupture that is made in the inguinal region (groin).
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and the location swells.
And it does not allow any cause of the intestines to the genitals; and if it does, it is little; and it returns in all hours.  (This likely means that the hernia doesn't significantly involve the genitals, and even if it does, it's a small amount and frequently returns to the abdominal cavity).
But if the time with this is prolonged, a fixed fissure is made in the inguinal canal until the intestine descends into the inguinal canal; and this happens because of the extension of the inguinal canal which is in the groin as we have said, which is caused because the canal is stretched, and then it is softened and the place is suppurated and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery, as its narration has preceded. (This means the cure is by cauterization, as previously described).
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner: and it is that the patient lies on his back between your hands, and then dry the location of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with incision across the width according to the amount of three fingers.
And then tighten the white inguinal canal that is under the scrotum until it is uncovered. (This refers to constricting the inguinal canal to better expose the hernia).
And then take the ray (likely referring to a surgical instrument, perhaps a type of cautery) and place it on the prominent part of the inguinal canal, and press it towards the pre-pubic area of the abdomen.
And then sew the two prominent locations on the extremity of the inguinal canal with the ray (instrument), and join one to the other with suture.
And then remove the extremity of the ray (instrument), and do not incise the inguinal canal completely, nor extend the wound, nor anything else like that as I will make you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat it with the treatment for wounds, and as you incise the fibers, cleanse it and restore the wound until it is consolidated; because the inguinal canal is contracted and the prominence of the rupture is not adjusted.
And cauterization is better in this location, because it is closer to health (i.e., a more effective and safer method).
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the swelling of the testicles.
Many times the swelling of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and this is harmful to their health/appearance.
Then it is necessary for the one who is charged with his cure to make the patient lie on his back, and incise all the swelling which is softened above the natural swelling; and then he should join the two lips with a suture.
And if you wish, sew in the beginning the superfluity of the softened swelling with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and then treat it with the treatment for other wounds until it is healed and the stitches fall out.
Chapter 69.
Concerning castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, but because of that it is incumbent upon me to mention it in this book; and its mention is necessary only in two ways, of which one is so that this is within the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it, that he knows the treatment for it when it occurs; and the other way is that we often have need of castration of other animals for our benefit from them, such as of mules, and of goats, and of pigs, and of similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by abrasion, or by incision.
But in that which is done by abrasion, the method of operation is that we cause the animal to be immersed in hot water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. And then take them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear under the touch.
But in castration done by incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and grasp the scrotum of its testicles with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory ligament, and incise over each testicle with a cut until both testicles are exposed.
And then incise them after you have skinned each one. And do not place any substance on the cyphus (presumably a type of wound or sore), except the fine cyphus which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better, which is done by crushing, because strong crushing leaves no substance of the testicles, so that the animal desires no copulation.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two kinds: one is where it appears that there is a space in the scrotum, in that which is between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva in which there is hair.
And sometimes urine flows from that which is in the scrotum.
But in women, there is one type:  it is that the vulva, above the pubic area, is like two testicles of a man, somewhat smaller on the outside part, like a man's prepuce and two testicles.
And the cure is of three kinds: two for men and one for women. And it is necessary that you incise those tightened flesh until the swelling subsides, and then that you cure them with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second type in men, from which urine flows that is in the scrotum, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Concerning swelling and prominent flesh due to causes in women.
A slight swelling is often caused by natural reasons until it is pressed and made less noticeable; and sometimes it is magnified in some women to such an extent that it spreads like in men and interferes with intercourse.
Then you should hold the excess swelling with your hand or with an instrument and incise it.
And do not go too deep in the incision, especially near the root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
And then treat it with wound care until it is healed.
Excess flesh is flesh that grows in the mother's womb until it fills it, and it quickly protrudes externally resembling a tail (and because of this some first called it "tailed disease"); therefore, we must incise it as we incise a swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of gout.
Gout is when the vagina of the woman is not perforated, or is a small opening. And this is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a preceding illness, and that is caused either by poorly formed flesh or by a subtle or thick membrane.
And it is formed either in the front of the mother, or on her sides, or in her upper part, or in her lower part, and it prohibits conception and pregnancy, and it strongly prohibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by observing the womb; if the obstruction is apparent, it is obvious; but if it is not apparent, then you should seek it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin membrane, and is close to the two labia, then you should cut and break it.
And you should place powders on the two labia, and then place your index fingers of both hands together.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then spread the two labia forcefully until that thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil and place it on the area, and keep it moist throughout the day so that the incised area does not heal.
But if the membrane is thick and dense, then you should incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to an abscess, then drain it with a lancet, and incise it.
And have with you medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and lint, mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep it there for some days and administer some dry poultices.
And then it should be treated with the other healing methods until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the mother's birth of other flesh [likely referring to a difficult birth or complications], and then it is necessary to incise in this manner. But if this is due to a cranky abscess, to the cranky abscess which is in the mother, no harm should be done with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids and boils and red swellings that occur in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the orifices of many veins, from which much blood continuously flows; and when they are old hemorrhoids, boils are formed.
And I have already described in the division the types of these and their signs, and I will say thus which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and boils, when they are in the womb and do not appear outwardly, then in those cases no treatment should be done with iron.
But those which are at the orifice of the womb, upon which feeling falls, are those that are cured.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cool place and then extend the boil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them to the roots.
And then sprinkle on them, to stem the flow of blood, one of the incisive powders without biting, such as acacia and similar things.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place her in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of pomegranate leaves and bark, and of walnuts and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this and apply it to her back.
And if the blood is stopped, it is good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice, or in plantain juice, or in mugwort juice with red wine, and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointment until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of a fistula occurring in the mother.
Many kinds of abscesses happen in the mother, such as cancer, and stony abscesses, and ulcerations, and  abscesses, and herpes, and hemorrhoidal fistulas, and fissures, and boils, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses, and their types, and their signs, and their cures in the division (section/chapter).
And it is fitting that I speak in this treatise of a hot abscess which appears in the mother when it is among the abscesses that gather pus, and if you wish to know in what manner the operation is performed with iron (a cautery).
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute with percussion and phlegm and fever, and sometimes it reddens and if you touch it you feel pain. Then do not attempt perforation, and treat it with what assists in its maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms subsides.
And then you must make the woman sit on a stool that has two legs; and let her lean on her elbows, and adjust her feet to the part below her belly, and her thighs should be spread, and place her arms under her knees, and tie each one with a suitable bandage to that.
And then the midwife stands on the right side and provides the instrument with which the orifice of the womb is opened, the form of which will come in those things which are done next.
And it is necessary when you want to administer this instrument that you place with the ray (or beam, referring to a light source) the neck of the woman's womb, and that what is entered into the womb by the instrument is no more than the neck of the womb and it hurts the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the neck of the womb, then you must apply powders to the texture of the neck of the womb so that the entrance of any instrument into the neck of the womb is prevented.
And it is necessary that you place the turning device (or rotating mechanism) that runs in the instrument on the upper side, and that the attendant holds the instrument and turns the device until the orifice of the womb is opened.
And when the incision appears, and is touched by the hand and is smooth and moist, then it is necessary that you dry that area, wiping the head with a broad sponge.
And when the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place on the wound a wick submerged in rose oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the poultice on the section in the neck of the womb.
And place outside the womb and over the vulva clean wool submerged in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then it should be healed in about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this purpose until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed in such a way that it is cleansed with water and honey, and then with water in which the root of lily or of birthwort has been boiled, and then it returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is deeply within the enclosed womb, then it is necessary that you abstain from treating it with iron, taking care of it with what we have said in the division (chapter/section).
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner live embryos should be treated when they are expelled contrary to the natural order/course.
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the appearance of natural childbirth by its signs; and it is that when you see the woman straining in her lower parts and desires to smell the air, and moreover is relieved by what is upon her from the birth, and hastens the exit of the embryo, then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and it is the exit of the child head-first, and the afterbirth with it, either suspended or hanging from the umbilical cord.
Therefore, when you see those signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily, for when they descend head-first, the afterbirth descends with it and is cleansed of those superfluities with good purification.
But that which is otherwise is an unnatural and unfavorable birth, for when the embryo is expelled feet first, and its body is expelled before its head and feet, and one of its hands, or its foot, or its head and hand, or its belly, and quickly it is twisted at the neck, and similar unnatural positions.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and skillful in all these matters, and to guard herself against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what skill is needed in them so that it may be understood and acted upon accordingly.
When the fetus is expelled head-first by natural exertion, and this is difficult for the woman and the birth is difficult, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then have her sit, or have the women support her, and evaporate her vulva with boiled fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small, thin spatula between her labia and with it splits the secundines, or scrapes it with her fingernail until she removes the moisture within. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be given a clyster of mallow and fenugreek mucilage with a syringe.
Then, after the clyster, order her to be compressed and make her sneeze with sternutatories; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then you must reduce them carefully, little by little. But if it does not obey the introduction, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs, and then shake the stool on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she does not fall from the shaking.
And if the hands are not returned and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the remains of the fetus, or tie the body to its hand with a cloth and extract it carefully, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus is presenting feet first, then you must push them upwards, and then carefully turn the fetus, and then grasp it, and then take one foot and gently extend it.
But when it has emerged between the thighs, then you should command her to strain, and make her sneeze with a potion, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then you return the fetus little by little until you place it in its natural position, for then it will be expelled with ease.
And if it is hindered by all that we have recounted, then take marshmallow mucilage and dissolved gum, and pound everything well in a mortar, and carefully anoint the woman's vulva and the lowest part of her abdomen with it. And carefully make her bathe in lukewarm water, up to her hypochondria (the area below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts are softened, then give her a fumigation of myrtle and hold it under her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and carefully make her sneeze and hold her mouth, and lift her up, and press the lower part of her abdomen with gentle pressure, for the fetus will immediately be expelled.
Thus of the fetus lying sideways, holding one of its hands. Be ingenious in reducing her hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her thighs with vehement shaking, and then administer bandages which I have recommended made of mucilage.
And if labor has commenced, then make her sit on a stool, and then work on the reduction of the hand, and grasp the fetus according to its natural position, and make the woman sneeze and command her to bear down until it is expelled.
Thus of the fetus, its hands outstretched over the neck, and its face is towards its mother. It is necessary that the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then turn it little by little, and that she does this with ease.
And when it is grasped, then place on its head that which we have recommended of oil and mucilages.
And then instruct her to cough little by little, and when you have done this, then make her sit on a stool and command her to be inclined to the right side, and then command her to bear down; and make her sneeze with a concoction. The prepared fetus exits on its side.
When the horror of the mother lessens according to this manner, consider, and then you incise the secundines with your fingernail until it is emptied of humidity.
And if the fetus is suspended over its face and neck, then you incise it at the umbilicus, so that it may not be retained and die.
And then gently push the fetus inwards until you place it as needed, and tell the woman to strain a few times, and it will be expelled with ease, if God wills it.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four are born, but this is strange and rarely happens; but there are causes for this in nature, and they do not live.
And they share, even if they are multiple, one placenta, and they are separated by a membrane, distinguishing them between the ligament in the umbilical cord of each of them. And sometimes they are expelled as if it were one fetus by easy natural expulsion, and sometimes they are expelled by difficult expulsion as we have recounted.
And if some of their births are difficult, and it comes to what we have said about unnatural presentations, then you treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and skill, and with gentleness, and is resourceful according to every presentation, as she is prepared by her skill leading to health.
Concerning that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and even ten or more are formed in the mother, and it has been verified by me that one woman aborted and expelled six, and another ten, all formed, which cause was determined by the measurement of the glorious wise men.
And all this must be in the knowledge of the midwife, because it easily seems to her like this, and therefore she opposes it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
On the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medication and it has not been expelled, and you are forced to use a manual operation, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and is not suffering from any of the accidents which threaten her with danger.
Then the woman should be laid on the bed on her back, with her head inclined downwards and her thighs raised.
And the assistant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mother's vulva with warm oils and with a mucilage of mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then let the midwife anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient places for this: then you should consider if the fetus is positioned head-down, and then you should grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or at its clavicle, or in the appropriate places on the ribs, or under the hypochondria.
But if the presentation is breech, then at the buttocks, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary that you hold the umbilical cord with your right hand, and place its curvature in that space which is between the fingers of your left hand, and gently insert your hand and place the umbilical cord in one of those places which we have mentioned, until you reach the empty cavity.
And then place and insert another umbilical cord, or a third if necessary, alongside it, so that the traction is equal, and there is not extension only along its straight line.
Rather, let it be moved in all directions, so that its removal is eased, just as is done in the extraction of a tooth.
And it is necessary in this that the area between them is softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some of her fingers with oil and insert them skillfully and loosen with them that which is retained.
Therefore, if a part of the fetus is expelled, then the umbilical cords should be moved to a higher location; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
But if one hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then extract it until the whole is expelled, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you should do with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or there is constriction during its exit, or there is collected water in its head, then it is necessary to insert between the fingers expectorant and split the head and extract the water, or to uncover it with an instrument which is named "alundat," the form of which will come with the forms of other instruments in the chapter which is near this one.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: then it is necessary to incise it with an algematum, or to grasp it with an alunda as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is out but constricted below the clavicle, then it should be split so that the fluid which is in the feet may be drained.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it impede delivery, then you should incise the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscessed or dropsical, then it is necessary that you perforate it so that the fluid within it may drain.
But if the fetus is positioned on its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction towards the mother's birth canal is easy.
But if it is constricted around the belly and feet, then you must extract it with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and you must incise the belly and feet so that what is inside them may drain out.
But if the other limbs are removed and only the head remains, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's birth canal is open, then she inserts her hand into the womb and searches for the head with it, and pulls it with her fingers towards the mother's birth canal, and then inserts a hook, or two hooks, and pulls it with them.
And if the mother's birth canal is constricted by a hot abscess which harms her, then it should not be disturbed; rather, it is necessary to administer a perfusion of moist, oily substances. And administer plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is lying on its side, then, if possible, it should be rotated; and what we have said about a live fetus should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave no part of the afterbirth inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer what we have said about the treatment of hot abscesses which may occur in the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and soon after that, she became pregnant again, and soon after, the fetus died as well. A long time ago, an abscess occurred in his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied plasters of strong attraction to him, and I extracted a bone from him, and after some days, I extracted another bone; and I was greatly amazed, since there should be no bones in the belly.
And then I considered that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I cleansed the wound, and extracted many bones from him, the woman being in her menses; and she lived in such a manner for a long time, discharging a little pus.
And I did not consider this to be strange, but because in this there is science and help, so that the skillful physician administers care by hand.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
The form of the incision by which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a constrictor in which the membranes are grasped, in which grasping there are two incisions at the two ends of two shafts. However, this incision should be more subtle than a constrictor, and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each shaft be about two fingers, and the thickness of each about one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle space of the two shafts are two fittings of the same wood which are fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the womb so that it is opened with them when you turn the two prescribed incisions.
Another form similar to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, in the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity are two additions like tongs, and the length of these additions is about a palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you wish to open the womb with them, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her legs hanging down, that which is between her thighs open. And then open your hand with the instrument, as you would with scissors, gradually, according to how much you want to open the mouth of the womb until the womb does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
The form of the instrument with which the fetus is expelled:
The method/instrument of manipulation with which a large head is countered/managed.  (The "2" likely indicates a textual annotation or variant reading, not part of the original text.)
And it resembles forceps which have teeth at the end, like tongs, and sometimes it is made long like forceps according to this form, like tongs, which have teeth like saw teeth, with which it is incised and managed. The form of a small instrument/tool.
The form of a hook having a spine/point.
The form of a hook having two spines/points.
The form of a spatula having two ends/extremities with which the head is separated/cut.
The form of two wide spatulas.
All those instruments are exercised, and when the types of those are more multiplied and prepared by the artisans, then it is easier in its operation and more valuable to men because of its quantity/number.
And do not omit any of those instruments already prepared by you, for the necessity of them is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should order the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze forcefully, and hold your hand over her mouth and nose, and if it is expelled by this, it is good.
And if not, then you take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid and put in it herbs, opening the mother's mouth; thus [use]  chamomile, rue,  anise, chamomile, Arabic gum, cassia lignea, and centaury. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that on the fire.
And then place a reed cannula in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's womb.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it is not expelled, then you instruct the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or mallow poultice, and then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it; and when she has found it, then take it and remove it little by little. But if it is attached to the womb of the mother, then she should insert her hand as we have recommended, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it little by little as instructed, so that the mother does not faint from the strong traction. Furthermore, it is necessary that it be turned to the right and left sides easily.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already recommended treatment with sneezing and herbal oil medication.
But if it is not expelled by all the means we have recommended, then be careful not to tire yourself with repeated attempts at extraction; however, it is necessary that what has come out of her into the mother's mouth be tied to the woman's genitals, and then cauterize it with ointment and tetrapharmacon; for that will cause it to fester for a few days and it will be dissolved and expelled.
But when it festers, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then it is necessary that fumigations suitable for this be administered.
But some of the Ancients already tested fumigations of henbane and dried figs.
Method of using the instrument with which a woman is fumigated for the retention of menstrual blood and afterbirth:
Let it be made of glass or brass.
And let the narrow end be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation be placed on the embers of the fire.
And let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed and then repeat with another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are very narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that narrow passage with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil on it; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And if anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then be treated with what we have said about the treatment and the lead [likely referring to lead-based ointments].
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that develop below [presumably, below the surface or in the anal region].
Fistulas that develop in the lowest part below are knotty and thick, which develop near the anus externally or in the space between the parts [likely referring to the space between the buttocks].
And it is one fistula or several.
And when that knot is old and discharges a watery white humidity or subtle pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because what comes out of them is accompanied by flatulence; and sometimes the patient suffers from flatulence, and worms easily come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the space,  into the bladder, and into the urethra; and some penetrate into the hip joint and into the buttock.
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating into the rectum from one that does not penetrate is that you insert your finger into the rectum, and insert a thin probe of brass or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor does any matter, worms, or flatulence emerge from the opening, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then attend to its cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the urethra, then its indication is thus: the urine from it and prohibition of it so that the area is consolidated with medicine.
But if it penetrates into the joint of the hip or into the bone and causes pain in the surrounding area, then its sign is that the probe penetrates there if there is no tortuosity in the bone, and pain in the surrounding area; and that suppuration always occurs from it; and that medication is of no use, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no certain cure, and the cure of those is the labor and vanity of foolish physicians upon which they are fixed.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is hoped for, as I shall relate to you, and this I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back and raises his legs, his thighs being inclined towards his belly, and then insert a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels pain around the anus, then you should insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel with your finger the probe already discovered by itself penetrating, besides what you feel between your finger and that abscess or flesh, then you know for certain that this is penetrating; and then do not work on that, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
However, some would say that it is healed slowly in some men.
And of the medication by which help is hoped for him, it is that you ignite a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been explained before, and insert that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those fistulated, fleshy parts resembling the quill of a bird's feather are cauterized.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen until all the cauterized flesh sloughs away, and then treat it with medicines that generate and consolidate flesh, and if it is healed, it is good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate beyond your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or abscess, and you see the fistula in such a way that it reaches the surface of the skin, then dry the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will see it dry with the probe, and let it be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the end of the probe arrives, and through the probe it is released and removed.
And then cleanse those fistulated fleshy parts which resemble a bird's quill, and let no part of them remain.
But if you see a flow of blood come between you and your work when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and lightest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire achieves two good effects: that is, the cauterization of those affected tissues and the staunching of blood and drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed flax until the place is clean, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate hard flesh.
And these are medicines in which there is stypticity and drying; when then it is restored it is healed and we do not fear a recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the anus and is elongated from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and examine it with that.
And so if you feel the fistula and between you and it there is a veil of fat or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation there except by way of trust and hope.
Which cause is to be treated in one of three ways:  either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that it is possible for you to introduce the poultices and the nearby cure; and do not proceed much with incision lest you cut the muscle containing the anus, and that may happen to the patient thus without his will; and then treat it with what we have said for it will be healed quickly; or to lengthen that veil and clean it after drying it near the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp extremity, and then cleanse those fistulous parts which are in the incision where you can with cauterization; and then strive to make the restoration of the whole wound with incision, and if the orifice above is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the anus, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this way:  when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the anus around the surface of the body with the tail and in the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle for preparing hides in this form:
and insert it into that fistula wrapped in five threads or around them; and then insert a probe with a thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it is penetrating into the margin of the anus further inside, then you extract the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the anus; and extract the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each, and leave it for a day or two, and when you further cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until those parts of flesh are cut which are between the two ends of the thread and that they fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, except that it should be very carefully done, for it is not for you to do this so that you do not cut the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The form of a pointed probe with which the fistula is probed: its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not cut what is not necessary, as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds, for either they are between the buttocks similar to charcoal, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of these there are small and large ones, and the blood flows from them constantly and it is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the months of little humidity, from which flows a citrine liquid or a little blood with continuous flow, and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you carefully grasp and suspend them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, and then incise them to their roots.
But if the hooks cannot hold them because of their humidity and softness, then grasp them with rough cloth or cotton or a pledget of wool, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle on them some hot powder near the incision, so that it acts on them in place of cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatments until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey [the command to] protrude, then stimulate the patient with a small, mildly irritating clyster, and let it be washed with that water which is in the anus and which lightly obeys [the command to] protrude when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and it is that you grasp them with your fingernail, or that you grasp them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, it is necessary to suture it in this manner: take enveloped thread and insert it into a needle and then bring the hemorrhoids together from the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle at the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lowermost part of the needle, and it should be latitudinally [i.e., horizontally]; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong suture, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all [the hemorrhoids], and leave one of them unstitched, so that the superfluous blood may flow from it.
And then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place that on the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointments or with another cure until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then it is necessary that you scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are made moist and the crust above is removed, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are consolidated.
Then remove them with a more vigorous scraping than the first, until much blood flows and they are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a whitlow, that is, a felon, is nothing but a round sore, according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a clove, and it is found throughout the body and especially in the parts below the feet and fingers, and because of them pain occurs from the swelling.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the felon be incised, and it should be held with forceps or a hook, and eradicated from its root; and then treat it.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been previously described in its chapter.
Moreover, a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round eminence on the surface of the body, and often appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is that you spread and incise it, or that you bind it tightly with thread or with a skin so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize that, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Moreover, a mole is a small, hairy or fistulated lump on the surface of the body, often occurring on the face; and when friction encounters it, it is felt like the biting of an ant, and it is found all over the body.
And its cure is that you take a cannula of goose quill or vulture quill, and let it be strong, and place that over the mole so that the circle of the cannula contains the parts of it; and then revolve your hand with the cannula until you incise that which is in the circumference of the mole, and place that into the depth of the flesh, and so eradicate the mole from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a brass or iron cannula according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn out to a thinness, blunt (that is, not perforated), wrapped so that on the said [mole] the apprehension of it may be avoided and its revolution [around the mole] [may be performed smoothly].
And if you wish, cauterize the mole as is previously mentioned in the cauterization of athel in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the form of the instruments that are used to give a clyster.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or of latten (brass), or of molten or hammered brass; and sometimes it is made of those instruments, small and large, according to those to whom they are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose buttocks are fragile or painful, the clysters for them should be small.
The form of a large and a small clyster:
The head of the cannula above, to which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and let there be a distinction where the bag is tied, as you see.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the rectum, should be blunt (that is, not open) very smooth, tending towards subtlety; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of the rays and a little larger.
And let the bag in which the medicines are placed be of animal bladder or sheepskin, and let its length be one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes, as it is rolled; and let the space which is between the holes be the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong, raw flax thread, about ten threads, or thereabouts; and fasten the bag with it; and when the bag is placed in him, bind it tightly at the head of the clyster with two distinct strong constrictions, and then cauterize each hole with it: But the way to cauterize with this is that you fill the bag with the medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then let the patient lie supine on his back, and raise his legs and place a cushion underneath him, on which are placed men or a sheet; and then anoint the end of the clyster with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or the like; and then insert it into the rectum easily, and then the attendant should take the bag and squeeze it with two hands and vigorously until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the clyster and the patient lowers his legs.
And if the medicine forces him thus, hold it as long as he can; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
But if you use this clyster for diseases of diarrhea and its expulsion hurts him, then you repeat the cautery until he is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very thin and soft, of the kind with which infants are clystered.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have already said in the division on the treatment of wounds what was appropriate for the book, and I say thus the treatment of those with hand and with medicines according to what is fitting.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what wounds are made with and according to the place on which the wounds fall.
And the causes for which wounds are often made are thus:  a stone throw, or a cut from a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar things from many causes.
But the wounds that are in the parts of the body are such as a wound which falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the thigh, and similar to those of the other limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or the like, and it has only grazed the scalp and is a large wound, and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then immediately attend to him and perform phlebotomy of the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and draw blood accordingly, according to the strength of the patient, unless some cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has issued from the wound is scant.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and from heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his food be reduced, and let him not approach meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs in him, a pledget soaked in plain rose oil or in wine in which a styptic has been dissolved.
But if there is certainty of a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent, its own blood and, if the air has not changed it, the powder which I shall shortly mention.
And if the air has already changed it, then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is from a sword cut or the like, and its lips are not properly approximated, then approximate them with sutures according to the method of suturing which I shall describe.
But if the wound has already bared the bone and hangs, and there is no retention of it but a slight suspension, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the bone, and order the patient to be fed on foods lacking coarseness: heads and fine cooked meats and similar things, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood may occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you should investigate the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you should cut it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity requires that what we have said is not sufficient, then you cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is light; therefore you should sprinkle this powder on them, for it consolidates them before the air alters them.
And the manner of making the powder is this: take olive oil, one part; dragon's blood, two parts; unslaked lime, three parts; let it all be well ground and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and let it be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with lint and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is thus, unless it becomes inflamed, for the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes only heat does this, when you do not have olive oil and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary to apply it to them and they are fresh and bleeding wounds which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially if it is summertime, then this powder is not suitable; but rather treat them with what we have said, so that you apply some digestive ointments to them, or apply a powder of barley flour with water and honey until they discharge pus; and then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with forceps.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a simple wound of the head.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are cut, then if it is a nerve, there is no other remedy but to avoid treating it with anything that causes strong astringency, such as vitriol and similar things, because it is harmful to the nerves and contracts them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold and its continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is cured with light medicines, such as washing with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or plain oil, and then cleaned with fresh water and other minor remedies in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or draw the edges together, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep, or if there is pus in the lowest part of it, then perforate it at the lowest point.
But if an artery is incised in the wound and there is a flow of blood, then separate or dry it, or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity requires it.
But if the vertebrae of the neck are already incised, or some of them, and the veins of the neck are saved, then you adjust the edges of the wound with sutures on the neck cane and do not touch it; first level it and return it to its natural shape, and then tighten it with a decent bandage, and leave it for some days; and then treat it with ointments which have styptic and healing properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has caused damage to the bone, then cauterize it, and then if there are bone fragments in it, extract all those that you can; and what you are not able to remove, leave it until the wound suppurates, and is separated and the damage is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle on it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that which will be recalled in its chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if there is a venting of air from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is fresh with its blood, then you should put powder on it in the first hour, and do not bind it tightly so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but put attractive ointment on it, and unbind it twice a day.
And if you do not have ointment, then place old linen on the opening of the wound so that it draws out the humidity; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that what is contained within flows out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and it does not cause the patient spasms, or tremor of the heart, or constriction of the throat, and you see the other good dispositions of him, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with cauldrons [likely a misspelling or archaic term for a type of poultice or dressing] and with other treatment until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore treat that according to its chapter [i.e., according to the instructions in another section of the text].
And if the wound is from an incision of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it with what is preceded by suture, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has caused damage to the bone, and pieces have been detached from it, then you must clean the wound and attempt to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound is cleaned, so that their extraction is facilitated.
And for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the method of treatment is the same as what we have described.
Of the wounds of the abdomen.
The illness which happens in the abdomen is either great, or small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is treated differently, and it is such that its reduction is more difficult, if you do not take care of its replacement immediately, because it opens and swells, making its reintroduction difficult.
Therefore, the best [type of] rupture is moderate, because in that case the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two previously mentioned types.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways, but two sutures are common and suitable for suturing other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures are peculiar [to the abdomen],  communicating with other wounds, but nevertheless they are more proper for the suturing of the abdomen.
And I recount to you each one with explanation and clarification.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has emerged through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or because the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is so, then it is necessary that you warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or that you boil in water  squill, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with it until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes this is done with a liquid which has astringent properties, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And then, when you see the inflammation to be resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marsh-mallow have been boiled, for by this the entry of it is eased with little effort.
But if the return of it is hindered despite this treatment, then incise the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is appropriate that there is no incision, and there is no need to worry about drying due to necessity and debility.
Concerning the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part not-sharp, and the extremity is thin, and not with the thinness of a spatula, rather it should be slightly blunt.
And therefore, when the wound is widened and the intestine protrudes, then its reduction should follow its natural form, into its proper place, if possible in this way, for it is better.
But when the rupture is large, and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you should make the patient lie on his back, and raise his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you should raise his head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the abdomen, then always direct your attention and consideration to ensuring that the side where the wound is located is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant gently place his hands between yours, who will hold the entire rupture with his hand and adjust its lips, and then cover that from that, so that some other cause of the suture is nearby.
And this is the common method of suturing: one in which you take the needle, or a number of needles according to the size of the wound; and then, leaving the extremity of the rupture of the annular finger, and tighten one needle, so that you do not put thread in the two ends of the suture, with two ends of the thread that is under the suture of the inner parts, until you make it penetrate that part; and you have now added two ends of the suture and two ends of the thread, and this makes four orders (or layers).
Then tighten with double thread in the circuit of the needle, and sometimes from two sides together, and then bring together the two lips and the wound by decent approximation; and then leave the thickness of a finger and insert another needle into that, and then tie that with thread as you did with the first needle; and do not cease doing this if necessary with the needles until you have completed the entire closing of the wound.
And let the needles be medium between thickness and thinness, for thin needles very easily cut the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the suture, and therefore it is necessary that they be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be inserted too deeply into the two ends of the suture, so that the flesh is not lightly incised and the wound does not open before its consolidation.
And let the sutures not be prolonged, lest the wound be hindered from consolidation.
Then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not harm the patient during their sleep; and you shall place over the suture finely powdered material from every part, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave those until you know that the wound is already consolidated.
And this type of suture with needles in what manner is most convenient in small wounds, because in the suture of those, one needle or two or around those will suffice.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you stitch the four ends, of the wound and two ends of the lip, at once, with a needle around which thread is wrapped equally in fineness and thickness.
And then, when with the needle you have penetrated these four ends, return the needle to the same part from which you began, so that the drawn thread falls from the highest part to the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth suture with which cloths are tightened.
And place between each suture the amount of the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture men know.  (or: And this suture is known to men.)
And with such a suture I sewed up the wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than a palm, and two palms of intestine had come out around the middle of the wound, and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he is healed; and after that he lived for many years, and is preserved in his faculties (or abilities).
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle, because I did not cure him with ointment, for it was in a place where ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I placed old linen on the wound twice a day and washed it frequently with honey water until it was healed.
But I will describe two kinds of sutures proper according to the account of Paraula (or Paulus; possibly referring to a medical authority).
And it is that you begin with the suture of the co-dena (likely a type of stitch or suture; the word is unclear), and insert the needle from the outside part to the inside part.
And then, as the needle penetrates the co-dena and that same laceration, according to the straightness and length of the whole body (or wound), leave the end of the thread in which you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate at the other end from the inside part to the outside part at the other end of the wound.
And then, when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again at that same end of the wound from the outside part to the inside part; and leave the end of the thread that is at this point, and make the needle penetrate at the other end from the inside part to the outside part.
And make it penetrate with that penetration with which you make it penetrate into the thread at the end of the wound which is on his side until all of it penetrates.
And then begin from that same side and sew that with the end of the thread on the other side, and extract the needle from the co-dena which it penetrated, and then return it into that co-dena and sew the end of the thread that is on the other side with this end of the wound; and extract it from the co-dena which is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this one time then another time until you sew all that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a leather-worker's stitch, with which one sews hides, in such a way that you sew the thread one time from the side of the end of the co-dena, and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.
But the second type of suture, which you properly call *Gualia*, and this is spoken of by him and recounted by him, is that you sew it according to the similarity with which some medicant sews it, so that you join each part to its corresponding part conveniently by nature; and apply the end of one side and the end of the wound to the other end of the side, and this is done as I have recounted to you.
So that you put the needle into the end of the wound near you on the outside part, and make it penetrate to the inside part in that sole place, and leave two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside in the two ends of the thread.
And then turn each of them, and make each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part in two other ends of the wound that are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common, light suture, and it is the suture of which I previously made mention.
This is the discourse of Gualia.
And moreover, some say, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is, that you insert into each of them a thread, and begin with the insertion of the needle from the side nearest to you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew hides similarly.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than in other parts of the belly.
But the judgment of the medication of a plague is like the judgment of other plagues.
The reason is that when you treat a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine and sew it and dress it properly, then apply an incarnative powder to it.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then apply some ointments which are at your disposal until they form a hard scab and the threads fall out and are consolidated, such as ciphac and myrrh; and then treat it as you would treat other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have those medicines at hand, then apply immediately, as soon as it begins to discharge pus, old lint, and change that twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, because you will not need other medication in many instances when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will spread infection to the principal limbs, then you should submerge lion's wool in good quality steeped oil, or in rose oil, and place this in the area around the armpits and under the arms.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in their bowels, because this often happens, then cauterize it with dark, tepid, styptic wine, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and made a penetrating exit into its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is of difficult healing because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a wound that falls upon it at all; and this is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of the magnitude of those that are in it, and because of the subtlety of the body of that, and because of the proximity of that to the nature of the nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is the web [likely referring to a membrane or tissue], and you obtain that recently, reduce it according to your skill with which you reduce the intestines similarly.
But if it has passed into the space and has already become green or black, then bind it with gall over the place that is blackened by it, so that a flow of blood may come, for in the web are veins and arteries.
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and place two ends of the gall hanging from the part below the wound outside it, so that the attraction of it towards the falling of the web and the emission of the arm of the wound may be relieved for you.
A recollection/reminder of wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, because of this, for I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with a lance thrust and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was ancient and had become a fistula, and from it issued gesticulation and flatulence.
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his healing, but I continued to refine the treatment until he was healed and the place was consolidated (healed completely).
And I, since I saw the place already consolidated, feared for the patient that an accident of illness might befall him in his belly; and no such ill accident happened, and he remained in his usual good condition eating and drinking, and then he entered a bath and exercised his limbs.
And some experienced people say that when a wound occurs in the intestine, and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner:  you take ants with large heads; then bring together the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants, with its mouth open, over the two lips of the wound; then, when it bites and clenches its mouth, cut off its head, because it adheres and does not come undone.
Then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease doing this with ants, one next to the other, according to the size of the entire wound.
Then sew up that area and close the wound; and let those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm will come to the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be tied with fine thread which is made from the intestine of an animal, since it is easier to thread; and it is that you take the end of these intestinal threads, and it is well cleaned, and then tie its end with a thread, wrapped, and then insert that threaded needle and sew the intestine with it, and then reduce it into the rupture.
And this method of suturing with ants is only a matter of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, then it is only a reduction, and not a complete healing of it.
Concerning the treatment of a fistula and its formation (or: Concerning the healing of a fistula).
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus continually and is not excised, then, in whatever limb it may be, it is universally called a fistula, and we call it *azukem*. (Note: "azukem" seems to be a word specific to this text, possibly a variant or local term for fistula).
And a fistula, truly speaking, is a hardened, white, knotted swelling, without pain, and which has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and for this reason it is called a gnarled ulcer, that is, claw-like.
And in some men it is moist, discharging pus; and very quickly the moisture is excised (or, removed) in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes it is thin, and sometimes it is coarse, and sometimes it is subtle (or fine).
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs, and their cure with medicines, in the division (of the treatise).
And it is fitting that I should make known to you the treatment of fistula, which is done by way of operation with the hand.
