And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body; for there are fistulas which reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the joints composed by composition, such as those of the hand and foot; and those which damage the proximity of the principal member and similar things.
Therefore, that one of these fistulas which is according to this manner is among the illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive a cure, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
And if it is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to arrive at knowledge of it, then take a probe of brass or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, and search for it with that.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily bent around the tortuosity.
And if the fistula has very horrible twists and turns, it is not possible for you to probe it.
Then, inject a clyster into the opening, for the moisture with which you inject it will go through the other openings and clean them out.
And then inquire carefully, in whatever way is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves here, and if the depth of the fistula is distant or near, or if it is a fistula which has many openings, and stay on this entirely with the sum of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your compression with the hand on the place, and similar signs and accidental causes to the abscess.
And when you have considered all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the treatment with confidence: that is, you consider then if it is a fistula near the surface or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you.
Then you dry the fistula as was said before in the description of it, and remove what is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and that which has grown in the opening of flesh or skin and similar things, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it in the front as much as is possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; and then administer cauteries wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce them into the depth of the fistula, which you have grasped with an instrument (iron).
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you should investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not prohibit any cause from a vein or nerve or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that will cause it to grow new flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not reached the complete eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again and meticulously perform the scraping and cleansing with your utmost strength, and then restore it.
And so if it is healed, good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which has not been reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to remove it, then you remove it with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then meticulously remove all of them, and do not leave any behind with your best effort.
But if some of the moist tissues impede you, and there is no fear or mistake in cutting into them, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are present, and free the bones.
But if you are not ready to remove the bones at this time, then apply something to the wound that will rot the wound, and leave it until it has reached the surrounding bones, flesh, and ligaments, and keep the wound from closing, and keep the incision that you made during your operation constricted.
Furthermore, if you are hesitant, place on it a cloth soaked in crushed herbs with oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, for when those ligaments rot, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction is facilitated.
But if it is a large bone, such as a leg bone or thigh bone or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape that with a rasp only superficially until that blackness and corruption is removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to cut away all of it until the place where the corruption has reached is excised, and then treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh so that you may use it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, about thirty years old, who suffered pain in his thigh, and from the inner parts, a morbid matter spread through the body until that matter reached his thigh; and it was the matter of an abscess, with a large abscess, and there was no external cause for it.
And then time passed with it due to the error of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and much matter flowed from it, and its treatment was delayed until the leg was fistulated, and there were many orifices in it, all of which discharged pus and the humors of the body.
And then many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no improvement in his condition, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those orifices in great quantity; and the body of that man was completely weakened, and his complexion was yellow (citrine).
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and it reached the bone.
And then I quickly examined all the orifices and found that they extended to all parts of the leg.
Then I carefully incised one of those orifices until I exposed a part of the bone, and I found it corrupted, and it was already eroded, corrupted and blackened; and a part of the leg was corrupted, so that it had penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sawed away what was revealed to me and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I believed that in the bone there was only that corruption which I excised and sawed away, and I had already eradicated it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidative medicine for a long time, and it did not heal.
And then I iterated and uncovered the bone a second time, over the first uncovering, and I found continuing corruption in the bone.
And I sawed away what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was frustrated in making its cure and restoration, and it was not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and did not cease to incise the bone little by little, piece by piece, and I forced its restoration, and it was not restored until I incised about a palm's breadth of it, and that I extracted with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicines, and it was lightly consolidated and healed.
And further iteration in its operation and section is not necessary except for the disposition of its weakness and for the smallness of its tolerance and fear of death, for syncope afflicted it at all hours because of the superfluity of evacuation.
And therefore it is healed by a complete healing, and nothing is in place of the hard bone flesh; and its disposition in its body is made good and its strength returned; and it is converted to its dispositions and did not cause it impediment in walking nor harm in any matter.
But if a bone protrudes from the body already excised, then you must cut that in this manner:  you take a ligature and bind that to the extremity of that protruding bone, and command someone to extend it upwards; and place that woolen ligature thicker than the first ligature; and then tie that over the flesh that is beneath the bone, and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh to the parts beneath, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place a block or board beneath the bone decently, for when you do this, the saw is not prohibited from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be done over the corrupt place little by little, for if corruption is in the concavity of the bone and does not appear in its outward appearance to the senses, then force the sawing of it another time.
And if the bone is corrupt and not protruding, with one part continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle part or in a part of it, then you uncover the flesh from all parts completely; and then place wood underneath, and then saw that from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the location of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupt joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no reason for it except for ingenuity/skill.  (The meaning is somewhat ambiguous;  "engen" likely refers to surgical skill or cleverness needed to address such a complex case.)
And if the corruption is in the bone of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, it is necessary that you inquire into the corruption in whatever manner it appears to you, and that you open that, and clean that according to each disposition which is possible for you, and with each tool it should be rectified for you, when it does not oppose you in vein or nerve.
And know that the incisors and saws to incise these bones are many according to the placement of the bones, and the preparation of those, and the thickness of those, and the subtlety and the size and the smallness of those, and the hardness and their rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument convenient to that operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the type of instrument of which you have need; because with you there is great study and long and knowledgeable experience of the ways of this art, and witness of the ways of illnesses, for then you already have for yourself that which is convenient for the instruments for every illness.
And I am informing you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will place as simple tools with which you will proceed in your prohibition which you will experiment upon others. Of the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
The extremities may become diseased either by intrinsic or extrinsic causes, or if you have treated that corruption with medications and the treatment is ineffective, or you see the corruption spreading through the limb without any apparent cause, then you should incise that limb to the point where the corruption has reached, so that the patient escapes death or the worse affliction of limb loss.
And the sign of this affliction is that the limb turns black, as if fire has already burnt it, or the blackness spreads until the disease reaches the point where it affects the limb and spreads to the whole body; therefore, treat it with incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the striking of some venomous creatures, such as a sea scorpion, a viper, or a shrew, and the like.
And if the corruption or the injury is in the extremity of a finger, then you should incise the finger and not give space for the corruption to spread to the hand.
And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you should incise it from the root of the wrist, and do not allow the corruption to spread to the forearm.
And if it affects the arm, then incise the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that case is the death of the patient; and to administer any other remedy besides that is a waste of effort.
And similarly, for the foot: when corruption affects a toe, then you should incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the instep of the foot, then you should incise the whole foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you should incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy for him but resignation, and to leave the patient to death.
And the manner of incising or cauterizing a limb is that you should tighten a ligature on the lower part which you wish to incise, and tighten another ligature above the location; and let the assistant stretch a ligature to the lower parts, and let the assistant stretch another ligature above; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures, little by little, until all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be incised or cauterized.
And it is necessary that a linen cloth be placed over all parts so that it does not touch the healthy location, and that the patient receives a well-adjusted lesion and a hot abscess.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while operating, then you should carefully cauterize the affected area, or apply some blood-stanching powders; and then return to your medication until it is completed.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot with a burn that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe until it spread to the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, because he saw the cure going to the limb with such vehemence that he was overcome by pain and burning, and he incised that foot to the joint, and it healed.
For a long time, he experienced the same corruption in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I tried to repel that superfluity with what I applied to his hand in medicines, for the purification of his body; and the superfluity was not repelled and began to spread to another finger until it began in the hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, and I did not want to do that, hoping for the repulsion of that superfluity, and even more fearing the incision of his hand might be fatal, because the patient's strength was failing.
And after he left me, he returned to his land, and it came to me that that man prepared and incised his whole hand, and he was healed.
And I have included this narrative only so that it may be helpful in the case of this illness, and that it may serve as a sign by which it may be recognized and according to which it may be treated.
On the treatment of sinuses and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy member and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus has come out of it, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is over it is like thin cloth, and it has not reached a further corruption, and does not impinge on bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
However, if the space is prolonged until the corruption affects any member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And its cure is that you incise that skin entirely as it is swollen, and especially when it is thinned and made like cloth, and ensure that it does not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you suspect that the skin adheres and that the corruption has not reached further consequence, and the skin has a change of color, then you treat that with a clyster.
And consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the pus that is in it is of a foul odor, then clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine which is made of oil and sour wine and honey, taking equal parts, and let them be mixed in a vessel, and let them be boiled over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
Then take what is necessary from it and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with this, and constrict its orifice, and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
And then extract that with a sponge; and do this for as many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul smell is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with lye water, when this medicine is not available to you; and you take the ash of vines, and put water on it; and then strain this and clysterize with it until you are sure that the sinus is then clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the lye water, then clysterize with water and honey and some crushed honey added, and clysterize with wine and honey mixed, for it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes it adhere to the sinus, especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are sure that the sinus is now clean, then clysterize it with something that will cause the flesh to grow there.
And you should take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or clysterize with other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, so that the clyster cannula cannot be inserted, then widen it little by little with a small iron instrument, and place a pledget wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment inside until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should bring its lips together with a suture, leaving enough space for the clyster to enter without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the pus flows is elevated in the upper parts, then dry the lowest point so that the pus flows downwards, for when the pus is confined within the depth of the sinus, the flesh will not regenerate there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus to the lowest point as desired, then ensure that you prepare the limb with a preparation by which the pus flows lightly as is suitable, and that nothing hinders the flow of the pus.
But the cloth that is over the opening of the sinus, this should be changed every hour so that the pus that is contained within it may be removed. And so that you may know if the pus that flows from it is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this, consider the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter that is discharged onto the opening of the sinus is already adhering, or is about to adhere, then on the following day, or the third day, remove it; and when you clean the wound, then change the cloth to another and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus, nearby in the space, there is subtle poison, then do not despair of its adherence, because its healing is approaching, for there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and of these there are those in whom the opposite is true.
And if from the sinus, for many days, undigested pus has issued, then know that the sinus will not close; and if the closing of the sinus is delayed and the cause prolonged, then you should adjust to drying medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they are dry such as diafinicon ointment, when superfluous addition of zegi is adjusted there.
And the best remedy with which it is cured is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and add old wine equal to it in substance, and mix that with it, and use that.
And it acts similarly in that situation, when there is no pus: diafinicon ointment, honey cooked until thickened, and use it; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, crush everything and powder it over the honey while it is on the fire, and then spread this on a cloth and bind it over the sinus; and spread that with the honey which you have cooked until it is thickened, and powder it on a cloth and bind it.
And sometimes are administered with it aristolochia longa and roots of lily of the valley (and it is lily of the valley) and flour of barley and centaury, and use those simples or compounds, crush those, sift them, and powder them over the honey which has been mentioned; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the depth of it, and it has already investigated the bones or other hard members or ligaments; then you treat it with fistular treatment, as above is said.
Concerning the treatment of paronychia and worn nails and added digit incision and section.
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of the finger or toe of the hand or foot, and quickly spreads to the other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its treatment is less valued, or it is suppurated by a hot abscess, or it is corrupted, and throws out pus until it corrodes the root of the nail, and quickly corrupts the whole of it, and quickly its corruption penetrates to the bone until it has a fetid odor, and the extremity of the finger is swollen, and its color is darkened.
And therefore when you have treated it with what we have said in the division and you do not obtain a cure, then it is necessary that you incise with an iron all the superfluity that remains of the nail and then cauterize the wound, because cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is healthy and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail, emptied, already compress the flesh at the edges, and begin to prick and injure it, then it is necessary that you place a thin file under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and push it up and incise that flesh easily, and place on what remains of the flesh corrosive medicines until it is all removed; and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already penetrated the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and extract it, for a bone that is corrupted will never heal.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption going into the finger, then you incise it at one of the joints as its healing is considered appropriate, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if a blow or friction happens to the nail, and vehement pain results, then it is necessary that you first bleed the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp instrument with a transverse cut from above downwards, and take care that the cut does not reach the flesh that is under the nail, for you will cause the patient vehement pain, and it will be the cause of the generation of superfluous flesh in that place; and then treat that place with something that relieves pain.
But the said finger, which is born in the hands of some men, is very quickly fleshy, and very quickly bony, or very quickly has nails.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or their birth is in some of the joints of the fingers. But that which is born in some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born in the joints is very quickly painful.
And of those that are born fleshy, the excision is easy, and it is that you incise that one below its root with a broad instrument.
But of that which is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its excision.
But in that which is born on a finger between the joints, it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a round incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then cure that until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between their knuckles, whether by the birth of a man born thus, or by the consolidation of a wound, or by burning with fire, or similarly, then it is necessary that you separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not easily consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as necessary.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you separate that consolidation according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the member.
Of incisions of vices, there are various.
Vices are various, and are twisted, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and most often in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers and laborers and those who carry burdens.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But the cure of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that it be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be elevated and entirely extracted.
The incision of it is in this manner:  first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick, feculent blood is resolved from the leg. Then bind the patient's leg tightly from the highest part of the thigh to below the knee with a bandage, and then incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision. Then, with your hand, press the black blood from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from above downwards, until the quantity of blood you desire and deem sufficient has been released, and which will remove the patient's malady.
Then bind it up, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But the elevation of them is according to this manner: you who shave the patient's leg, if there is much hair on it, and then place him in a bath and bathe his leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are distended; or, if a bath is not available to you, have him engage in vigorous exercise until the limb is warmed.
Then incise the vein in the right place, either along its length, behind the knee, or at its lowest point below the ankle.
Then open the vein with a lancet and scarify it on all sides until it bleeds, and it is by its appearance that you see that red vein tending towards blackness.
And then, as it drains from the vein, you see that white thing which is like a string.
Then you place a linen strip underneath so that it is elevated and is drawn away from the vein, and suspend it with a golden or lead weight.
Then make another incision near that incision for three fingers' breadth, and scarify the vein in the part above the vein until it bleeds.
Then elevate it and incise it in the second incision near the heel, and then extract it and elevate it until it is drawn out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision that is above that.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is most superior or highest of all the incisions, until it is all removed; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you to be extracted and elevated, then introduce a needle with strong double thread and tie it and extract it, or introduce a linen strip underneath and rotate your hand in every direction until it is removed. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is made difficult for you to elevate it and it becomes a danger to the patient because of that damage.
And when it is all expelled, then place on the wound site wool soaked in wine and red and un-red oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the intestine is constricted, having a tortuosity to which there is a twisting in parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you make incisions on it at every point of its tortuosity and in the places of its twisting, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it entirely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
The form of the incision with which the intestine is cut:
It is not sharpened like hooks, nor is it of a sharp point so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of a thick and flexible nature, because if it were thin, it would cut the vein due to its thinness; it has superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little humidity, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And it is quickly generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, and maggots and worms are generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the onset of this vein is that a vehement inflammation happens in the leg; and then the area is blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, which resembles the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachm to two drachms, and tie it and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as any part of it emerges outwards, wrap that in lead and tie it. And if the growth is prolonged, then you incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise the root until it has emerged, for if you incise it, it is retracted and enters the flesh and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is completely expelled and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein, in some men, a growth emerges which is five palms and fingers in length, and it has even reached me that one emerged which was twenty palms.
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then introduce a probe into the opening and perforate it with a long opening into the body, until everything that is in the material is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the place for several days with medicines; and then cure it according to the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices come to it, and a branch emerges from each orifice.
And therefore cure it as we have said in the division and with what is said above.
Concerning the incision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine sickness.
This illness is called in some lands near us bovine illness because it often happens to cattle and is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and goes throughout the body rising and falling, and is shown by the feeling of its movement from limb to limb until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms and snakes and earthworms and ascarids happen in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, for when it goes into the body and is elevated to the head, and has reached the eye, then very quickly it opens it and enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure this and extract it, then it is done only by observing its movement and appearance to the touch, and then it is necessary that you constrict what is above it and what is below it with a strong bandage and constriction, and then dry it above and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you apply a cautery with fire to the place until it dies, and the greatest danger to be feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see it has reached the head near the eye, then bind above the eyebrow with a strong constriction, and then dry above it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use purification of their body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and guard against foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the illness nafir. (or, On the incision of nafir)
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in some members, and then is moved from member to member.
And I have already seen that, as I will recount to you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the superficial vein of the arm, and after it had remained for an hour, I saw that inflammation moving like a worm climbing upwards, but more lightly than one might fear, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and there it remained for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then I realised that it was moving throughout her body, as I saw; and I was very amazed at its movement and the speed from limb to limb. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes as clearly as in the woman.
And I considered that this was so because the woman was old, with dry flesh and exposed veins, and in her, that shifting windiness was apparent to the senses.
And it is necessary that it not appear, according to this reverse cure, in those who are delicate and with moist flesh and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, but it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press lightly on that area and dry it until that constricted windiness is expelled, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it with rubbing of the body and with things that expel windiness, and drive it away with foul-smelling pills and pills of serapium and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body in which they lodge, for their diversity is such that some have large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and some have three edges, and some have barbs; and some have feathers.
And of the signs in the brain, when an arrow lodges there and penetrates the bone, and emerges from the cranium which is over the brain, for it causes a sudden vehement headache, and dimness of vision, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of mind, and vomiting of bile, and the speech is quickly cut off and the voice is destroyed, and there emerges from the place of the wound a white humidity resembling pus, and pus from it resembling the watery part of flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it lodges in the heart, is that it is near the left breast, and it feels as if it were stuck in something hard, and not in something empty; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood runs from the wound, and there is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope; and therefore know that death approaches without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are thus: frothy blood comes from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are inflamed, and the color of the patient is changed, and he breathes with great difficulty, and desires the intake of cold air.
And if the arrow lodges in the diaphragm which is in the chest, then the pain is near the lower ribs, and great swelling is produced with vehement pain and with sighing, and not all the members of the muscles are affected.
And if the arrow lodges in the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes out of the wound.
And if the arrow lodges in the abdomen, and is stuck in it, there comes some pus from the wound, or the intestine, or bowel which is ruptured, and there is no hope in its cure without extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow lodges in the bladder, and urine comes out, and some matter proceeds from it to the external parts, and very quickly pain overcomes the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and similar members, are saved according to the best dispositions when the arrow does not find a vein nor an artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you that I have seen of the being of these arrows, so that you may signify by this concerning your cure.
The reason is, because in a man an arrow fell into the lacrimal duct of his eye at the root of the nose, and I extracted it for him from the other side, below the lobe of his ear, and he is healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which fell into him in the lobe of his eye, below the eyelid further down, and the arrow was already hidden and I only found a small extremity with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion head on it which had no barbs.
And the Jew is healed, and no ill accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and this is the kind that has barbs; and then I incised upon it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I proceeded subtly with it, and I extracted it, and the Christian is healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and then, since thirty days had passed and nothing had changed in his condition, I incised over the arrow and pulled it out, and he is healed, and no ill accident befell him.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his back, and the wound had healed over him, and since it was almost seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman into whom an arrow fell in her belly, and the wound is healed within her, and nothing is changed in her condition, and the woman finds no harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his face, and the wound is healed and the arrow broken, finding no great pain here, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the man of the king's princes which had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already declined a little to the right side, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his cure, concerning the arrow wound, within three days.
And then I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; and then I examined it with a slender probe, and I did not feel it; but he felt a prickling under his right ear, and then I suspected that this prickling was due to the point of the arrow.
And I plastered the place with a plaster which has attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the place would suppurate so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could operate on it.
And no significant accident occurred in the place that the arrow had reached; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no accident occurred; and the wound healed meanwhile, and the patient desisted from extraction for many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and recounted this to me.
And then I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with the probe I examined it, and I found the point of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I applied to the appearance of the wound that sharp medicine until the point of the arrow appeared visibly to me, and I spent time with this for the space of four months.
And then, since the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and remove it, and I did not succeed in this way, then I did not cease to refine and devise a method for it, until I managed one day, using ingenious methods, to grasp it with specially made forceps, as the form of those will appear at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored that wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, and I restored it, and the wound healed, and the patient was completely healthy, and he suffered no harm from it.
And I recount to you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows so that you may make a rule and explanation of that which I have not said, for the parts of this art and its definitions are not comprehended by narration nor do books encompass them.
And skilled artisans compare the small with the large; and that which is present with that which is absent; and they find new operations and new instruments because of the unexpected occurrences of extraneous causes in the art.
Therefore I say that arrows are not removed from the members into which they are fixed except in two ways: either by traction from the place where they enter; or by the opposite action from the other side.
And that which is removed from the place where it entered, is an arrow lodged in a fleshy place, and is pulled and removed.
And if it does not yield to such removal at the time of extraction, then it is necessary that you leave it for some days until the flesh around it softens, and its extraction and removal are eased.
And similarly, if it is fixed in bone and does not yield to removal, then leave it for some days and repeat the attempt with traction and movement each day, and it will eventually be removed.
And if it still does not yield after some days, then it is necessary that you perforate the bone around the arrow on all sides with a thin drill until the space around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden within the body and is beyond feeling, then you should probe for it with a probe; and if you feel it, then extract it with whatever instruments are suitable for its removal.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the probe, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then you should incise the wound until it is widened enough to allow extraction of the arrow.
And if it is held fast by barbs, then you should free the flesh in which it is embedded from all sides with all the skill you can muster; and devise a way to overcome this, if you cannot manage it by freeing the flesh from the barbs and twisting them until they release.
And when you undertake the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use twisting with your hand and forceps on all sides until you make it yield;  and proceed with great delicacy so that the arrow is not broken, making its removal difficult.
And if you cannot manage it at that time, then leave it for a few days until the parts surrounding it soften, and then attempt it again, for it will then be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then apply the remedies we have described in the appropriate chapter.
And be careful about cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use every possible means to remove the arrow; and do this with ease, calm, slowness, and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And so, that you may counteract the arrow so that the patient is positioned according to the direction in which the arrow entered, because that is more helpful. But if that is not possible for you, then apply what is possible according to the circumstances.
But if the arrows are protruding from the opposite side, or if some part of it already extends from the inside to the outside, or the tip of the arrow is crooked due to the feeling of the part above the deep wound and sometimes that protrudes; then you cut on that side; and let the incision be according to the size that the forceps can grasp; and then pull it out, for it will easily come out that way.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then you twist the blade in the opposite direction until the arrow is loosened from the bone, and widen it yourself, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is inside, then push it in with him/her.
But if the shaft has fallen off and you want to administer a push, then you introduce, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that the concavity of it enters the tail of the arrow and then push it in.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then you push it in with a strong instrument entering into it, because the arrow is relieved by it.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you remove all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and then treat it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow falls into the foot, or into the belly, or into the bladder, or into the side, and it is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to grasp it, then grasp it and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and extract it, and then sew the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the forceps with which arrows are drawn out:
Let the ends of their jaws be like the beak of a bird, made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they do not let go of it.
And of these, many types are made, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its tightness.
like a ray, so that the entry into the concave arrow is eased and it is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the usual number is thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; that is, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called *alhasisain*; two veins which are in the two apparent temples; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes which are called *aspicientz*; and the vein situated in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose; and two *guidegi* which are at the neck; and two veins which are in the upper lip; and two veins which are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins which are called *algelic*—; and there are two veins which are under the tongue. But the veins which are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of these, one is the cephalic, and it is on the outer side of the arm; and the common people call it “the vein of the head”, and it is a superficial vein, and is composed of a branch of the basilic, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people call this one “vein of the whole body”, and the basilic is a vein which is situated on the inner side of the arm, and it is called “citillar”, and the common people call it “vein of the belly”; and the *funis* of the arm, and it is situated upon the forearm, and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears upon the bend of the elbow by manifest appearance; and the *salvatella*, and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger, and it has two branches in it.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of these is below the bend of the knee on the outer side; and the second is the saphenous, and its location is below the leg on the inner side; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the outer side; and in the leg are other veins similar to these.
The opening of the veins near the ears is beneficial for chronic catarrh, migraine, headaches, and old, bad ulcers of the head.
And the manner of bleeding those veins is as I recount to you: that is, the patient's head should be shaved, and the patient's head should be rubbed at the occiput in the location of both veins with a wide cloth with good friction; then the patient should tighten their neck with a bandage until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed places on the head; and feel for them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of these with your finger, then bleed them by incision.
Then you take the lancet, which is sharp, and you insert it under the vein in the direction of the bone until the blood flows. Then raise your hand with the vein and the elbow to the upper parts and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two fingers placed together, or thereabouts; and let out as much blood as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And already and previously, at the beginning of the book, the incision of these and their cauterization [was described].
But of the two arteries that are in the temples, the benefit of phlebotomy is for chronic and sudden severe migraines, and persistent ophthalmia, and a body with acute superfluities effused to the eyes.
And the manner of phlebotomizing these is as I recount to you: constrain the patient's neck strongly so that the two veins appear to the touch by manifest appearance and their pulse is demonstrated beneath your finger; and then you bleed with a lancet.
Then you lift the skin above the vein at the upper parts with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy incision underneath, and raise the vein upward; and incise it just as you have done in the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to the quantity of your necessity.
And then loosen the constriction of the patient, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then place cotton and powder on it and bind it above with strong compression, and leave it until it is healed.
And now the remembrance of them, and of their incision, and attraction is preceded at the beginning of the book.
Therefore, the benefit of phlebotomy of the frontal vein is in addition to phlebotomy of the cephalic vein for ancient diseases of the face, and for migraine, stiffness, and foul ulcers.
And the quality of his phlebotomy is as I have recounted to you; that is, that the patient should constrict his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is named a lancet, of which this is the form:
Place the prominent point which is at the head of the lancet upon that vein and strike it with a hammer or with something else in the manner of it, and let the blood flow according to the quantity which you wish; and then loosen the perforation of the patient and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a lateral phlebotomy, however it is not fitting that the phlebotomy be of an acute extremity like the other phlebotomies, rather it should be of a blunt extremity, and phlebotomize it across, because the bone is near and would easily break there with the phlebotomy when it is sharp.
But the benefit of the two veins that are at the tear ducts of the two eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for stiffness and swelling in the eyes, and for diseases of the face.
And the manner of the phlebotomy is that the patient should constrict his neck with a bandage, and then phlebotomize those veins, and you should be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made across rather than lengthwise, a little, with a small phlebotomy instrument, because in that place there is no flesh; and if the phlebotomy instrument is sharp, its point will very easily be broken.
And then let the blood flow as much as is necessary to you, and place cotton over it, and bind it tightly for one night, and then unbind it.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and violent headache, and for diseases of the face and migraine and inflammation that occurs in the face, and especially when it is chronic.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is that the patient should constrict his neck, and then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a long, thin phlebotomy instrument and insert it into the middle of the bridge of the nose, straight, because the vein is not apparent to the touch there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
As you move your hand with the small phlebotomy instrument little by little, let blood flow according to your needs; and then bind it for a night, for it will heal easily.
But bleeding from both temples is helpful for constriction of the throat and the beginning of leprosy, and for many illnesses, impetigo, and bad, corrosive ulcers, as well as herpes.
And the manner of phlebotomy in these [temples] is that the patient should hold each side of his neck tightly with a bandage; and the practitioner should stand above the patient's head, and the patient should be seated on a stool.
Then phlebotomize the vein lengthwise with a wide phlebotomy instrument little by little; and draw out blood according to a moderate quantity or according to what you see is necessary; and then do likewise to the other vein.
Then untie the bandage and tighten the two veins with a moderate constriction so that the patient is not suffocated, and leave it until morning, for the wound will be healed.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the alveolar veins [veins near the gums] helps with small abscesses and pustules of the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and bad ulcers and fissures, and bad ulcers that are in the nose and around it.
And the manner of phlebotomy of these [veins] is that you make the patient sit before you, and bind his neck with a bandage, and then roll back his lips and look for two veins, of which one is on the right of the lip and the second on the left; and the identification of each is by the blackness of each of the two, and this is more so because in the vicinity of these are small, black veins; and then incise each with an appropriate incision.
But if you are in doubt, and you do not know which is which, then you should incise the larger of those veins, and the most apparent one.
And do the same for the two veins that are in the upper lips.
But of the two veins that are below the tongue, the phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is suitable for affections that are in the throat, for diseases of the uvula, and for diseases of the mouth.
And the method of phlebotomizing these is that you hold the patient between your hands facing the sun, and raise their tongue, and look under the tongue, on one side you will see one vein and on the other side another vein, and the color of each is tending towards blackness.
And then phlebotomize each one; and be careful not to extend your hand too far when phlebotomizing these, because below these are arteries.
And the veins that are phlebotomized in the neck are those which are common to all men, and their phlebotomy is done in two ways: either they are opened with a myrtle- or olive-shaped phlebotomy instrument of fine point; or they are incised with a lancet-shaped phlebotomy instrument, and this is called the "alnessil", and this is its form:
Let its width be like this, and it is suitable for opening concave, full, prominent, thick veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotomy instrument is of lesser width and a more subtle point, and is suitable for phlebotomy of fine veins containing thin, citrine blood.
But the benefit of phlebotomy of the basilic vein, which is one of three veins: because it draws blood from illnesses that are below the throat and neck, and from what affects the feet and abdomen.
And it is necessary for the phlebotomist, when performing this phlebotomy, to carefully observe and be very cautious, because beneath it is an artery, and if he errs and is inaccurate in the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will incise that artery and cause a flow of blood; therefore, it is necessary that one does not perform this phlebotomy with a sharp phlebotomy instrument, before it is cut with a lancet.
And if the basilic vein is not clearly visible, then you should abandon it and move to another, or seek one of its branches; or perform phlebotomy at the end of the arm in its place, for that is clearly visible; and incise it with a lancet-like phlebotomy instrument as we have said.
But if you wish to perform phlebotomy on the same vein, then before constricting the arm you should touch the area until you locate the pulse, and then mark it with a pen; and then tie the arm and incise the vein with a concave incision using a lancet-like phlebotomy instrument as we have said; and be careful that the incision does not fall into the area of the artery.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, swelling in the area you marked with the pen, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, and therefore avoid that area.
And if you see the blood flowing from the phlebotomy like a child's urine, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is blood from the artery; and then you should quickly place your finger over it for a long time; and then remove your finger, and if the bleeding stops, because many times it stops, then you should loosen the arm and leave it.
And guard the patient from their movement, and let there be a great care for them, and do not anger them for some days until they are healed.
But if the bleeding is not stopped, and you see that you do not have a remedy for this, then you should incise the artery, if it appears to you; because its extremity is constricted, and the bleeding will stop; or that you take bark of the fig tree, and grind it, and take one half and apply it tightly to the area of the vein with a descending ligature and powders until the next day.
And then if the bleeding is stopped, it is good; and if not, then treat it with that which is preceded in the recollection of the place of the powders that stop the flow of blood.
And the stopping of their blood is difficult in many cases because of the small size of the wound and the possibility of tying the arm.
But in the case of the dark vein, phlebotomy is helpful, extracting blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; for this reason, because it is composed of branches of the basilica and of branches of the cephalic, as we have said.
And it behooves the phlebotomist to be very careful in the phlebotomy, because beneath it is a nerve; and if he happens to pierce the nerve during phlebotomy, the patient will suffer numbness, and its cure is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely. And this nerve often appears visibly; and if it is hidden from some men and is subtle and is not shown, then it behooves you to use a phlebotomy tool with which you phlebotomize with a slanted incision, and avoid the nerve with your skill.
And if the vein is between two nerves, then you should open the vein lengthwise.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and helps with eye diseases.
And it is fitting in this case, if you wish, that the phlebotomist punctures with an olive-shaped or myrtle-shaped phlebotomy tool; because it is the safest of all veins, for there is neither artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, it is often quickly abscessed in some men, when it is not phlebotomized at the first puncture, but this abscess does no harm.
The first of these is that you should know that phlebotomy is administered only for the preservation of health and for its hope and caution against the onset of illnesses, and that phlebotomy should be performed in one of the veins that are in the arm, that is: cephalic, median, and basilic; and that phlebotomy should be performed at the beginning of spring, on an empty stomach in spring and autumn, and in winter near mealtime, when signs of repletion appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the third day or the first, after three hours of the day have passed.
But phlebotomy, which is administered, more when it provokes suitability and necessity for it, either by night or by day, at any hour and at any time.
And it is necessary that a child should not be phlebotomized until they are fourteen years old, and a pregnant woman should not be phlebotomized except from the fourth month until the seventh, and old women who have already passed sixty years should not be phlebotomized.
And if someone wants to be phlebotomized in any way, they must cleanse their bowels before phlebotomy with a clyster (enema), if they have much retained waste in them, so that the veins do not draw the superfluities from the bowels into the phlebotomy, which are putrid, and that they may not harm the principal members.
And let not patients with persistent nausea and diarrhea be phlebotomized, nor those recovering from illness or fever until that is remedied in them, nor patients with dropsy; and let phlebotomy be feared near colic, vomiting, and abdominal pain, and near excessive sexual intercourse, and hard work and exercise, fasting or vigils, and any cause that dissolves strength, whether corporeal or animal.
And consider carefully the thinning of humors beforehand, if the blood is thick, with foods and drinks, and with medicines if this is possible for him.
And then enter a bath if no cause prohibits it, or be exercised by some activity so that the blood may be thinned.
And perform the phlebotomy in the middle of the day, that is, around the third hour, as we have said.
And perhaps you should ensure that the patient's feet are guarded on that day from all harmful accidents, both animal, such as anxiety, anger, fear, and all bodily accidents, such as excessive work and exertion, sexual intercourse, and things similar to these.
And in his room, let there be present things whose use is customary among men, with aromatics and perfumes, and with balsams and similar things, according to what is possible.
And let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion that is higher than the cushion of the one who is to be phlebotomized.
Then, extend his arm, and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand two or three times; and then tighten the ligature with a strap and wrap it around twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, for if the constriction is not even, or if the constriction is excessive, then it prevents the flow of blood; or if it is too loose, then it prevents the blood from flowing out properly.
And near the constriction, it is necessary that the one who is to be phlebotomized rub his hands together until the veins are swollen and become clearly visible.
Then let the phlebotomist anoint his phlebotomy lancet with a little old oil; and then place the index finger of his left hand on the vein, below the place where he wants to perform the phlebotomy, so that the vein is slightly displaced, and the percussion is stopped, because those veins which you find thus contracting, like a fleeing string,  should not be phlebotomized; and those that are full of air and contract when you place the phlebotomy lancet on them should be avoided by the phlebotomist; and if the lancet does not open the vein, or if it does open it, it is too tight.
Therefore, the phlebotomist must be skilled and attentive in all these matters.
Then apply the phlebotomy lancet; but if the vein is opened at once, it is good; and if not, repeat the process a little below that place, or above it carefully, if the place is not abscessed.
And if the place is abscessed, or the patient is frightened, then leave it for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage, for it will quickly draw a hot abscess, nor should he enter a bath.
Then repeat the phlebotomy if needed.
And if the opening is small and the blood flows subtly, and you fear that the desired quantity of blood will not be drawn, then repeat the phlebotomy in that same straight line and enlarge the opening slightly, and do this carefully before the place becomes abscessed, for in many men the place becomes abscessed from a small opening.
But if you see that it is already abscessed, then do not repeat the procedure there, for it will not benefit you in any way; and place some olive oil on it, for it relieves the body of blood. And olive oil is better in this instance than other oils.
Similarly, administer olive oil in every phlebotomy with which you open veins to prevent blood clotting; and they [the texts/doctors] prescribe theriac and sagapenum when one of these is placed on the location, because the blood is thinned and its thickness is resolved.
And if a large abscess develops at the site of the phlebotomy—for this often happens, especially to someone who has been bled only once or where the opening in the vein was small—then make a compress and place on the site a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water, and squeeze it for an hour, for this will dissolve it.
And it is necessary to do this immediately after the bleeding from that vein, or from another vein, is complete.
And if some blackness or greenness remains at the site for some days, do not worry; and if you wish, apply something made of dissolved aloe and myrrh, or something made of juice of mint or calamint, or something similar.
And abscesses and swellings often occur after phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
Then place your hand on it, and if you then find softness under your compression, then that swelling is malignant and bad; and then be careful to apply something of what we have said, for blood will quickly flow from the artery.
And make a plaster with something that has styptic properties, so that the place is hardened, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract blood from each person according to the quantity of their strength and according to the color predominating in the blood as it appears.
For if the blood is black, then you let it flow until it is red; and similarly, if you see it thick, then you let it flow until it is thinned; and similarly, if it is sharp (thin), until its sharpness is diminished.
And it is necessary for someone who is full and strong and needs bloodletting at once, that the phlebotomy of the vein be widened and the phlebotomy be wide.
And in someone who is weak, the opposite should be done, and it is necessary to extract blood in several times, and the opening should be narrow.
And the best way to administer phlebotomy of a vein is that the section be transverse, not a puncture.
And this manner of phlebotomy is safe from excessive bleeding and the cutting of nerves, and it is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy according to length and breadth (i.e., a long incision).
And to someone accustomed to fainting during phlebotomy, it is necessary that you treat them before phlebotomy with a little bread infused in pomegranate wine or simple warmed oxymel; and extract the blood in three or four times.
And if there is coldness of complexion, then before phlebotomy take bread infused in syrup of myrrh or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices such as pepper, cinnamon, ginger, and the like, or in an aromatized and very fragrant syrup.
And if syncope occurs during phlebotomy, and the cause is the loss of much blood, then it is necessary that he drink meat broth and a very subtly fragrant syrup; and use aromatics with vinegar, and let his feet be bathed with it.
And use the other remedies that we have mentioned in the section on syncope due to evacuation (of blood).
And whoever wants to bleed his arm and dissolve his blood a second time, should, if the phlebotomy is for much evacuation, and his strength is weak, evacuate the blood little by little, that is, according to the amount of strength over continuous days.
But whoever wants to bleed his arm and shed his blood a second time, and his body is strong, then let him do so about seven or nine hours after the first phlebotomy.
But whoever wants to draw blood from his body away from the affected area, should be bled on the second or third day.
But that body in which there is much already heated blood, and it is sharp and causes fever, should have blood drawn from it at once, and a large quantity should be drawn, and the opening should be enlarged until syncope occurs, provided you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy; and that you place your hand on his pulse near the blood vessel, so that death does not result instead of syncope, for this often happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent.
And it is not fitting, when you want to untie the arm and evacuate the blood a second time, that since the opening of the vein is already closed and the exit of the blood is made difficult, that you press on it with vehemence and bend it slightly, for this is very harmful.
Having left it until it is bled a second time; or remove it with the sharpness of the phlebotomy instrument that is coagulated by the blood in the opening of the vein; or place on it theriac alfarach, or sage, and compress it with light compression until the blood has flowed out.
And if the vein is abscessed, then leave it and do not touch it until the abscess is calmed.
And if necessity compels you to evacuate the blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then you bleed it in that place or in the other arm in another vein.
And the vein called "rope of the arm" is phlebotomized instead of the cephalic or basilic, when they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of those.
And the quality of his phlebotomy is that the patient puts his hand in warm water until the vein reddens and appears manifestly.
Then it should be tightened with a bandage with moderate stricture; and then phlebotomize according to the small transversal, neither laterally nor lengthwise.
And let the opening be wide, and perform your phlebotomy where you phlebotomize it, a little above the joint of the hand.
And if the flow of blood is impeded, then put the hand back in warm water and let the blood flow into the water until you achieve your need.
But if you are in the summer days, then you are excused from putting the hand back in warm water, and the reason is that the body's blood is warmer in winter.
And phlebotomy from this vein is safer than from all other veins, because below it there is no pulsating vein, nor nerves.
And phlebotomy of the little finger vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver diseases.
And the manner of phlebotomy is that you bind the hand with a ligature or with your hand, after you have placed it in warm water, until the vein is swollen and clearly visible; and then phlebotomize it transversely; and if you incise it completely, it will not harm in any way.
And take care not to extend your incision too far with the phlebotomy, because below are the nerves of the fingers, denuded of flesh.
And then return the hand to warm water and let the blood flow into it, for if you do not return it to the warm water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein, and the flow will be impeded.
And when the blood has been drawn in the necessary quantity, then place oil and salt on the vein, so that it is not quickly consolidated; and similarly you should proceed in every narrow branch.
But the benefit of phlebotomy in the left hand is that it is helpful for diseases of the spleen.
And perform the phlebotomy on him similarly as you have done on the other.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the saphenous vein is for diseases that are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the womb, and retention of menses, and for the diseases of old sores and ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar diseases.
And the manner of phlebotomy on him is that the patient should put his foot in warm water and rub it until the veins appear well; and tie it above the joint of the foot with a strap; and the place of the vein is usually apparent above the big toe, and from that, many branches are spread out on the surface of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize the largest branch below the leg, above the ankle, because it is better and healthier.
And if you phlebotomize that on the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves that are below it on the surface of the foot, and perform your phlebotomy in such a way that you phlebotomize across, as if you wanted to incise it.
And let the phlebotomy be skillful.
But if the blood's outflow is prohibited; then you immerse his foot in warm water and let the blood flow into it until it is evacuated.
And if the phlebotomist errs on the first attempt, then repeat the phlebotomy a little higher, because the location is safe; and do not fear harming him, but beware of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly do the same to the saphenous vein of the other foot.
And of the sciatic vein, the location, as we have said, is below the heel, under the anklebone on the outer side.
And the benefit of phlebotomy for him is for hip pain when it is due to hot blood.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is that the patient enter a bath, and tighten his thigh from the side of the hip up to above the ankle with a long, thin bandage for four fingers, because it will not appear otherwise.
And then when it appears, then you phlebotomize that according to whatever method is possible for you and according to the transverse vein; that is better. Or incise it by incision or cut it by section, for its location is safe.
And it is often hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it does not appear to the touch at all, then phlebotomize one of its branches, and that is the one that appears on the back of the foot between the little toe and the ring finger.
And beware of the nerves, and let the blood flow in the quantity you desire.
And then loosen the bandage and place cotton over the phlebotomy site and bind the area tightly, for it heals easily.
Concerning cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping vessels are made of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one is with scarification and blood extraction, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And the cupping vessels that are administered with scarification and blood extraction are applied to fourteen places of the body, of which one where cupping vessels are placed is at the occiput, which is the back part of the head; and the middle of the neck on both sides together; and cupping vessels on the chin below the jaw below the mouth; and cupping vessels on the two shoulder blades; and cupping vessels on the loins, which is above the end of the coccyx; and cupping vessels on the two hips, and they are on the two flanks; and cupping vessels on the two thighs; and cupping vessels on the two buttocks.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not diminish the vital strength in the same way as phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity calls for cupping because of illness or a continued condition, we administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it and at any time.
Therefore, when one sees any of those conditions, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we command that cupping be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it relieves heaviness in the head and that which is affecting the eyes; but this should be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not advisable to use it on someone of cold complexion, or in whom there is a catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a person.
And therefore it is not advisable to use it on the elderly, and on those whose heads are afflicted with cold illnesses.
But to one on whom it is continuously applied, forgetfulness is caused; and therefore you should instruct the cupper to place his hand lightly with the cupping glass at the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
But cupping with alkahel (likely referring to a specific substance or method) is in the place of phlebotomy of the dark vein (vena fusca) and the basilic vein: and therefore it is beneficial for asthma, and for the constriction of the throat, and for the rupture of an instrument in the throat (possibly a reference to a choking incident or injury), and for coughs, and for repletion (excess fluids).
And it is necessary that the alkahel cupping be applied a little higher, for if it is placed lower, it produces weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping with alhagdahan (likely another specific substance or method) benefits pains affecting the head, and ophthalmia (eye inflammation), and migraine, and suffocation, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper not to press his hand with the scarification, lest he injure the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits canker sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar mouth diseases; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the [likely a specific vein in the lip area,  possibly a mis-transcription or archaic term] which are in the two lips.
But cupping that is done over the two shoulder blades benefits against caphatan (likely a specific illness or symptom), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two inner parts of the ankles is helpful instead of phlebotomy of three veins, that is, the basilic, median cubital (fusca - possibly a variation in naming), and cephalic veins, because it draws blood from all those smaller veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And command the cupper that he not proceed excessively in scarification, because the place is devoid of flesh, and underneath are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping glass with which one cups over the buttocks and it benefits against hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you command the cupper that it be a large cupping glass, and that it be of brass, because the place requires strong suction; and quickly thin cupping glasses are broken and it should be scarified with scarification.
Moreover, cupping of the legs diminishes by manifest reduction, because it draws blood from the whole body, and it benefits old pains of the joints and of the womb, and of the bladder; and it provokes menstruation, and benefits against boils and swellings; and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and of the two iliac veins.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause fainting in many men.
And of the iliac cuppings, the benefit is similar to the benefit of cuppings of the two legs.
And the quality of the placement of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw by tempered suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place those lightly, and remove those carefully, and that the humors come to the place by equal admixture. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the redness of the blood appear.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and consider the condition of the body.
