Because that man who is of tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And command the cupper to enlarge the scarification and make it somewhat deeper, and to moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary to scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened for the blood and its watery part. But in the second [scarification], to the scrutiny acquired by the extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let a third scarification be made so that the desired result is achieved.
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with deep scarifications.
And the term scarification refers to the depth of the incision only.
And if the skin of the one to be treated is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are constricted, the places for the cupping glasses should be anointed with emollient, opening, and resolving ointments; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, violet oil, sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or blood oil, and similar things.
And if the excess [humor] is thick and cold, then the oil should be amaranth oil, or balm oil, or aloe oil, or anise oil, and similar things.
But if the patient has large pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be prohibited, and for such a one, the places for cupping should be washed with vinegar or cold water of nightshade, or with poppy water, or with  water of borage, and similar things.
And the one whose blood is of much humidity, wash his cupping sites with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar things.
And for the one whose humors are thick, wash his cupping sites with old wine, or with water of bugleweed (or marjoram), or with a decoction of anise, or of chamomile, and similar things.
And the cupping should be performed before the bath; rather, it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and he should sleep after the cupping.
What is required to regulate the patient before cupping or phlebotomy?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: if the person to be cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp, inflamed blood, then he should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, endive in sour wine, lettuce, hemp seed, julep, and similar things.
And give him foods such as chicken, and the meat of domestic birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and the like.
And for one whose complexion is cold, he should drink syrup of almonds, or syrup of hemp seed, and sumac, and very fragrant wine, between autumn and winter; and he should be advised to use little drink and food, and give him foods such as chicken, hemp seed, peas, small birds, and doves.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and phlebotomy, his drink should be greater than his food.
And it is necessary that at certain hours some men be given theriac, or medications of musk, that is, nutmeg, or theriac before cupping or phlebotomy, or shortly before, so that the principal members may be comforted and the blood may be thinned; and they should not take those heating things.
But the cuppings that are without scarification should be cuppings that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the places of the knees, and the urinary bladder, because these members do not tolerate scarification on them.
And it is not desired for those except the attraction of blood from a member, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in a disease of nasal bleeding; and we use this so that cold, invisible windiness may be dissolved from the member, just as we place it on the belly and navel; for this rarefies the member and warms it and diminishes the pain because it resolves windiness.
And it is placed on the nostrils when obstruction or swelling occurs in them, for by the virtue of strong attraction it quickly opens the obstruction and eradicates the swelling from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of windiness enclosed in them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in diseases of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger; that is, hot water alone, or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Method of applying a fire cupping glass: let the width of the opening be two fingers, as we have formed it; and the length of the cup be half a palm; and let it be on the side of the patient, in the location that is in the circumference, half of it; the hole should be small enough that only a needle can enter it.
Let it be made of bronze, copper, or tin, with thick edges, smooth, even, and polished so that it does not injure the limb due to its position on it.
And in the middle of it let there be a transverse bar of bronze or iron, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And now let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and thin people are different from the cupping glasses for youths and those with large bodies.
And the way to apply this cupping glass with fire to the limb is to extend the wick with a decent wood fire before a small wax candle, and place that on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is kindled around the part underneath so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be on the hole we have mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glasses and do not need to remove the finger and have the vapor escape through that hole, then the cupping glass will immediately be dissolved (i.e., the suction will cease).
And then ignite the wick according to its manner, and repeat this if you need to.
But in cupping, which is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar or hole, and is not administered unless it is filled with water, and is placed on the limb only.
And this is its form: And this cupping glass, the larger it is, the better it is, because it can receive a greater quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs in which the placement of cupping glasses is not possible, because of their smallness, such as the ear and gums and similar parts; or on a limb lacking flesh, such as a finger and nose and similar parts.
And the quality of using those is that you should find leeches in fresh waters of clean stagnant pools.
And then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and that no substance remains in their bellies; and then let the body be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and cupping; and then rub the limb of the patient until it reddens; and then let them be placed upon it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if it is possible to draw blood from the area with a cupping glass, it is more beneficial afterwards.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with warm water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged near the application of the leeches, and it is oozing, then you dip a cloth in cold water, and place that over it until the oozing is stopped.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, over it until the bleeding stops; or place halves of roasted beans on the area, and leave them until the beans stick to the area and the bleeding stops.
And it is necessary, if needed, to reiterate the application of leeches, when it is possible to change those.
And if the leeches are prevented from adhering, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood appears; and then apply them, because when they sense any blood they immediately attach.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle a little crushed aloe, or salt, or ash, on them, and they will fall off immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
O, son, know that foolish and common physicians boast of this chapter, those who never looked at the books of the Ancients nor read a letter of them.
And for this reason, this method among the learned in our land is neglected; and I have never forgotten this at any time; and I have not acquired this except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study of their understanding, until I extracted the science from them.
And I have applied myself to experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have described in this chapter all that my knowledge contains and on which my experience is based; since I have abbreviated it for you and delivered it from the burden of length; and I have abbreviated it to the point of brevity; and I have shown it to the point of clarity; and I have shown many forms of the instruments that are used in it, as is done by way of explanation, as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter one.
Summaries and collections on the subject of bone fractures, those which are necessary to preface before we begin the discussion of broken bones and the various types of each. Therefore, I will say in the preface to this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters to the understanding of which the craftsman is obligated at the beginning and to stand upon the truth of those things; that is, you and those who are ready to know or learn this noble art besides yourselves.
And therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall happens to someone, then you must attend to it at the beginning with phlebotomy or purging, or both together, if no cause prohibits it, such as weakness of virtue, or if the one to whom it happened is a child, or an old person in decrepitude, or it is a time of intense heat, or intense cold.
And furthermore, let him be content with a diet of cool herbs and the meat of birds and rabbits; and we prohibit rich meats and filling foods and all food that fills the veins with blood, until you are assured of a warm abscess, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his former regimen, with which he is accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the patient be fed nutritious foods, of much and thick and heavy nourishment in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads [presumably of grain], and peas, and cow bellies, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and things similar to those; and with this regimen, the coagulation of the fracture is made more lightly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in the first age [youth] and in the decrepit, it is not possible that they are continued and consolidated according to their nature because of the desiccation of their limbs.
However, nature adjusts and brings to the broken bone from every part some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and binds that until they are together conglutinated and tied together, until they come to the end of strength and firmness, like fire at the beginning, until no cause prohibits it from its work.
And for this reason it is necessary that you provide the patient with foods in which there is gravity, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other members are different among themselves.
And I come with a remembrance of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinct from the others.
Much diversity of fracture in itself. And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there is an arcuate fracture, besides those that happen in the ribs; and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the shaft and splinters and additions proceeding and not proceeding, just as is done in the quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And for each type there is an appropriate device or treatment in its restoration, as will be remembered distinctly in its place.
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: its crookedness, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and that of it when we compress it with the hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is it felt by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there is a crookedness or small fissure, in which case you should not move it with your hand and with complete compression; however, place on it the medicines of which mention is made near that moment which suits the place; and then it should be bound tightly with a thin bandage.
And know that if the place is arched and is not split in two, or other things which do not happen to it, such as being completely separated from its counterpart, then you must begin at your hour with its rectification before an abscess or burning happens to it.
And if a burning abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days until the abscess ceases; and then dress it according to whatever light and ingenious method you can.
And know that the restoration and healing of it is easier than of a bone in which a similar injury has occurred.
And then bind it as the recollection of it shall come.
But if the injury is in the bone, then extension of that broken member is necessary from two parts, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or with one cord and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the member in a dressing according to its natural shape, while the parts of the broken member are being extended, then you are forced to return those additions with ingenuity and in any way that you can to their places.
And take care, according to your strength, that neither pain nor injury befall the patient through your operation; and force yourself, according to your ability, to apply one bone to the other, its companion, according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that time you profitably touch that with your hand, and if you see here some different cause, rectify that and adjust that according to the amount of your skill.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish physicians do, who often cause a hot abscess through their operation, or cause contraction in the member, as we have seen this through their operation many times.
And the quality of the binding of the quieted member is that which I recount; so that you know that broken members are diversified in their smallness and in their greatness and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the penis and similar to those, then it is necessary that the wrapping be of very fine cloth.
But those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, it is necessary that in those there be a wide and firm wrapping, for the wide binding connects the large member and tightens it on all sides with even binding, in such a way that no blemish enters it.
Then begin, then your evacuation of equalization, to place a bandage suitable for this, over the place of that fracture with three or four wrappings according to what the member deserves; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then come with that to the highest part of the place of the fracture, and tighten less than you did your binding with which you have tightened the place of the fracture. And then extend yourself with a wrapping of the place of the fracture a little, and soften the binding little by little, until you take from the place its great part.
And then take another bandage and wrap that other time over the broken place with a wrapping, and then come with the wrappings to the lowest part of the fracture; and let your operation be in the tightening of the wrapping and in the softening of that according to what we have said in the first wrapping above.
And then place between the wrappings tow or linen cloth with which the tortuosity of the fracture is filled, if there is tortuosity in it; and if not, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over it, and then wrap over these bandages well-made splints immediately, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the place, and then place over it that which dries the abscess and removes the inflammation; and leave that for some days; and then you tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be half the width of a reed, and well-formed wisely; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over the fracture be a little thicker and wider than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to what is needed for the limb in size and smallness; and then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then tie over it with thread decently as we have said of tightening, and it is that your tightening be according to the place of the fracture more, and the more it is distant from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen by the operation of certain fools placing their threads of twisted ropes, and it is a great error, when the tightening loses equality.
And likewise, very fine threads are not convenient, because you will not achieve with them what you want by tightening, and it is not necessary that there be less than one finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is hurt by the ends of the splints near the binding in those places, then place under those parts linen or carded wool until that does not injure him.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption occurs in the bone, and I will come to a remembrance of that in the following chapter according to its singularity.
And know that not every broken limb is splinted on the first day; the reason for this is that, when the limb is large, you should not place splints on it except after about five or seven days, or more, according to your certainty of the occurrence of a hot abscess.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients used to restore, which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
Method of a common plaster, briefly described, for the restoration of fractures, suitable for many constitutions and especially for women and children, because it is of such a nature that neither heat nor cold overcomes it: and it is that you take some miller's dust, which is the pith of the flour that settles on the walls of the mill during the milling process.
And prepare it as it is, except that you sieve it with egg white, and make the application neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of a plaster restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of ladanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and of megrach (which is the root of wild pomegranate), of each one drachm; of myrrh and aloes, five drachms; of aloechil, twenty drachms; all should be crushed and sieved and mixed with aloe water or with egg white if the patient's constitution is hot; and then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores lightly broken bones, and benefits many people.
Another method for a plaster which benefits fractures and sprains: take megrach and the root of white mallow, ten drachms each; of myrrh and aloes, five drachms each; of acacia, six drachms; of Armenian bole, twenty drachms.
All should be crushed in a mortar with great care, and be sieved, and be mixed with water or with egg white and be applied.
A method for a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and if pain occurs in those and in fissures and fractures: let soiled wool be taken and submerged in sour wine and boiled oil and be placed on the area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of restoration, but rather of drawing out hot abscesses and expelling pain specifically.
Another method for a plaster that restores a broken bone: take fresh fig leaves and wild poppy leaves; pound everything and make a plaster from them.
Method for a brief plaster administered for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you desire resolution of the remaining abscess: take roots of marshmallow, of chamomile, and violet flowers, of barley flour, of all equal parts; let all be crushed and mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then prepare it with moistened coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method for a plaster more potent in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of the limb: take roots of marshmallow and flaxseed, and of althea, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of all equal parts; let all be crushed and mixed with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the repose of its color.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration except a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it be of medium consistency between thick and thin.
But in Gaul it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the substances which are drying with little heat are like myrrh, incense, and similar to those substances that we have mentioned.
Method for a plaster that benefits injury and pain: take  mint,  wax,  shaved human hair or bird down, and marshmallow, and salt, equal parts; let them be crushed and sifted and mixed, and let a plaster be made.
But the amount of time the bandage should remain and then be loosened, is that you should consider whether no pain or itching has occurred, and the bone is not broken or dislodged from its place; then you should not loosen it for many days. And if intense pain or itching or swelling occurs at the site, then immediately loosen or untie it at that moment, and remove the plaster from it.
And take linen cloth and a moist sea sponge, and submerge it in lukewarm water, and with that wash the area until that itching ceases and the pain is calmed; and then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place over it soiled wool submerged in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it for a night until you are sure of a hot abscess, and that the swelling of the limb has subsided, and its pain is reduced.
And then repeat that subtle constriction and small plaster and do not tighten it with your first constriction, but subtly with that until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, the redness, the pain, the swelling, and all the symptoms are now completely reduced, and you judge that the plaster and constriction are unnecessary, then you repeat that as you did at the beginning, similarly.
And if none of the conditions we have mentioned occurs in the limb, then do not untie it before three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days, according to what appears to you upon unbinding the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh over it.
And then adjust the constriction more than in your first constriction overall, and adjust it to the increase in the patient's flesh, according to the progress of their recovery.
But if you see the site of the fracture drying and thinning more than is appropriate, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore, bathe it with lukewarm water every time you untie it; and let this be done every three days; and loosen the constriction a little, and by this operation, nourishment will reach the limb, and it will be healed easily.
But what foolish restorers of a fractured limb do, when it is not set properly at the beginning, and is set crookedly, is an error according to their operation, and a great danger, and if this were proper, the Ancients would have greatly magnified it in their books and would have worked with it.
And I have not seen any of them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable that you do not do it.
Chapter Two: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow from a sword, and the blow crushes all the bone into the ciphac (likely referring to the spongy bone), which is beneath the bone, just as a wedge does in wood, and for this reason this type is called *ascial* (likely a term for a splintered or comminuted fracture); or the sword cuts some part of the bone, and cuts its flesh but does not penetrate to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called *levatio absoluta* (likely a term for an incomplete or superficial fracture).
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small, and of a fracture which is comminuted or abraded; and its cause is a blow with a blunt object or a fall onto a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, so that it approaches the periosteum which is beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone, as fine as a small hair, and for this reason this type is called a capillary fracture.
And there is a fracture which is caused by a fall or by a blow from a stone and the like, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter into the inner parts, and is made in a concave place, as happens to the plates of bronze when they receive a blow and part of them enters into the inner parts.
And this happens especially in the bodies of moist bones, such as the heads of children.
And in all these kinds of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will bring back all of this into its place (I will reduce it).
And we recognize all these kinds of fractures by probing them and by investigating them with probes, and by removing the corrupted flesh that is over them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is uncovered on the bone, and is wiped clean, and is examined with a probe, for the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider first the patient's symptoms.  And then, if you see by the symptoms that it signifies by manifest indication danger, such as vomiting of bile, and prostration, and loss of consciousness, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the eyes and redness of them, and similar symptoms in the more serious cases, undoubtedly it is not healing.
And if you see no unusual symptoms and you expect recovery, then you begin his treatment.
The reason is that if the wounded person has come to you at the beginning, as wounded, and this is in the winter days, then it is necessary that you study the setting of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to all circumstances.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you strive to set the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone is corrupted by the pus, and before the symptoms that we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain, and is with small pieces and attrition, then it is necessary that you incise the comminuted and atrophied part, as I shall explain to you.
And it is so that you shave the head of the wounded patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as much as possible according to the shape of the wound, and that it is light on the patient.
And if you encounter, during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you oppose it with what is necessary, and you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is settled and the bleeding is stopped; and then begin with the encircling of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow incisor, and this is its form:  And then apply another incisor, this one, a little wider than that one; and this is its form:
And then apply even wider incisors than the second.
And it is necessary that you have a number of different incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others, and let them be sharp at the ends, and be made of Indian iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisor so that you do not strike the head and injure it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you apply the incisors with the drills, which are called shallow drills.
And they are not so named except because those do not reach the limit of the skull bone near it, because the drill has a round end or edge on that which is below its sharp head, similar to a rim or small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each of which suits the thickness of each bone, until you have at hand for all parts of the skull a drill whose point is sharp, the length and shortness being according to the thickness of that part of the skull.
And this is the manner of three types of drills: The method of perforating the circumference of the broken bone is that you place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone is pierced; then move the drill to another place, and place them so that the space between each hole is the size of the radius, or thereabouts.
And then incise with incisors that which is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as you can in order to lighten it, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with some other means by certain instruments which you will have prepared for this, such as fine pincers and forceps.
And it is necessary that you take great care that the incisor does not touch anything of the brain.
And so when you have encircled the bone by perforating it, and it is separated from the brain if it is continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you scrape and smooth its roughness with another instrument similar to an incisor, but however it must be thinner and more delicate than the other incisors.
And if any part of the small and splintered bones remains, then take that away easily with whatever instrument you have; and then cure the wound with liniments and ointments which we have mentioned.
And another method of operation is easier, far from fear, which Galen described and praised highly; and this is his discourse: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin uncovering the part of the bone at the place where the fracture is most violent and most evident, until when you have uncovered that part and have placed beneath it the extremity of that slow and gentle incisor of which this is the form:
And it is so that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is divided into two edges lengthwise, like a wedge; so that the lenticular part is fitted to the brain with its face, the sharp edge cutting into the bone.
And then strike the incisor on one side with a small mallet until you incise all the bone easily as it is revolved, and you are sure of the pannicle that no accident happens to it in any way, even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and even if he were sleeping.
Then, if any part remains attached to the bone of the pannicle in any place of the bone, incise that with the extremity of that lenticular incisor, and remove that easily, for it is removed without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the pannicle, and what is separated or removed from the bone is its surface, and there remain roughness and small splinters, then it is necessary to scrape away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps for which you have prepared several, of different sizes, so that it is possible for you to use the most suitable and correct of these according to what you are doing in your operation on that broken bone and its form.
And it is necessary that you begin your rasping of the bone with the widest of those rasps, and then use progressively the finer ones, and do not cease to do this continuously until you arrive at using the finest and most subtle of all those.
But in each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer care according to what you are doing for their rectification.
And it is a matter which is not hidden from all those who have studied that art. And I stand by what we have shown written above regarding large fractures.
And if the panniculus (skin and subcutaneous tissue) is retracted when you reduce the bone, then it is necessary that you take linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse that in rose oil, and place that over the opening of the wound; and then take another doubled or tripled cloth and immerse it in wine and rose oil and place that over the thin cloth, and do this as lightly as you can, so as not to weigh down the fractured bone; and then apply a linen bandage over that, and do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds the wound only.
And leave it with this dressing for a day or two until you are sure of a hot abscess; and then untie it and then administer medicines with which to draw out superfluous moisture, such as flaxseed, barley flour, and powder of lentils and birthwort, and similar things.
And it is so that you make a powder of these medicines to be dusted over the wound, once they are dry; and universally, apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to bite (i.e., cause further irritation).
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment remains clean; and let there be no filth of ointment or other matter; nor allow virulence to accumulate there; for when it is applied to the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts and poisons it, and this evil causes great harm to the patient.
And it happens in a fracture of the head, as the bone is cleansed from it, and especially when its treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore consider then, if the patient suffers the accidents that we have described, know that it is pernicious without any doubt.
And if there is blackness not from the medicine that is placed on top, but it is by virtue of that medicine that causes that blackness, then it is necessary that you take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these moisten a cloth, and then place that on the fracture; and then treat it with the appropriate healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two parts joined together, or one of these sometimes, because in it there are two bones, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and there is only abrasion, crookedness and sinuosity to it.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then it is necessary that you insert your little finger into their nose, and that you align that fracture inside with your index finger, and with the thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and take care that you do not cause pain to the patient through your operation.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it must be aligned with the end of a rod which has a small thickening.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same for both.
And take care to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the seventh or tenth day you should treat the resulting inflammation with cautery.
And then insert into the end of the nose a small roll of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or two small wedges, if the fracture is in two parts, and let the size of the wedge be such as to fill the opening in the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the wedges should be soaked in pitch and changed every day, but I have not seen this done; rather it is necessary that the roll be soaked in egg white mixed with mill dust; and then leave the wedge there until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a wedge, a goose feather, wrapped in linen cloth, is inserted into the nose; and this is especially useful when the nasal fracture is more severe and the patient's breathing is not obstructed.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, use wedges.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, apply a plaster to the nose with cerate or cotton soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to plaster it externally with sesame flour and boiled lentil powder mixed with egg white; and then place linen cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then it is necessary to incise over them, and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then sew the incision, and treat it with what promotes granulation and consolidation, and with plasters suitable for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then it is necessary to treat it with wedges and administer lead canulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external, and is not broken in two and is bent inwards at the parts, then its diagnosis is easy.
And it is necessary then, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and push with that the projection of the fracture inwards, easily towards the outer parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; thus decently equalize it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from two sides according to straightness until its equalization is possible.
And if there is concussion of teeth with disruption, then bind those which you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or silk; and then place over the broken jaw a cerate, and then place over it a doubled cloth, and place over the cloth a suitably made large compress, or a piece of linen equal to the length of the jaw; and then bind that above as the binding appears suitable to you and as the application requires, until it is healed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food of sorbitious lions.  (Note: "sorbicios leons" is likely a corruption or unusual phrasing.  It may refer to easily digestible foods, or a specific type of food mentioned in other medieval texts - further research into medieval culinary terms would be needed for a definitive translation.)
And if you estimate that the form is already altered in some way, then you should consider and unbind it on the third day; and then rectify that which is already altered and plaster it with powdered barley, with egg white, or with starch, after you have removed the cerate, and place wool over the plaster.
And then, since this plaster is attached and adjusted to it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it; do not unbind it until it is healed and the fracture is firm; for many times this fracture is fixed in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and that abscess is softened.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of the bone is separated or splintered; then you should carefully remove those splinters with whatever is suitable for that in removing them from the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should enlarge it with a fleam according to your need.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you should sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that is suitable for it and heals it, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Often the clavicle is broken in the front part above the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore; or it is such that splinters are present, and it is difficult to restore; or it is such that the fracture is accompanied by a wound.
And the operation in this case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the broken clavicle and supports it, and the other extends the neck towards that side.
Then bind the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correctly formed, and there is neither prominence nor concavity.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, its size according to your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the pad with your hand until you bind the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it is stuck to the sternum, then the patient should lie supine on their neck, and you should place a moderately sized cushion on the muscle.
And the assistant presses the muscle downwards until the bone of the clavicle which is above the sternum is elevated, and then you straighten the fracture and bind it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are loose, then you should make an incision over it and remove those splinters easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you should carefully incise it with one of the scalpels which you have prepared for this, after having placed under the clavicle the instrument that serves as a splint; and this instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size or smallness of the bone.
But its length should be as long as is possible for you during the operation; it should have two ends like this, one end wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is a risk of a hot abscess, then you should join the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in sharp vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it on top.
And ensure that the splint is not moved from over the broken bone.  Therefore, you must see the patient each day, because when the ligament is softened or you see that the splint has shifted, then you must correct this and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's pillow under his neck, and place a small cushion under his scrotum at night beneath the pillow. This ensures that, with this elevation, the help (support) is given to that part, and thus the fracture of the clavicle (furcula) is elevated by the muscle; or his arm should be tied to his neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless an abscess or swelling occurs at the site, before twelve days have passed.
Then renew the plaster if you see the need, and adjust the bandage, and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the clavicle (furcula) is healed and consolidated.
And it is strengthened and healed, according to the case, in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken at its lateral side, and not shattered, but only the margins are broken.
And so when the location of the break — and it is broken in the middle of the location — is unknown except by touch, then, according to the nature of the fracture, forcefully prepare it and reduce it to its natural form in every way possible to you.
Then place on the area powdered finely ground albumen of an egg and lion's mane; then place a dusting of doubled cloth; then place over it a thin linen bandage, according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or a little more.
But if the bandage shows a concavity in any part of the shoulder blade, then you should fill that concavity with lion's mane, and then tighten the bandage evenly.
And then bind it above with a long bandage with a decent constriction and firmness of the bandage until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the binding each day; and every time it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it, and adjust the bandage if it has moved from its place.
And have the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And so when that number is complete, then you unbind it, and you should know for certain that it is from the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if it proceeds from the location of the collarbone and extends below the clavicle, then you incise over it, and remove it, and proceed as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle regarding the cauterization of an abscess if it should occur here.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this heals slowly; but its extremities heal much more quickly, even if they are crushed and comminuted.
And among the accidents when the middle of the foot is broken, it inclines towards the inner parts and causes great pain, difficulty in breathing, cough, strong fever, and vomiting of blood; and it is evident in the cavity of the broken bone and is not hidden from sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient should be laid supine on his back, with a cushion placed between his shoulders; then press gently on his muscles, and adjust the bones with the hands on both sides, subtly working on it and manipulating it in every way possible and appropriate, until the bone's shape is restored as it should be.
Then place a plaster and lint over it, and place over this a splint of thin willow or lime wood, or similar in lightness, after wrapping it in cloth; then carefully bind this over the broken bone so that it is not loose; and bind it with a circular bandage to the back sometimes, and tighten it with appropriate firmness; and then check the bandage frequently, and tighten it when it becomes loose.
And if necessity demands loosening of the bandage because of corrosion or itching, or pain, or an abscess that might occur at the site, then carefully unbind it, remove the plaster and correct what has happened using appropriate treatment; and then reapply the plaster, if the situation allows; and adjust the firmness until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs only happens in the thicker parts, which are two; and in their extremities towards the front,  destruction does not occur because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense of its examination by touch.
And the restoration should be thus: equalize the fracture with your fingers as much as possible, until it is aligned as it should be, and then plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if it needs that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is towards the inner parts, then the patient suffers vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that occurs in one who has pleurisy, and this is because the bone pierces the membrane; and it causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the Ancients were ingenious in treating this with many devices.
And of them it is said: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that cause inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we avoid this so that it does not lightly cause a hot abscess if it does not already exist; and if it already exists, then it helps with that.
And some of them say that a cupping glass should be placed on top and that it should draw with strength; and this seems reasonable, but it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the place because of weakness.
And some of them say: the place should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is full, so that the bandage is even as it envelops that area according to its roundness; and then the patient should be treated with a treatment for pleurisy, with diet and medicine.
And if a violent cause constricts the patient, which he cannot bear, and the bone pierces the skin with a lacerating puncture, threatening the patient, then it is necessary to cut open the area, uncover the broken rib, and then place on it an instrument that preserves the *ciphác* (likely referring to a specific surgical instrument or technique, the exact meaning lost to time), the remembrance of which is mentioned before, and that we incise the bone easily and extract it.
And then we join the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and we treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess forms inside, then you should take and pour powders into rose oil and place it on the area, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let him be supine on the side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Chapter 9: Of the restoration of a fracture of the spine and of the neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and it is slow to heal, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider this: if you see his hands already softened, numb, and dead, and he cannot move, extend, or clench them, and when you constrict them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he will not be healed, and in most cases it is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels constriction with the fingers and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the same thing in the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if it will heal or not, then observe his feet.
Then, if you see that they are already softened, the same thing has happened to them as happened to the hands, and when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and defecation without his will, and sometimes he lies on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then know that it is mortal, and therefore do not work on his cure.
But if none of these things occur, the cause is better and milder.
And his cure is that you should apply to the inflamed area a hot poultice placed on the affected vertebra, with plain or rose-scented oil, or with well-beaten egg whites; apply this several times a day until the hot abscess is sedated.
And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it tightly; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where he feels pain until it is healed.
And if it happens that because of the attrition of the bone, splinters or something else has become separated from it, then it is necessary that you make an incision on the wound and remove that bone; and then join the two lips of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment until it is healed.
But if the posterior part of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the femur, then you must insert the index finger of your left hand into the rectum, and realign the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by counter-traction; and then apply a plaster and bandage, if you need them, and then bind it tightly.
And if you feel broken splinters in it, then you should make an incision there, and remove them, and treat the wound with the treatment previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the hip.
The hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are broken, then their fracture is only a separation at the extremities of those bones, and they are split lengthwise and incline inwards; and the patient experiences pain and throbbing in the area, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you bind that according to what is at your disposal for equal binding, until it is similar to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or inclines downwards, then you make the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared by you. And then when you have bound it, place a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or reed over that, and tighten it with a bandage over which you do not fear puncture of the fracture nor displacement of the splint.
And bind the concavity with something that fills it until it takes the bandage equally, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is settled, and place on it that which settles it as is prescribed; and then return to its restoration, and bind it as is necessary.
And if splinters occur in the bone or are comminuted, then you remove them.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the side and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a piece of wood of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at the two extremities, and then let it be suspended from a raised place.
And place the patient on a seat; and then place his broken arm on the wood until it reaches his wrist, in the middle of which the curve of the wood should be attached.
And then suspend some heavy object from it, or the assistant stretches it downwards; and then the physician joins the fracture with his hands until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other method is that the patient lies supine on his back and suspend the hand from his neck with a bandage; and then command two assistants that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the part below; and each of them stretch it to his side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten it above the fracture with a bandage and above that with another bandage, and each of the two assistants extend to their respective sides.
And so if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the wrist and the other below the fracture near the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that place and on that elbow.
Swathe that with ease and without trouble, and then when it is swathed as necessary and is applied with good application, then tighten it unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the bandaging until the seventh day, and place on it clean wool soaked in sharp wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten that.
And the manner of tightening it is that you place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm over that support, and place your open hand over its muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm over the support so that the arm is in a stellar position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is so that you place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten over the splints with the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is so that your bandage over the location of the fracture should be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you act.
And if you fear a purulent abscess, then you leave the bandage and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then consider the bandage in all parts for three days so that itching or inflammation does not occur in the area; or it is forbidden for food to reach the limb due to the tightness of the bandage; and therefore rectify all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that none of these things will happen, then do not untie the bandage until three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their side, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place over the injured area a poultice full of well-carded wool, and check every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is not softened; if so, rectify it all with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until strength begins to return; and then it is fitting that you give him thicker food.
For it is customary for the injured area and his leg to be bound for 40 days; and then it is fitting that they be unbound and a bath be administered, and it should be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is badly crushed, then you should not unbind the bandage and splints for 10 days or for two months.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the little finger, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the little finger, on the lower part.
And very easily the larger bone is broken alone, or the smaller one, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small bone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the bone is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller bone above, then it is convenient for the physician, during its restoration, to apply slight extension with ease until it is aligned.
But if the large bone is what is broken, then it is necessary to apply a more forceful extension.
And if both bones are broken together, then you must apply the traction much more strongly.
And you must position your hand during its restoration and extension stretched out on a cushion, and the wrist of your hand should be higher than all the other fingers, and the thumb should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be lying on their back on a square cushion.
And there should be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer discomfort.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should manipulate the bone until he reduces it to the best possible position.
And if the bone fracture is jagged, then you must forcefully reduce all the pieces to their place with your skill.
And if a separated, jagged piece appears that protrudes, and there is no hope of restoring it, then you should saw it off and remove it according to the method we described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already devoted a separate chapter to this, and therefore take care of that.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your reduction, then you should line a cloth with a cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and let it be of medium consistency between thick and thin, and bind the cloth over it with a subtle ligature until the abscess subsides; and then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from powdered barley flour with egg white, and then apply stars; and let the star which is placed over the fracture be more spread out and stronger.
And know that the number of stars for the arm is six in the greatest arrangements, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
And then apply your binding over the fracture site more firmly and more tightly, and if the binding is closer to the upper parts of the fracture or to the lower parts of it, apply the binding a little more loosely, as was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp cloths, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is bound should be of a consistency midway between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any cause arises for which rectification is necessary, such as itching which occurs in the limb, then you must bathe the limb with warm water until that itching ceases.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the fracture is now softened and the bone is reset and similar to the others, then correct all this with your skill.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb by excessive tightness of the bandage, for then you must loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to loosen [the bandage] until approximately twenty days or thereabouts.
And then let the patient's hand be suspended by its own agitated movement, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or even quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the disposition of the complexions and the strength of the healing power.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures rarely happen in the wrist and finger joints; instead, attrition [or wearing down] occurs very often.
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then the patient should lie down, and a cushion should be placed in front of him according to his size, and then he should place his hand on that cushion.
Then the assistant extends the broken bones, and the physician joins them until they are united with a good connection.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless a hot abscess occurs; and then apply a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold it with his fractured palm; then it should be tightly bound with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather with cloth in which it is softened, so that the leather may be applied to the palm; and tighten it as needed.
But if the fracture is on the outer parts, then it is necessary that you place a bandage above and another bandage below on the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, as if the hand were bent at the wrist between the fingers with ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be straightened as needed and then bound to the palm; and if you wish, apply a small, straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index finger, or in the ring finger, or in the little finger, then it should be straightened and bound to the finger next to it; or all should be bound together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and in addition to that, the disposition of a hot abscess and oppose—with your operation—what is necessary when any of these conditions occur, according to the repeated description of it.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and this is revealed by the sensation, depending on whether it is broken in the front or the back part.
And its restoration is done in this way: a bandage is tightened above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on his stomach and face.
And then the physician extends the entire bandage to its proper place according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the hip joint, then let the bandage be close to the knee on the side of the kneecap, so that the extension is upward, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then let the bandage be placed near the knee, and let the extension be towards the lower parts.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both his hands until he reduces it to its natural form and the bone is joined by a good conjunction; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then you leave it for a few days until the hot abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made thus: you should wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and double it two or three times, and remove the excess; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the hip joint.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the thigh and the leg near the knee on the lower parts, and let the ends of the cloth come from the upper part of both sides, and then roll over the leg and thigh what is superfluous of the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one splint on the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is firm.
And then begin with a tight bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four wraps, and the further you get from the fracture site, the looser, lighter, and softer your bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the cloth to the lower parts until it reaches the ankle, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not displaced from its place.
And then leave the bandage on, provided that it does not cause the limb to become corroded, or develop a hot abscess or swelling, or anything similar; and if any of these things occur, then carefully loosen it, and correct whatever has occurred, just as we have said many times before.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone which protrudes, then you must set it as best as possible; and if not, bind it and keep it in place, and treat it with the remedies previously mentioned until it is healed.
And sometimes this thigh fracture is tightened beyond what is achieved by the application of splints, as we have described for the treatment of the arm.
However, in the restoration of the limb made in this manner, the patient does not experience lameness; and if it is restored alone, without the application of support to the other leg, then it is necessary that he will always limp on that side.
And know that the thigh is bound for forty days, or a little more or a little less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but often causes considerable attrition (or wearing away/damage).
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and all of this is accompanied by pain.
And its restoration is such that what is broken of its parts is joined together with said splints until it is united and joined as much as possible by equalization, ease, and fabrication (or crafting).
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you have one; and bind it above with a suitable bandage.
And then consider all the preparations that we have recommended in other fractures, such as a hot poultice and similar things, so that any accident is countered with what is suitable for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
On the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is large and is called by the name of the leg; and the other is thin, and is called the fibula.
And whatever kinds of fractures happen to these two bones of the leg also happen to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is one and the same.
And if the two bones are broken together, the leg is distorted in all its parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is distorted in the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is one of those things that are not easily seen by you.
Then administer extension, setting, and ligation of the splints according to that same method.
However, if the leg fracture is large, having many splinters, the extension should be less and gentler, and relieve it with your skill.
And in the leg, there is some cause for operating on the arm, and it is when you align the splints and have completed all your work, then you will take pieces of pine wood which are placed under the bandages that are placed on the soles, and they are carefully made from palm branches; choose those of small thickness and not the smallest, and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee to the foot.
Then wrap each cloth in two layers according to its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side, from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then tie the two splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle, for with this structure the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and it remains in a good position.
And a small wooden splint has already been prepared according to the length of the leg; and let the leg be placed on it so that it is kept from movement.
And it is especially necessary that you do this in a fracture, and when there is a wound properly so called.
And then examine the leg every day and study it carefully, and watch for abscesses and inflammation or other similar conditions; and if any of these occur, then treat it as necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the talus and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the whole talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot do occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes rarely occur, and only attrition in the case of a predisposition.
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and sometimes these bones are raised up above each other, then the patient should place his foot on the ground prepared as before.
Then place one foot on that which is elevated of those bones, and then press, and force those bones until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and lint over it, and place under the sole of the foot a small board to which two straps are attached.
Then tighten that with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other necessary materials.
And when three or four days have passed, then untie the bandage, for you will find the bones set, whether they were broken or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if some are said to be broken, then restore those, and set them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the broken bones of the hand. Then apply a splint to the broken finger according to its length, and let it be a little longer; and place under the foot that board which I have mentioned to you, and tighten it with a proper bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in linen cloth, and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot, and let it rest on one of the ends of the board across the instep so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you not forget to consider all the things which I have told you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the hip of the woman is broken, then make that part lie down flat, and gently incline it slightly towards the back, and let it be held from behind.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it should be made into a ball in her hip; and then the woman should be held, and her back raised little by little, because all this is useless until the part of the hip is in front in the form of a sphere and the broken bone returns.
And then place powders on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and then she urinates, and reposition and return it according to the method by which you positioned it at the beginning, and then return to the first powder.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and attach a small cane tube to its opening; and introduce the whole bladder into her hip, and then inflate it through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, for this will help the fracture to heal.
And then it should be filled with cotton and left for some days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and healing what we have recommended for the hip bone.
And that which is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures which seldom occur, especially for one who studies and uses and understands this my book well; for this fracture signifies to you in the greatest dispositions the correct way in its restoration and its binding, and therefore understand well.
But in the case of a man's penis when it is broken, take a hen's gizzard and place the penis in it; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and then let it be left for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of bone fractures when they are accompanied by a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar to those, that you carefully attend to and bleed him immediately if the conditions for bloodletting testify to this as we have said above.
And if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you attend to its retention so that you pulverize crushed sage over it, if no other cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you should leave its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, uncovered, then it is necessary that you carefully reduce it and align it with your hands easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length is seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the size of the wound; and for this, it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of them, according to the quantity needed for this in the treatment of every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it be of little thickness so that it is not bent by the compression on it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp end which has a margin at its extremities; and let the top of it be of increasing thickness, and from its middle to the sides.
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Therefore it is necessary that its sharp end be placed raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And push that in at once until the bone returns, and let it be equalized with some equalization/adjustment.
And then strive to equalize the ends of the fracture between them, and if the broken end of the bone is thin, and does not take the instrument well, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have recounted, then incise it with whatever is suitable from the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whichever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds vehement and persistent pain, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do what you consider will bring great benefit to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound cerate or anything else containing oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption there.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you incise the opening with forceps and use dressings according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and caused death or a corrosive or suppurating illness, that is, gangrene, in their patients.
