And if your constriction is light, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have recounted, and you find pain in the place, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a dressing of hard cloths instead of stitches and bind it tightly with these.
And when, after a day or two, the wound begins to suppurate, then you remove the cloth which you had placed upon it with wine, and then administer poultices, ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is necessary for you to unbind the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and closed, and it is necessary that the limb be positioned in such a way that the pus flows downwards easily.
If many days pass and the wound is not consolidated nor is the pus discharged from it, then know that there is stagnation of humors, wherefore it is necessary to probe the wound with a probe. And of those collections of pus, those which are separated, remove and extract them; those which are not separated and compress the member and cause pain, it is necessary for you to incise them, as far as is possible for you.
And if a worm or maggot infests the wound — that is, a stiomenios, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction — then it is necessary that you treat each of these with what is appropriate for it, the treatment of which is described earlier in its chapter.
And this is what my discourse should address, and you should keep in mind: when a large bone is fractured, apply a dressing to the limb, just as to a fractured thigh bone or similar large bones, and do not attempt traction or extraction, for in many instances death results from this; leave it until it suppurates, for it will fall off naturally in about 20 or 30 days; and then treat the wound if you deem it necessary, but if not, leave it be.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20: On the treatment of a swelling that occurs at the site of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of a healing fracture, especially near the joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb; and it quickly prevents the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and apply medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, incense, myrrh, cerussa (white lead), and similar substances;  take some or all of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or egg white, or sour wine, and apply this to the swelling on a piece of linen, and bind it firmly.
And leave the bandage tight for many days; then unbind it and apply another, until the swelling is reduced; or apply a thin lead plate, because lead has the property of reducing anything swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already become hardened and constricted and necessity compels its removal carefully, then incise it in its highest part, and excise the excess and prominent part, or scrape it with a sharp instrument until it is reduced; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Chapter 21. Of the restoration of a fracture when it is set, and the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its natural state.
When a bone fracture is set, and afterward the limb remains thin and weak, this happens for many reasons: one of which is the excessive loosening of the ligaments and their binding not as it should be; or by excessive tightening of the bandages so that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb; or by an excessive amount of superfluous embrocation; or by excessive movement not at the proper time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and by their weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and to make his heart cheerful so that his blood is increased; and administer baths; and instill joy and mirth in him.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws much nourishment to it, or continuous embrocation with warm water, until nourishment flows and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Chapter 22. Of the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly and the proper operation is prevented.
When, in a limb already set, there is a recurrence of crookedness and swelling at the fractured bone and a knot, and because of this there is deformity of the limb, but nevertheless the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not heed the words of anyone who claims that limb is newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land; and this operation is very reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is death.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed with warm water in which are boiled softening herbs, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar things; and an emollient plaster should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be made and mixed with white clay or with olive oil, and applied as a plaster; or fatty figs should be crushed with pigeon droppings, and similar things from among the medicines called consolidating unguents.
And sometimes it is resolved with frequent, light friction with moistened hands, and movement of the limb in every part should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become severe and hardened, and it becomes necessary to treat it with iron, then the uppermost part should be sawn off and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which superfluously swells from the knotting of the bone itself should be incised with fine scalpels, and relief should be provided with care and diligence.
Chapter 23.
Concerning discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints from their place, whereby movement is prevented, and the shape of the limb is distorted, causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to immediately strive that it be reduced, and not delay this reduction, for if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and very quickly it becomes difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And for this reason, it should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, because many times it causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, then you should attend to the phlebotomy of the patient, and then let him rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated in such a way that its healing will bring it back to its proper place.
And I have now ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what preceded on fracture; from the uppermost part of the body down to the lowermost part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, it will not be long; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are moved from their places by slight displacement, and are somewhat softened; or they are dislocated by complete and total dislocation, until they are softened at the joint, to the point that saliva flows from the patient and they cannot retain it nor close their mouth, and their tongue is entangled in their speech.
But when the dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and total, then a reduction must be administered promptly, and not delayed at all.
And let the assistant hold the patient's head, and let the physician insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or let him insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and let the other fingers of his hands be outside with which he will pull; and he commands the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to be moved from each side; and the physician pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer a vaporization with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And then, when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on it some linen cloths with cerate, and it should be made of wax and rose oil; and then it should be bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head should be supported between two pillows, so that it does not lean to the right or left; and do not force them to chew anything; beforehand, their food should be finely chopped, until, when the pains are lessened and the jaws are healed, then let them eat what they wish.
And this should be administered with care, and do not exert effort on opening the mouth during eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is healed and mended.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and persistent thirst occur, and very quickly the patient's belly is bloated, and very quickly they vomit pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and many times he who experiences this dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inward from its articulation with the sternum; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then constrict the area with your palm with firm pressure, for it will return.
And then apply a plaster and powders to that area and bind it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is next to the hip and is continuous with it, is not dislocated in any way; then it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as is appropriate for you.
And then apply a plaster, and powders, and a bandage, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated in only three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated from the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated in the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and protrudes outwards in the lower parts below the armpit, and especially in those who have little flesh, it is easily dislocated; but in those who have much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And it quickly happens to some men through a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed by a hot abscess, and it is thought to be dislocated because of this.
Then it is necessary that you explore this until you are certain about it, and then you proceed to its cure.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the armpit; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a manifest difference between them; and you find a concavity at the head of the muscle and below the armpit near the touch of the head of the muscle, like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all movements.
If it is dislocated near the feet or above, then you find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises his hand to the upper parts; and then places his hands under his armpit, and raises the joint strongly to its place, and the minister raises his hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it below because it returns lightly.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and from the beginning that the dislocation happened, many days have passed, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and a softening and relaxing embrocation be administered, such as boiled roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and be applied.
And then the patient should be supine on his back and a wool ball, of a consistency between soft and firm, placed under his armpit. Then the physician places his heel on the ball and elevates the head of the muscle forcefully, and pulls the patient's arm so that it is not pulled downwards. Another assistant holds the patient's head to prevent it from being pulled downwards, for then it quickly returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method:  have a man present who is taller than the patient, standing on that side, and insert his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raise the patient's armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. Another assistant pulls the patient's arm from below his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him to weigh him down, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced easily in another way: a long, round-topped pole, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, is fixed in the ground. Then, soft cloths are placed on top of the pole, and the patient stands along the length of the pole. Then his arm is extended downwards on the opposite side; and his body is forcefully extended on the other side, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if reduction is difficult by all the methods we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a stick two cubits long and four fingers wide,  and its thickness should be reduced at the end that goes under the armpit in this way:
And then soft cloths should be tied over the rounded end so that the stick does not injure the patient.
Then it should be placed under the armpit of the patient, and the arm or hand should be extended over the stick down to the lower parts, and the stick should be tied to the armpit, arm, and hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a step of a ladder according to its width, and the hand should be extended downwards, and the rest of the body should be suspended on the other side, for the joint will enter immediately.
And when the reduction is complete in whatever way possible to you, then you should place under the armpit a ball of wool of sufficient size; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered elm leaves and egg white over the entire muscle, so that it is wrapped around it, and then tighten it under the armpit with a suitable bandage; and the bandage should be wrapped over the plaster, and the patient's hand should be attached to their neck, and it should be left, and the patient's hand should not be moved for seven days.
And the patient's diet should be light until the limb is strengthened, for this makes healing easier.
Then the diet should be increased gradually after seven or five days and some movement should be exercised; and so if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity affecting it or some other cause, then cautery with three punctures should be administered in that place, as previously described in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and the bandage are reapplied several times, and it is not firm and hangs and cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that the nerves of it, which are at the head of the muscle, are severed or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
For the dislocation that occurs between the foot and the ankle or the parts behind, reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And let another bandage be applied to that [area], and treat it until it is healed.
And if during healing heaviness occurs in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths many times until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its former natural state.
Chapter 27.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and especially from the front to the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is evident to the sight or to the touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated, and when you align the dislocated elbow with its socket, this is manifestly declared to you, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its muscle with it.
And it is necessary that you take care, and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess develops, for if a hot abscess develops, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed at all; and especially when it is in the back parts, for it is the worst of all other kinds of dislocations, and of greater pain, and often causes death with it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that his hand be extended with two hands, and his arm be expanded, and the physician's hands be on the elbow and below it, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then return it in such a way that you bend the hand with your hand, until you strike the elbow with the root of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous and very strong extension.
And it is necessary that two assistants extend the arm, and that two other assistants hold it firmly so that it is not moved during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, after it has been wrapped with a long, folded cloth over his hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this will aid in lubricating the joint with ease; and then push the joint with forceful pressure until it returns to its place.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that he be placed on a plaster which has styptic and drying properties with egg white, and it should be tightly bandaged with a descending compression bandage, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left like that for some days.
And afterwards it should be unbound; and then if the joint is set in its place, then you untie his bandage and leave it thus.
And if you see the joint is not well tightened, then you renew the plaster and bandage, and leave it like that for some days until it is tightened; and then unbind it.
But if the patient experiences pain after the reduction of the joint, and his condition is aggravated by movement, then administer moist applications in a bath and gentle friction, and compression, until he is relieved; or place on the joint soothing herbs, that is, something very oily; and then it should be bandaged, and left on the bone day and night. And then remove that, and put him in a bath; and when he is sufficiently warm, then you rub the joint with a temperate friction, and then again apply soothing herbs, that is, very oily rosemary, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the application of a bath, until it is softened.
And if you wish, place on it a warm, moist cow's bladder and bind it tightly; and do this several times, for it is moist and helps restore it to its proper condition.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated wrist, that is, of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated wrist is sometimes easily reduced, unlike other joints. However, speed is necessary in its reduction at the time it is dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed or a hot abscess develops there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table, and the assistant extends the patient's hand, and the physician places his hand or palm on the prominence of the joint, and pulls it until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already turned towards the belly of the hand. Then the patient places the back of his hand on a table during extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be inwards on the table, so that the physician's hand falls on that prominence of the joint.
And so if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a poultice that softens the inflammation and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise you will not be able to succeed in its reduction once days have passed.
However, if the joint remains in its twisted position, and the patient feels no pain, only that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything, then you should know that the nerves are pinched or crushed, and then there is no remedy except to bind it with a cautery near the injury, and quickly give it something to hold.
Therefore, when the limb is reduced, then apply the poultice we have described, and let it be left and bound for five days; then let it be unbound and left, and let his hand be exercised.
But if its movement is impeded and some heaviness occurs in it, then soothe it with warm water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes fingers are dislocated in every part.
And when one of those fingers is dislocated towards the back of the palm or the belly of the hand, then extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your own hands, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is straight and set in its place that day.
And when it is night, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for some days until it is tight/firm.
And do similarly if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform this first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are dislodged from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death threatens the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excretion is expelled involuntarily, and they cannot retain it; and often some of their limbs are paralyzed, or their two legs, or their two arms, or one of these; or a vertebra is dislocated, because often it is dislocated and often the displacement is in four parts.
Therefore, that is already dislocated to the back parts and is called yellowness (jaundice/possibly referring to a discoloration or symptom).
But the cure for it is that then you consider if the yellowness (jaundice/discoloration) comes from a fever, for then in that case there is no cure nor healing at all.
But in that which comes from a fall or from a blow or similar to those, the Ancients already described the methods of cure with long discourse, of which the most returns with profit; but I have already abbreviated that, of which the little excuses the excess of it, for my proximity to my intention of explanation, with which I have expounded and formed this from various instruments of that with which I will show this and expound that.
And of those, only that which happens in the two properly is cured with what I have recounted.
And it is that the patient should be extended on his face on a fairly level bed near a wall, and a soft mat should be placed under him so that his feet are not hurt.
And then a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the earth at the head of the bed near his head, and another pole near his feet at the other end of the bed.
And the attendant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another attendant should hold the other according to that method.
Then wrap a cloth around the patient's head and tie it firmly with strong linen; and extend the end of the linen to the pole which is near his head, and tie it to him; and then tighten another linen around his hips and thighs, and around his legs; and then adjust all the linen and tie them to the other pole which is near his feet.
And then each attendant should extend the linen to the part of the pole, and the two poles and the linen should not be removed from their places where they are fixed, but however, they are not fixed as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with firmness until it returns, or place a board on it, and then adjust his feet on the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three braccia, and make a hole in the wall which we have said is near the patient, into which the end of the board fits. Then place half of the board on the spine, and the physician place his feet on the other end, and press firmly until the spine is reduced and returns to its place.
And if you wish, use a lathe which is turned by hand; and you should place in the ground near the patient's head at the back of the buttocks two poles, the length of each being one braccia, and near their head at the back of the buttocks, similarly two poles; and the distance between each pole should be one palm, and in each pole there is a hole through which the lathe passes, and each should be firmly fixed in the ground so that it is not moved.
And let there be a round pole on which the lathe is placed, and let it be revolved in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end is a hole into which the pole is fitted, the length of which is one palm including that which revolves; and in the other two poles is something similar to that.
Then tighten the ligatures that you have tied to the patient's feet in the hole which is near their head, and the ligatures that you have tied to their thighs in the lathe which is near their feet.
And then near each lathe let a servant stand who turns the lathe with his hand, and the physician adjusts the vertebra as we have said.
And then, once the vertebra is returned and the place is adjusted, then you should apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a tablet of wood whose width should be three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length should be enough to cover the area of the hump, and the healthy part of the spine.
And then it should be bound with the ligature which we have mentioned, and nourish the patient with light food until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of the healing, then medicines should be administered that soften and smooth, with the administration of the tablet for a long time, which we have recommended; and a lead plate is then applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the rear of the spinal vertebrae, and is caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already swells, so that no attempt should be made to treat it with this medicine, because it will very quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint suffer only dislocation, and not what happens to other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its dislocation inwards is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without pain.  And it is the place where the pain is inflamed with a hot abscess, because the head of the femur has already arrived there.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens outwards, is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but nevertheless cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk he cannot do so forwards; and his urine is retained and his groin is inflamed, and he walks on his heel.
And a sign of dislocation happening backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the hip, and his leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is behind that place.
But the reduction of these types of dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and longstanding,  if it has been in that position for a long time and you administer reduction to it, and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not entirely cured; and then it is not necessary to make a prognosis in this case.
But in the case where the dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types of dislocation, then see to it that you return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it quickly returns and does not need other treatment.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant and extend the patient's leg from the lower part, either with his hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant extends the hand of that one from above in such a way that he puts his hands below the patient's testicles; and then tighten a strong bandage around the root of the thigh, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be from him or from the inside part at the groin to the part of the fork (presumably the pubic area), or from the back part to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all these be at once until the patient is lifted with his body from the ground and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type that which we will say of its proper cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inner parts, it is necessary that the patient be supine on his sound side; and then place the ligatures on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the groin; and then extend the ligature upwards from the part of the groin to the highest part of the fork (pubic area).
And then another strong minister grasp his arms, and raise the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outwards with a forceful extension, for thus it will return to its place.
And this type is of easier cure, with which this member is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is excused on your account and does not yield to treatment with this type of cure at all, then it is necessary that the two feet of the patient be tied together with a strong, light ligature, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each be about four fingers, and let the injured leg be extended more than the other by the amount of two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended from the head of the beam, and let him be in the house; and let his height from the ground be about two arms' lengths.
And then order a strong young man to lower the head of the thigh, and let another young man be suspended with the patient, and let the other young man, pulling strongly, lower the thigh, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the actual reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, the patient should lie on his back as we have recommended in the case of coldness; and let the bandage be tightly applied to the affected leg and to his feet.
And then two beams should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then let adjusted beams be placed in the middle of the mound placed on top of which wet cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the beam is between his thighs, so that during the extension the lower parts are not pulled.
And then each assistant extends on his side, and the physician with his hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narrative on a mound; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the patient's hip joint, and then grasps it with the other hand, and with that grasp he pulls it downwards towards the knee joint.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, and he is raised above the earth; rather it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the elevation if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about his lying on two cushions; and is on his face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, there is little more [to do].
And it is necessary that compression be applied with a board to the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as necessary — and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see them equal and the patient bends his leg and extends it without difficulty, then you know that the limb is returned as necessary — and then you adjust the two thighs and apply a plaster and bind it tightly so that the hip is not moved in any direction; and the patient should rest for three or four days.
And then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is now healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and plaster that, and bind it according to your first constriction, and leave it for three days.
And then untie it, and have him delay walking for some days, until he is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, and inwards, and downwards, more towards the back; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to bring his leg to his thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its dislocations is that you make the patient lie down straight, so that he extends his leg if he can; then apply your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; and then apply his knee to your palms, and hold those between your fingers on his knee firmly, and have another assistant extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to the thigh with light, effortless movement.
And then plaster that and bind the leg to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if its reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with bandages, of which the method in the treatment of the hip was previously described.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the talus (ankle bone).
The talus is sometimes dislocated by a small movement, and sometimes completely dislocated, and its dislocation is either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the talus swells, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its displacement is gentle reduction; it is to be extended easily with hands and bandaged until it returns [to its place].
But in treating it when it is completely dislocated, you must make the patient lie down straight, and have a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind at the middle of his back.
Then you, with your right hand, take the foot from the upper part, and with your left hand the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand twice, and then extend with the left; and push the foot with the right hand towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice as we have described.
Then push the foot to the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
Then it will return in one or two times according to this manner, and if you see the foot straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation, because it will return.
But if the reduction is prohibited for you by what we have recounted, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and tighten a tightly bound small pillow on the ground, which fits between his thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around him so that the patient is not injured.
And then let his attendant hold his thighs, and then another attendant extend the foot with his hands or with a bandage tied above the ankle; and then let each attendant extend against the extension of his counterpart, and the pillow remains between the patient's thighs, holding it, so that his body is not pulled downwards against his extension.
And then the doctor straightens the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh downwards, because the dislocation easily returns.
And so when the dislocation returns and the success of its reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And it is necessary that you fear or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not too tight and severely injures them.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
And then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and worsened, and then it will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then you must administer to him what we described earlier, in another place concerning treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
It is necessary that what is dislocated be healed with little extension without effort; and this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in one of the bones of the foot, then you must make the patient place his foot on level ground or on a table, and remain there as if he were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from the joints, and then press that with your foot firmly until it returns, and you see that it is healed and no prominence appears in that place.
And then place under the sole of his foot a board that covers the whole foot, to which are attached two supports, and then tighten that with a suitable and firm bandage for three days; and then untie it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its recovery is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When any of these things happen, and you busy yourself in its cure and in his restoration, many times death ensues.
And therefore it is not fitting that anyone presume upon his cure except one who is wise in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash nor presumptuous; and that he use in the beginning only those medicines that soothe hot abscesses, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except in that case where you hope for salvation from danger, with alleviation of the sickness, and in which some hope appears. And then exert yourself immediately to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before a hot abscess develops.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regime which soothes hot abscesses and treat the wound with what is suitable for it of drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the jagged ends of the bone are separated, then you must force their alignment, and do in this case what we have said in the simple diseases of what precedes in those places.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, that entry into paths of fear as my preaching has preceded you; for this is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more healthful for your true body in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
